6 min
First Objection: Validity of Origination
Razi's first objection against the shared attribute proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection refutes the shared attribute proof by showing that if existence causes the validity of origination, then God would have that validity.

Core Idea
If existence causes the validity of origination, then God would be validly originated.

In the first objection, Razi examines the common attribute of 'validity of origination' shared by all creatures. He argues that if existence is the cause, then God, being existent, would also be validly originated—an impossibility. This objection targets the foundational step of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how Razi sets up the premise: all non-God is originated, so they share the attribute 'validity of origination'.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: common attribute needs common cause. The cause cannot be origination itself because validity precedes origination. So the cause must be existence. Then God, being existent, would have validity of origination—absurd.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this refutes the original proof. The proof's logic leads to a conclusion the opponents reject, showing the proof is invalid.
This is a standard reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع: إن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل قائم في صور كثيرة، مع أن النتيجة اللازمة عنه باطلة قطعا، وذلك يدل على أن هذا الدليل منقوض. وبيانه من وجوه:

الأول: إن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فهو محدث، فتكون صحة الحدوث حكما مشتركا بينهما. فنقول: هذه الصحة حكم مشترك فلا بد لها من علة مشتركة. والمشترك إما الحدوث أو الوجود. ولا يمكن أن يكون المقتضى لصحة الحدوث هو الحدوث، لأن صحة الحدوث سابقة على الحدوث بالرتبة، والسابق بالرتبة على الشيء لا يمكن تعليله بالمتأخر عن الشيء. فثبت أن صحة الحدوث غير معللة بالحدوث، فوجب كونها معللة بالوجود. والله تعالى موجود، فوجب أن يثبت في حقه تعالى صحة الحدوث، وهو محال.

الثاني: إن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فهو إما حجم وإما قائم بالحجم. ثم ذكر التقسيم إلى آخره، حتى يلزم أن يكون البارئ تعالى إما حجما وإما قائما بالحجم. والقوم لا يقولون بذلك.

الثالث: إن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فلابد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر، أو مباينا عنه في أي جهة كان. ثم ذكر التقسيم المتقدم حتى يظهر أن هذا الحكم معلل بالوجود، والبارئ تعالى موجود، فيلزم أن يصح على البارئ تعالى كونه إما محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه في أي جهة كانت، من الجوانب التي للعالم. وذلك يقتضي أن لا يكون اختصاص الله تعالى بجهة فوق واجبا، بل يلزم صحة الحركة على ذات الله تعالى من الفوق إلى أسفل. وكل ذلك عند القوم محال.

الرابع: إن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فإنه يجب أن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة. والمباين بالجهة لابد وأن يكون جوهرا فردا، أو يكون مركبا من الجواهر. وكون كل موجود في الشاهد على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة - أعني: كونه عرضا، أو جوهرا فردا، أو جسما مؤتلفا - لابد وأن يكون معللا بالوجود، فوجب أن يكون البارئ تعالى على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. والقوم ينكرون ذلك، لأنه تعالى عندهم ليس بعرض ولا بجوهر ولا بجسم مؤتلف مركب من الأجزاء والأبعاض.
The ninth question: What you have mentioned of the proof exists in many cases, while the necessary conclusion from it is absolutely false, and that indicates that this proof is refuted. Its explanation is in several ways:

First: Everything other than God is originated, so the validity of origination is a common attribute between them. We say: This validity is a common attribute, so it must have a common cause. The common factor is either origination or existence. And it cannot be that the cause of the validity of origination is origination, because the validity of origination is prior to origination in rank, and that which is prior in rank to something cannot be caused by that which is posterior to it. So it is established that the validity of origination is not caused by origination, so it must be caused by existence. And God is existent, so it must be established in His right the validity of origination, which is impossible.

Second: Every two existents in the witnessed world are either a volume or subsisting in a volume. Then he mentioned the division etc., until it follows that the Creator is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. And the people do not say that.

Third: Every two existents in the witnessed world, one of them must be immanent in the other or separate from it in any direction. Then he mentioned the previous division until it becomes clear that this judgment is caused by existence, and the Creator is existent, so it follows that it is valid for the Creator that He is either immanent in the world or separate from it in any direction from the directions of the world. And that requires that the specificity of God to the direction of above is not necessary, but rather the validity of motion upon the essence of God from above to below follows. And all that is impossible according to the people.

Fourth: Every two existents in the witnessed world, one of them must be immanent in the other or separate from it by direction. And that which is separate by direction must be either an individual substance or composed of substances. And the state of every existent in the witnessed world being on one of these three divisions — namely, being an accident, or an individual substance, or a composed body — must be caused by existence. So it must be that the Creator is on one of these three divisions. And the people deny that, because He is not according to them an accident, nor a substance, nor a composed body made of parts and limbs.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents four objections intended to refute a proof that uses shared attributes. The author introduces the ninth question by stating that the proof in question leads to false conclusions, thus it is invalid. Each objection demonstrates that if existence is taken as the cause of certain shared attributes among created things, then God would also have those attributes, which is absurd.

First objection: All creatures share the attribute 'validity of origination' (ṣiḥḥat al-ḥudūth). If existence is the cause of this validity, then God, being existent, would also have the validity of origination, implying He could be originated, which is impossible.

Second objection: In the witnessed world, every pair of existents is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. If this is due to existence, then God would be a volume or subsisting in one.

Third objection: For any two existents in the witnessed world, one must be immanent (muḥāyith) in the other or separate (mubāyin) by direction. If existence necessitates this, then God would be either immanent in the world or separate by some direction, which would imply He could move from above to below, contradicting His transcendence.

Fourth objection: Separation by direction requires being an individual substance (jawhar fard) or a composite body. If existence is the cause of these three categories (accident, substance, body), then God would fall into one of them, but He is none of these according to the opponents.

Each objection systematically shows that the proof's logic leads to conclusions the opponents themselves reject, thus the proof is refuted.

Key Terms
حدوث
صحة الحدوث
علة مشتركة
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the cause of the validity of origination could be something other than existence, but Razi shows only two options.
Correction: Razi systematically eliminates origination as a cause, leaving existence as the only possible cause according to the proof's own logic.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that origination cannot be the cause of the validity of origination?
A) Because origination is posterior in rank to the validity of origination.
B) Because origination is a common attribute itself.
C) Because existence is a more fundamental cause.
D) Because God is not originated.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that if existence is the cause of the validity of origination, then God would have that attribute, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that even the possibility of being created cannot be attributed to God without absurdity, does your heart truly acknowledge His absolute incomparability? Or do you, in your daily thoughts, sometimes imagine God as if He were subject to categories of time and change? Name one specific area where you catch yourself slipping into such thoughts and commit to correcting it through this lesson.
?
If the validity of origination were caused by existence alone, then every existent would share it. Since God is existent, this would force us to say He is validly originated. How does confronting this logical impossibility deepen your conviction that God is beyond all common attributes? What practical change does this belief demand in your worship?
?
The argument shows that a shared attribute like 'validity of origination' cannot be applied to God without contradiction. Are there other attributes of created beings that you (or those around you) mistakenly, even if subtly, attribute to God? What would it take to purge them from your understanding of Allah?

Second Objection: Volume or Subsisting in Volume
Razi's second objection based on volume and subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection uses the category of volume to refute the shared attribute proof.

Core Idea
If existence forces every existent to be a volume or subsisting in one, then God would be a volume.

The second objection draws on the witnessed world's division into volume (ḥajm) and what subsists in it. If existence necessitates this division, then God would be a volume or subsisting in one. Since the opponents deny this, the proof is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim: every two existents in the witnessed world are either a volume or subsisting in a volume.
2
Understand the implication
If this classification is caused by existence, then God, being existent, would fall into one of these categories. The opponents reject this.
3
Why this refutes the proof
The proof's logic leads to a conclusion its proponents reject, thus the proof is flawed.
This is a quick objection; Razi does not elaborate extensively.

Source Text

?

?

Third Objection: Immanence or Separateness by Direction
Razi's third objection on immanence or separateness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection uses spatial relations to refute the shared attribute proof.

Core Idea
If existence forces immanence or separateness by direction, God would be subject to direction and motion.

The third objection extends the argument to spatial relations. If existence necessarily involves one of two relations—immanence (muḥāyatha) or separateness (mubāyana) by direction—then God would be either immanent in the world or separate in a direction, implying possible motion. This contradicts divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the key terms: muḥāyatha (immanence) and mubāyana (separateness).
2
Follow the implication
If existence causes this dichotomy, then God would be either immanent or separate. If separate, He would be in a direction, and could move from above to below.
3
Why this refutes the proof
The opponents reject that God can be in a direction or move, so the proof is invalid.
Razi mentions 'the people' (al-qawm) who deny such attributes for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع: إن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل قائم في صور كثيرة، مع أن النتيجة اللازمة عنه باطلة قطعا، وذلك يدل على أن هذا الدليل منقوض. وبيانه من وجوه:

The author introduces the ninth question by stating that the proof in question leads to false conclusions, thus it is invalid. Each objection demonstrates that if existence is taken as the cause of certain shared attributes among created things, then God would also have those attributes, which is absurd.

First objection: All creatures share the attribute 'validity of origination' (ṣiḥḥat al-ḥudūth). If existence is the cause of this validity, then God, being existent, would also have the validity of origination, implying He could be originated, which is impossible.

Second objection: In the witnessed world, every pair of existents is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. If this is due to existence, then God would be a volume or subsisting in one.

Third objection: For any two existents in the witnessed world, one must be immanent (muḥāyith) in the other or separate (mubāyin) by direction. If existence necessitates this, then God would be either immanent in the world or separate by some direction, which would imply He could move from above to below, contradicting His transcendence.

Fourth objection: Separation by direction requires being an individual substance (jawhar fard) or a composite body. If existence is the cause of these three categories (accident, substance, body), then God would fall into one of them, but He is none of these according to the opponents.

Each objection systematically shows that the proof's logic leads to conclusions the opponents themselves reject, thus the proof is refuted.

Key Terms
محايثة
مباينة
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God can be in a direction without implying motion, but Razi argues that being in a direction allows for motion.
Correction: The argument shows that if God is separate by direction, He could move from above to below, which is impossible for the transcendent God.

Checkpoint
What does the third objection argue would follow if existence causes the necessity of immanence or separateness by direction?
A) God would be a volume or subsisting in a volume.
B) God would have the validity of origination.
C) God would be either immanent or separate, and if separate, motion would be possible.
D) God would be an accident or substance.

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that the shared attribute proof would force God into spatial relations that imply motion, contradicting His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God as being 'up there' in a literal sense? How does this lesson challenge that mental image, and what would it mean for your worship to truly believe He is beyond direction?
?
Is there any area of your life where you subconsciously treat God as if He is limited to a certain place (like the mosque or during prayer)? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself that He is not confined to any direction?
?
Razi shows that even logical reasoning can lead to absurd conclusions about God if we start from wrong premises. In your own reflection on God's nature, are there any assumptions you hold that might actually limit Him? What would it cost you to let go of those assumptions?

Fourth Objection: Three Categories — Accident, Substance, Body
Razi's fourth objection enumerating three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth objection forces God into one of three categories (accident, substance, composed body) if existence is the cause.

Core Idea
If existence makes every existent an accident, substance, or body, then God would be one of these.

The final objection synthesizes the prior arguments by stating that separateness by direction implies being a substance or composed body. If existence is the cause of being one of three categories (accident, individual substance, composed body), then God must be an accident, a substance, or a body—all impossible for God. This objection comprehensively refutes the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the three categories: accident (ʿaraḍ), individual substance (jawhar fard), and composed body (jism mu'talif).
2
Understand the logical extension
Razi connects separateness by direction to being a substance or body. Then he generalizes: every existent falls into one of these three categories due to existence.
3
Apply to God
God exists, so He would be in one of these categories, but the opponents deny all three for God.
4
Why this refutes the proof
The proof's shared attribute logic leads to a conclusion that is explicitly rejected, so the proof is invalid.
This is the strongest objection as it covers all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع: إن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل قائم في صور كثيرة، مع أن النتيجة اللازمة عنه باطلة قطعا، وذلك يدل على أن هذا الدليل منقوض. وبيانه من وجوه:

الأول: إن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فهو محدث، فتكون صحة الحدوث حكما مشتركا بينهما. فنقول: هذه الصحة حكم مشترك فلا بد لها من علة مشتركة. والمشترك إما الحدوث أو الوجود. ولا يمكن أن يكون المقتضى لصحة الحدوث هو الحدوث، لأن صحة الحدوث سابقة على الحدوث بالرتبة، والسابق بالرتبة على الشيء لا يمكن تعليله بالمتأخر عن الشيء. فثبت أن صحة الحدوث غير معللة بالحدوث، فوجب كونها معللة بالوجود. والله تعالى موجود، فوجب أن يثبت في حقه تعالى صحة الحدوث، وهو محال.

الثاني: إن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فهو إما حجم وإما قائم بالحجم. ثم ذكر التقسيم إلى آخره، حتى يلزم أن يكون البارئ تعالى إما حجما وإما قائما بالحجم. والقوم لا يقولون بذلك.

الثالث: إن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فلابد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر، أو مباينا عنه في أي جهة كان. ثم ذكر التقسيم المتقدم حتى يظهر أن هذا الحكم معلل بالوجود، والبارئ تعالى موجود، فيلزم أن يصح على البارئ تعالى كونه إما محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه في أي جهة كانت، من الجوانب التي للعالم. وذلك يقتضي أن لا يكون اختصاص الله تعالى بجهة فوق واجبا، بل يلزم صحة الحركة على ذات الله تعالى من الفوق إلى أسفل. وكل ذلك عند القوم محال.

الرابع: إن كل موجودين في الشاهد، فإنه يجب أن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة. والمباين بالجهة لابد وأن يكون جوهرا فردا، أو يكون مركبا من الجواهر. وكون كل موجود في الشاهد على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة - أعني: كونه عرضا، أو جوهرا فردا، أو جسما مؤتلفا - لابد وأن يكون معللا بالوجود، فوجب أن يكون البارئ تعالى على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. والقوم ينكرون ذلك، لأنه تعالى عندهم ليس بعرض ولا بجوهر ولا بجسم مؤتلف مركب من الأجزاء والأبعاض.
The ninth question: What you have mentioned of the proof exists in many cases, while the necessary conclusion from it is absolutely false, and that indicates that this proof is refuted. Its explanation is in several ways:

First: Everything other than God is originated, so the validity of origination is a common attribute between them. We say: This validity is a common attribute, so it must have a common cause. The common factor is either origination or existence. And it cannot be that the cause of the validity of origination is origination, because the validity of origination is prior to origination in rank, and that which is prior in rank to something cannot be caused by that which is posterior to it. So it is established that the validity of origination is not caused by origination, so it must be caused by existence. And God is existent, so it must be established in His right the validity of origination, which is impossible.

Second: Every two existents in the witnessed world are either a volume or subsisting in a volume. Then he mentioned the division etc., until it follows that the Creator is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. And the people do not say that.

Third: Every two existents in the witnessed world, one of them must be immanent in the other or separate from it in any direction. Then he mentioned the previous division until it becomes clear that this judgment is caused by existence, and the Creator is existent, so it follows that it is valid for the Creator that He is either immanent in the world or separate from it in any direction from the directions of the world. And that requires that the specificity of God to the direction of above is not necessary, but rather the validity of motion upon the essence of God from above to below follows. And all that is impossible according to the people.

Fourth: Every two existents in the witnessed world, one of them must be immanent in the other or separate from it by direction. And that which is separate by direction must be either an individual substance or composed of substances. And the state of every existent in the witnessed world being on one of these three divisions — namely, being an accident, or an individual substance, or a composed body — must be caused by existence. So it must be that the Creator is on one of these three divisions. And the people deny that, because He is not according to them an accident, nor a substance, nor a composed body made of parts and limbs.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents four objections intended to refute a proof that uses shared attributes. The author introduces the ninth question by stating that the proof in question leads to false conclusions, thus it is invalid. Each objection demonstrates that if existence is taken as the cause of certain shared attributes among created things, then God would also have those attributes, which is absurd.

First objection: All creatures share the attribute 'validity of origination' (ṣiḥḥat al-ḥudūth). If existence is the cause of this validity, then God, being existent, would also have the validity of origination, implying He could be originated, which is impossible.

Second objection: In the witnessed world, every pair of existents is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. If this is due to existence, then God would be a volume or subsisting in one.

Third objection: For any two existents in the witnessed world, one must be immanent (muḥāyith) in the other or separate (mubāyin) by direction. If existence necessitates this, then God would be either immanent in the world or separate by some direction, which would imply He could move from above to below, contradicting His transcendence.

Fourth objection: Separation by direction requires being an individual substance (jawhar fard) or a composite body. If existence is the cause of these three categories (accident, substance, body), then God would fall into one of them, but He is none of these according to the opponents.

Each objection systematically shows that the proof's logic leads to conclusions the opponents themselves reject, thus the proof is refuted.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
جسم مؤتلف
عرض
أبعاض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could be an accident or substance in a metaphorical sense, but the objection uses these in the literal Aristotelian classification.
Correction: Razi uses the technical terms of kalam: 'accident', 'individual substance', and 'composed body' are categories that apply to contingent beings, not God.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, what must be the case for an existent that is separate by direction?
A) It must be an accident.
B) It must be either a volume or subsisting in a volume.
C) It must be either an individual substance or composed of substances.
D) It must be immanent in another.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that the shared attribute proof would force God into one of three impossible categories—accident, substance, or composed body.

The Self-Defeating Analogy of the Karramiyya
Refutes Karramiyya's analogical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyya's analogical argument from the witnessed to the unseen fails, and how the author exposes its inconsistency.

Core Idea
An argument that even its makers reject cannot be sound.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the second argument of the Karramiyya sect, who attempt to prove that God must be either a volume or subsisting in a volume by analogy with the witnessed world. The refutation is immediate and devastating: the Karramiyya themselves do not accept this conclusion. Let's see how the argument works and why it collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and the literal translation. Notice the structure: premise, conclusion, rejection.
The argument is brief but contains a logical flaw.
2
Identify the analogy from witnessed to unseen
The Karramiyya argue that everything in the witnessed world is either volume or subsisting in volume, and then apply this to God. Why would this analogy be invalid?
Think about the principle of interpreting divine attributes.
3
Understand the refutation
The author points out that the Karramiyya themselves reject the conclusion. This shows the argument is self-defeating.
A sound argument must have premises that both sides accept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن كل موجود في الشاهد فهو إما حجم وإما قائم بالحجم. ثم فذكر التقسيم إلى آخره، حتى يلزم أن يكون البارى تعالى إما حجما وإما قائما بالحجم. والقوم لا يقولون بذلك.
The second: Every existing thing in the witnessed world is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. Then he mentioned the division to its end, so that it necessitates that the Exalted Creator is either a volume or subsisting in a volume. And the people do not say that.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the Karramiyya and the author's refutation. It begins by stating a premise about all existing things in the witnessed world (الشاهد) — they are either a volume (حجم) or something subsisting in a volume (قائم بالحجم). The argument then claims to apply this division to the unseen, concluding that the Creator must be either a volume or subsisting in a volume. The refutation is immediate and simple: the opponents themselves do not accept this conclusion (والقوم لا يقولون بذلك). The argument fails because the premise is not universally true for the unseen.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
حجم
قائم بالحجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogical reasoning from the seen to the unseen is always valid.
Correction: The author shows that the Karramiyya themselves reject the conclusion of their analogy, proving the analogy invalid in this context.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the second argument as stated by the Karramiyya?
A) The Creator must be a volume or subsisting in a volume.
B) The Creator must be a substance.
C) The Creator must be in a direction.
D) The Creator must be visible.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's argument that God must be a volume or subsisting in a volume is self-refuting because they themselves reject its conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya rejected the conclusion of their own argument. Third Argument: Contiguity and Separation Refuted
Refutes the argument that existence entails spatial relations for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that existence entails spatial relations leads to absurdities about God's nature, and how Imam Razi refutes it.

Core Idea
Spatial relations do not follow from existence alone — God transcends all direction.

This lesson examines the third argument of the Karramiyya, who claimed that any two existent things must be either contiguous or separate. They extended this to God, implying He must be in a direction. Imam Razi refutes this by showing it leads to the possibility of God moving, which they themselves reject.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: محايث (contiguous) and مباين (separate).
The argument begins with a general premise about the witnessed world.
2
Understand the extension to God
Identify the claim that the rule applies because it is caused by existence itself. Since God exists, He must also be subject to it.
The phrase 'معلل بالوجود' is crucial here.
3
Grasp the refutation
See the absurd consequence: if God is separate from the world in a direction, He would not be specifically tied to 'above', allowing motion. The Karramiyya consider such motion impossible, so their own argument fails.
The refutation is effective because it uses their own impossibility assumption against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن كل موجودين في الشاهد فلابد وأن يكون أحدهما محاينا للآخر أو مباينا عنه في أى جهة كان. ثم تذكر التقسيم المتقدم حتى يظهر أن هذا الحكم معلل بالوجود والبارى تعالى موجود فيلزم أن يصح على البارى تعالى كونه محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه في أي جهة كانت من الجوانب التي للعالم. وذلك يقتضى أن لا يكون اختصاص الله تعالى بجهة فوق واجبا بل يلزم صحة الحركة على ذات الله تعالى من الفوق إلى أسفل وكل ذلك عند القوم محال.
The third: Every two existing things in the witnessed world must be either contiguous to each other or separate from each other in any direction. Then he mentioned the preceding division until it appears that this judgment is caused by existence, and the Exalted Creator exists, so it must be valid for the Exalted Creator to be either contiguous to the world or separate from it in any direction from the sides of the world. And that necessitates that the specification of Allah to the above direction is not necessary, but rather the validity of motion upon the essence of Allah from above to below is necessitated, and all that is impossible according to the people.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument of the Karramiyya and its refutation. The premise states that any two existing things in the witnessed world are either contiguous (محايث) or separate (مباين) in some direction. The argument claims this judgment is caused by existence itself (معلل بالوجود). Since the Creator exists, He must be either contiguous to the world or separate from it in some direction. The refutation follows: if God were separate from the world in a direction, then He would not be specifically tied to the 'above' direction, and motion from above to below would be possible for Him. The Karramiyya consider such motion impossible (محال), so the argument fails.

Key Terms
محايث
مباين
جهة
معلل بالوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence alone necessitates spatial relations such as contiguity or separation.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to absurdities like God moving, which the Karramiyya themselves reject, so the premise is false.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does the author derive from the third argument?
A) God would be a volume.
B) God would be composed of parts.
C) Motion from above to below would be valid for God.
D) God would be visible as an atom.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's argument that existence entails spatial relations is refuted because it leads to the absurd possibility of God moving.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Fourth Argument: A Self-Defeating Classification
Razi presents and refutes the fourth argument by showing it forces God into categories the opponent rejects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument attempts to force God into created categories and why it is self-defeating.

Core Idea
The opponent's own categories cannot contain God without contradicting themselves.

Imam Razi presents the fourth argument from his opponents, which tries to divide all existence into three categories. By analyzing this argument, we see that it leads to a conclusion the opponents themselves reject, making it a self-defeating proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow.
2
Identify the three categories
The argument claims everything in the witnessed world is either an accident, an individual atom, or a composite body. Note how the sub-premises lead to this.
Trace the logic: two things are contiguous or separate; separate things must be atom or composite.
3
Spot the refutation
The argument concludes that God must be one of these three. But the opponents (the Karramiyya) deny that God is any of them. This exposes the argument as self-defeating.
This is the key: the premises come from the opponents themselves, but the conclusion contradicts their own beliefs.
4
Reflect on the argument's flaw
Why does this argument fail? Because the classification is based on the witnessed world (al-shahid), and applying it to God (al-gha'ib) is invalid when it leads to an absurdity even the opponents reject.
This highlights a principle in kalam: analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen is valid only if it does not contradict established tenets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كل موجودين في الشاهد فإنه يجب أن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر أو مباينا عنه بالجهة. والمباين بالجهة لابد وأن يكون جوهرا فردا أو يكون مركبا من الجواهر. وكون كل موجود في الشاهد على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة أعنى: كونه عرضا أو جوهرا فردا أو جسما مؤلفا لابد وأن يكون معللا بالوجود فوجب أن يكون البارى تعالى على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. والقوم ينكرون ذلك.
The fourth: Every two existing things in the witnessed world must be either contiguous to each other or separate in direction. And that which is separate in direction must be either an individual atom or composed of atoms. And the fact that every existing thing in the witnessed world is one of these three divisions – i.e., being an accident, an individual atom, or a composite body – must be caused by existence, so it is necessary that the Exalted Creator be one of these three divisions. And the people deny that.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which builds on the previous one. It states that two things are either contiguous or separate in direction. That which is separate must be either an individual atom (جوهر فرد) or composed of atoms (مركب من الجواهر). Thus everything in the witnessed world is either an accident (عرض), an atom, or a composite body (جسم مؤلف). Since this classification is said to be caused by existence, the Creator must fall into one of these categories. The refutation: the Karramiyya themselves deny that God is an accident, an atom, or a composite body (والقوم ينكرون ذلك). Thus the argument is self-defeating.

Key Terms
عرض
جوهر فرد
جسم مؤلف
معلل بالوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since everything in the witnessed world falls into these three categories, God must also be one of them.
Correction: The argument is flawed because the opponents themselves do not accept that God belongs to any of these categories, showing that the premise 'this division is caused by existence' is not universally applicable or that the witnessed world does not necessarily reflect the unseen.

Checkpoint
What are the three divisions that the fourth argument claims apply to all existing things?
A) Volume, substance, and accident.
B) Accident, atom, and composite body.
C) Contiguous, separate, and composed.
D) Above, below, and middle.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument collapses because it imposes a division that the opponents themselves reject for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you unknowingly confine Him to categories you understand from your own experience? Where have you limited God to a 'box' based on your logic? Commit today to one way you will intentionally stop applying worldly categories to your understanding of God.
?
The opponents denied that God fits into their own categories. Is there an attribute or action of God you have implicitly denied because it doesn't fit your logic or expectations? Repent of that limitation and ask God to expand your understanding.
?
Razi shows that even seemingly logical arguments can be self-defeating when applied to God. What in your own faith do you rely on logic for that might actually be limiting your awe and submission? How can you humble your intellect before the infinite reality of God starting right now?

Fifth Argument: Comparing God to the World in Magnitude
Presents and refutes the fifth argument using size categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya's argument that all existing things must be equal to, larger than, or smaller than the world, and how Imam al-Razi refutes it by showing the premise is not universally applicable to God.

Core Idea
Size comparisons apply to contingent objects, not to the Creator.

Imam al-Razi presents the fifth argument used by the Karramiyya, which attempts to force God into a category of size relative to the world. He then refutes it by showing that the premise is not universally applicable and that the Karramiyya themselves do not accept the conclusion. He also explains why he spent so much time on this argument: because the Karramiyya rely on it heavily.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument premise
Read the first part of the passage: every existing thing imagined with the world must be equal, larger, or smaller in magnitude. Notice the three categories.
2
Identify the claimed cause
The argument says this division must have a cause, and the only cause is existence itself. This leads to applying the division to God, who exists.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation is simple: the Karramiyya themselves do not hold that God falls into any of these categories. Thus the argument is self-defeating.
The author states 'والقوم لا يقولون به' — they do not say it.
4
Reflect on the author's purpose
The author explains he spent so long on this argument because the Karramiyya consider it strong. He then presented overwhelming objections.
This shows the importance of thoroughly addressing the strongest opposing arguments.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
The author ends with a prayer for reward, showing his intention is sincere and seeking Allah's acceptance.

The Third Karramiyya Objection: Vision Requires Direction
Refutes the argument that Allah's visibility entails direction, with a discussion on interpreting anthropomorphic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Karramiyya objection that Allah's visibility implies direction, the response showing its inconsistency with Mu'tazila reasoning, and the proper interpretation of anthropomorphic verses.

Core Idea
Visibility does not force direction on Allah; the same premise leads Mu'tazila to deny visibility.

The Karramiyya argued that since Allah will be seen, He must be in a direction. The response cleverly uses the Mu'tazila’s own agreement that every visible object requires direction to show the flaw: if direction is necessary, then an invisible Allah follows, contradicting the Qur'an. The author then explains how to interpret apparent scriptural indications of place metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Karramiyya objection carefully: they say vision requires facing, which requires direction.
2
Examine the response using Mu'tazila agreement
Notice how the response points out that Mu'tazila also believe every visible thing must be in a direction, but they use it to deny Allah's visibility.
This exposes a contradiction in the Karramiyya's own reasoning.
3
Understand the need for ta'wil
The passage then addresses scriptural verses that seem to indicate place/direction. These must be interpreted metaphorically.
For example, 'place' can mean rank.

The third objection of the Karramiyya in establishing His existence in a direction: They said it is established that He may be seen. And vision requires facing the seen thing or something in the status of facing it, and that requires that He be specified with a direction. The answer: Know that the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya agreed that every visible thing must be in a direction, except that the Mu'tazila said because He is not in a direction, it is necessary that He not be visible. In the reports there is what apparently indicates a place for Allah and a direction from place and direction, and these reports must be interpreted so that they do not indicate a place and direction. According to His saying: 'And He is the God in the heaven and the God on the earth' (az-Zukhruf 84) and His saying: 'And He is with you wherever you are' (al-Hadid 4). And the manner of interpreting place is thus: place in reality is a name for space and direction. And metaphorically it is used in the sense of rank and degree. As you say: Muhammad is the place of his father, meaning
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection of the Karramiyya to establish that Allah is in a direction, and its answer. The argument: it is established that Allah will be seen (رؤية) in the afterlife. Vision requires facing the seen object or something equivalent, which necessitates that the seen object be in a specific direction. Therefore, Allah must be in a direction. The answer: The Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya actually agree that every visible object must be in a direction. However, the Mu'tazila conclude that since Allah is not in a direction, He cannot be seen — contradicting the premise of the Karramiyya. The author then discusses apparent scriptural indications that seem to assign a place or direction to Allah, such as verses about God being in heaven or with us. These must be interpreted metaphorically. For example, 'place' in reality means spatial location, but metaphorically it can mean rank or degree, as in 'Muhammad is the place of his father' meaning he occupies his rank. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the refutation is clear.

The Karramiyya's Third Doubt: Direction from Vision
Imam Razi refutes the Karramiyya argument for direction based on the possibility of seeing Allah, using metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Karramiyya argument that Allah must be in a direction because He can be seen, and the necessity of metaphorical interpretation of apparent anthropomorphic expressions.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah does not imply spatial direction; scriptural terms must be interpreted metaphorically.

The Karramiyya argued that since Allah can be seen, He must face the viewer, implying direction. Imam Razi responds by showing that both the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya share the premise that visibility requires direction, but the Mu'tazila deny Allah's visibility for that reason. Razi then explains that all scriptural references to place, chair, and sitting must be understood metaphorically, drawing on interpretations from Jewish scholars to support this approach.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Karramiyya's argument
Read the first part of the passage where the Karramiyya claim that since Allah can be seen, vision requires facing the object, which implies direction. Note the logical structure.
2
Note the shared premise with Mu'tazila
Razi points out that both the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya agree that every visible object must be in a direction, but they draw opposite conclusions. Understand why this is a key point.
The Mu'tazila use this premise to deny vision of Allah; the Karramiyya use it to affirm direction.
3
Learn the principle of ta'wil
Razi explains that scriptural texts that seem to indicate place or direction for Allah must be interpreted metaphorically. He cites Quranic verses as support.
4
Examine the metaphorical interpretations
Study Razi's detailed explanations of the terms 'makan', 'kursi', and 'julus' – showing their literal physical meanings and their metaphorical applications to Allah.
Razi uses examples from the Torah and Jewish exegesis to reinforce his point.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the proper belief for verifiers (muhaqqiqun) is to affirm Allah's transcendence above physical attributes, using metaphorical interpretation where necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة للكرامية في إثبات كونه تعالى في الجهة: قالوا: ثبت أنه تعالى تجوز رؤيته. والرؤية تقتضي مواجهة المرئي أو شيئاً هو في حكم مقابلته، وذلك يقتضي كونه تعالى مخصوصاً بجهة. والجواب: اعلم أن المعتزلة والكرامية توافقتا في أن كل مرئي لا بد وأن يكون في جهة، إلا أن المعتزلة قالوا: لسكنه ليس في الجهة فوجب أن لا يكون مرئياً. وفي الأخبار ما يدل ظاهره على مكان لله وجهة عن المكان والجهة وهذه الأخبار يجب أن تؤول لئلا تدل على مكان وجهة. لقوله تعالى: (وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله) ولقوله تعالى: (وهو معكم أينما كنتم). وكيفية تأويل المكان هكذا: المكان على الحقيقة: اسم على الحيز والجهة. وعلى المجاز يكون بمعنى الرتبة والدرجة. كما تقول: محمد مكان أبيه، أي بدله في رتبته ودرجته. وقد جاء في التوراة أن حزقيال النبي قال عن الله عز وجل: «مبارك مجد الرب من مكان» (حز 3:12) وفسر المفسرون من علماء بني إسرائيل قوله هذا: «بحسب مرتبته وعظم حظه في الوجود». وكذلك كل ذكر مكان جاء في الله: إنما المراد به: مرتبة وجوده تعالى، التي لا مثيل لها ولا شبيه. وكذلك قالوا في قول الله لموسى: «هو ذا عندي موضع» (خر 33:21) قالوا: إن المراد بالموضع: «مرتبة نظر وتطلع عقل، لا تطلع عين». والكرسي الذي يدل على المكان: على الحقيقة: اسم لموضع جلوس في مكان عال. وعلى المجاز: الجلالة والعظمة. لأن الذي ارتفع عن الأرض وجلس على الكرسي صار عظيماً بالنسبة لمن جلسوا على التراب. وقد جاء في التوراة أن الله له «كرسي» وجاء فيها أن السماء عرش الله: «هكذا قال الرب: السماء عرشي» (أش 66:1) والمفسرون من علماء بني إسرائيل يقولون: «هي تدل على وجودي وعظمتي، كدلالة الكرسي على عظم من أهل له» هذا هو الذي يجب أن يعتقده المحققون، لا أن ثم جسماً يرتفع الإله عليه تعالى علواً كبيراً. والجلوس على الحقيقة يدل على القعود. وعلى المجاز يدل على الاستقرار والثبات على أكمل الحالات. وقد جاء في التوراة عن الله تعالى: «الساكن في السماوات» (مز 124:8) أي: الإله الثابت الذي لا يتغير بنحو من أنحاء التغير، لأنه قال عن نفسه: (إني أنا الرب لا أتغير) (ملا 3:6) وعبر بالسماء عن الثبات والاستقرار «لكون السماء التي لا تغير فيها ولا اختلاف» أعني: أنه لا تتغير أشخاصها كما تتغير أشخاص كائنات الأرض وفاسديها (كما يقول موسى بن ميمون). وإذا كان الشيء عظيماً ثابتاً مثل (كرة الأرض) فإنه يقاس على السماء في المعنى. ولما فسدت الأرض بطوفان نوح عليه السلام واستقر الطوفان على الأرض قالت التوراة: «جلس الرب على الطوفان» (مز 29:10) أي: أنه هو الإله الوحيد الذي عمله، وهو الذي يملك أمره، لا غير. وهذا التأويل الذي عند علماء بني إسرائيل في الكرسي له تأويل يشبهه عند كثير من أهل الإسلام.
The third doubt of the Karramiyya in proving that He, exalted, is in a direction: They said: It is established that He, exalted, may be seen. And vision requires facing the seen object or something in the ruling of being opposite it, and that requires that He, exalted, be specified with a direction. The answer: Know that the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya agreed that every visible object must be in a direction, except that the Mu'tazila said: Since He is not in a direction, it is necessary that He not be visible. And in the reports there is what outwardly indicates a place for Allah and a direction; these reports must be interpreted so that they do not indicate a place and direction, according to His statement: 'And He is the One who is in the heaven an ilah and on the earth an ilah' and His statement: 'And He is with you wherever you are.' The manner of interpreting 'place' (al-makan) is as follows: 'place' in reality is a name for space (al-hayyiz) and direction (al-jiha). And metaphorically, it means rank (al-rutba) and degree (al-daraja), just as you say: Muhammad is the place of his father, meaning his successor in his rank and degree. And it has come in the Torah that the prophet Ezekiel said about Allah, the Mighty and Majestic: 'Blessed be the glory of the Lord from His place' (Ezekiel 3:12). And the commentators from the scholars of the Children of Israel interpreted his statement: 'According to His rank and the greatness of His portion in existence.' And similarly every mention of 'place' (makan) regarding Allah: the intended meaning is the rank of His existence, exalted, which has no likeness and no similitude. And similarly they said regarding the statement of Allah to Moses: 'Here is a place near Me' (Exodus 33:21). They said: The intended meaning of 'place' is 'a rank of intellectual insight and contemplation, not an eye's glance.' And the 'chair' (al-kursi) which indicates place: in reality it is a name for a sitting place in a high location. And metaphorically: majesty (al-jalala) and greatness (al-'azama). Because whoever rises above the earth and sits on a chair becomes great relative to those who sit on the ground. And it has come in the Torah that Allah has a 'chair' and in it that the heaven is the throne of Allah: 'Thus says the Lord: Heaven is My throne' (Isaiah 66:1). And the commentators from the scholars of the Children of Israel say: 'It indicates My existence and My greatness, just as the chair indicates the greatness of the one worthy of it.' This is what the verifiers must believe, not that there is a body upon which the deity is raised, exalted be He, a great exaltation. And 'sitting' (al-julus) in reality indicates sitting down (al-qu'ud). And metaphorically it indicates stability (al-istiqrar) and firmness (al-thabat) in the most perfect states. And it has come in the Torah about Allah, exalted: 'He who sits in the heavens' (Psalm 124:8), meaning: the firm deity who does not change by any kind of change, because He said about Himself: 'I am the Lord, I do not change' (Malachi 3:6). And He expressed by 'heaven' about firmness and stability 'because the heaven has no change and no variation', meaning: its individuals do not change as the individuals of the earthly and corruptible things change (as Moses Maimonides says). And if a thing is great and stable like (the sphere of the earth), it is measured by heaven in meaning. And when the earth was corrupted by the flood of Noah, peace be upon him, and the flood settled on the earth, the Torah said: 'The Lord sat upon the flood' (Psalm 29:10), meaning: He is the only deity who did that, and He is the one who possesses command over it, no other. And this interpretation which is held by the scholars of the Children of Israel regarding the chair has a similar interpretation among many of the people of Islam.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Imam Razi presents the third doubt (shubhah) of the Karramiyya sect, who argue that since Allah can be seen (as affirmed in Sunni theology), vision requires facing the object, which implies direction, thus Allah must be in a direction. Razi's answer begins by noting that both the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya agree on the premise that every visible object must be in a direction. However, the Mu'tazila use this to deny the possibility of seeing Allah (since He is not in a direction), while the Karramiyya use it to affirm direction. Razi then explains that the scriptural texts that apparently indicate a place or direction for Allah must be interpreted metaphorically (ta'wil). He provides examples from the Torah and the interpretations of Jewish scholars to illustrate the metaphorical meanings of terms like 'place' (makan), 'chair' (kursi), and 'sitting' (julus). He shows that these terms, when applied to Allah, refer to His rank, majesty, stability, and perfection, not to physical location. He cites Quranic verses (ash-Shura 84 and al-Hadid 4) to support the need for interpretation. The passage concludes that this metaphorical interpretation is not unique to Muslims but is also found among Jewish scholars, and thus it is the proper approach for verifiers (muhaqqiqun).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Karramiyya's argument is strong because it links vision to direction.
Correction: Razi shows that the premise is shared but the conclusion is disputed, and the scriptural texts require metaphorical interpretation, so the argument does not prove direction.

C) The Mu'tazila affirm that Allah is in a direction but deny vision; the Karramiyya affirm both.
D) Both groups agree that Allah is not in a direction, but they disagree on vision.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing Allah does not necessitate spatial direction; scriptural terms like place, chair, and sitting are metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Meaning of 'Place' for God: Rank, Not Space
Interprets 'place' for God as rank, not spatial location, citing Jewish exegetes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars interpret scriptural references to God's 'place' as referring to His rank and station, not a physical location, and how this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'place' is His rank, not a location.

This lesson examines a key principle of divine transcendence: when scripture speaks of God's 'place,' it does not imply spatial location. Quoting Jewish exegetes of the Bible, Imam Razi demonstrates that 'place' refers to God's rank and station. This interpretation safeguards God's transcendence from anthropomorphism, showing that even pre-Islamic scriptures require metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how the author begins by connecting 'place' to rank and degree.
The passage cites two biblical verses.
2
Identify the key scriptural references
Find the two biblical verses mentioned: Ezekiel 3:12 and Exodus 33:21. Understand how they are interpreted.
These verses are used as evidence that 'place' means rank.
3
Understand the interpretive principle
The interpreters from the Children of Israel apply a consistent rule: every mention of 'place' for God means the rank of His existence, which has no likeness.
This principle applies to all such verses.
4
Contrast with the common misunderstanding
Many assume that 'place' implies a physical location. This lesson corrects that by showing the metaphorical interpretation.
This preserves tanzih (divine transcendence).
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this principle matters for Islamic theology: it shows that scripture must be interpreted in a way that avoids corporealism.
This is a key argument in kalam against anthropomorphism.

بدله في رتبته ودرجته . وقد جاء فى التوراة أن حزقيال النبي قال عن عز وجل : « مبارك مجد الرب من مكان » ( حز ۳ : ۱۲ ) وفسر المفسرون من علماء بنى اسرائيل قوله هذا : « بحسب مرتبته وعظم حظه في الوجود وكذلك كل ذكر مكان جاء فى الله : انما المراد به : مرتبة وجوده تعالى ، التي لا متيل لها ولا شبيه » وكذلك قالوا في قول الله لموسى : « هو ذا عندى موضع » ( خر ۳۳ : ۲۱ ) قالوا : ان المراد بالموضع : « مرتبة نظر وتطلع عقل ، لا تطلع عين »
His substitute in rank and degree. And it came in the Torah that Ezekiel the prophet said about Allah, to Him be Might and Majesty: 'Blessed be the glory of the Lord from His place' (Ezekiel 3:12). And the exegetes from the scholars of the Children of Israel interpreted this saying of his: 'According to His rank and the greatness of His portion in existence, and likewise every mention of 'place' that occurs in relation to Allah, the intended meaning is only the rank of His existence, to Him be exalted, which has no parallel nor likeness.' And likewise they said regarding the saying of Allah to Moses: 'Behold, there is a place with Me' (Exodus 33:21). They said: the intended meaning of 'place' is 'a rank of intellectual vision and insight, not the sight of an eye.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of anthropomorphic language in the Bible, specifically the term 'place' (مكان) when applied to God. The author begins by noting that the Jewish scholars of the Children of Israel interpreted Ezekiel's vision of 'the glory of the Lord from His place' as referring to God's rank and station, not a spatial location. They extend this principle to every mention of 'place' in relation to God: it means the rank of His existence, which has no parallel or likeness. The same interpretation applies to God's statement to Moses in Exodus, where 'my place' is understood as a rank of intellectual vision and insight, not physical sight. The passage thus establishes a strong principle of metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to preserve divine transcendence (tanzih), demonstrating that even pre-Islamic scriptures require such interpretation to avoid corporealism.

The Metaphorical Meaning of 'Sitting' (Julūs) for God
Explains 'sitting' as metaphor for God's stability and immutability, citing biblical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians interpret the term 'sitting' when attributed to God, distinguishing literal from metaphorical meaning and preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
When God 'sits,' it means His absolute stability, not a physical posture.

This lesson explores a key example of ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation) in Islamic theology. The term 'sitting' (جلوس) appears in scripture, but attributing it literally to God would imply change and limitation. The passage explains the metaphorical meaning and supports it with biblical references, emphasizing God's immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author distinguishes literal from metaphorical meaning right at the start.
Pay attention to the structure: definition, evidence (verses), analogy, and example.
2
Identify the biblical verses
The passage cites two verses from the Torah (Old Testament) and one from Psalms. For each verse, ask: how does it support the metaphorical interpretation?
The verse 'I do not change' (Malachi 3:6) is key to the argument.
3
Understand the analogy with the heavens
The author compares God's stability to the unchanging nature of the heavens. Why is this analogy useful?
Moses Maimonides is referenced to reinforce the idea.
4
Apply the interpretation to the flood verse
The final example 'The Lord sat enthroned at the flood' is explained as God's sovereign control, not physical sitting. Reflect on how this preserves divine transcendence.

الجلوس » على الحقيقة يدل على القعود . وعلى المجاز يدل على الاستقرار والثبات على أكمل الحالات . وقد جاء في التوراة عن تعالی : « الساكن فى السماوات ( ) مز ٤:٢) أي : الاله الثابت الذي لا يتغير بنحو من أنحاء التغير ، لأنه قال عن نفسه : ( اني أنا الرب لا أتغير » ( ملا ٢ : ٦ ) وعبر بالسماء عن النبات والاستقرار « لكون السماء التي لا تغير فيها ولا اختلاف أعنى : أنه لا تتغير أشخاصها كما تنغير أشخاص كائنات الأرض وفاسديها ( كما يقول موسى بن ميمون . واذا كان الشيء عظيما نابتا مثل ) كرة الأرض ( فانه يقاس على السماء في المعنى. ولما فسدت الأرض بطوفان نوح عليه السلام واستقر الطوفان على الأرض قالت التوراة : « جلس الرب على الطوفان ( ( مز ۲۸ : ۱۰ ) أى أنه هو الاله الوحيد الذي عمله ، وهو الذي يملك أمره ، لا غير
'Sitting' in its literal sense indicates sitting down (al-qu'ud). In the metaphorical sense, it indicates stability and permanence in the most perfect state. And it came in the Torah about the Exalted: 'He who dwells in the heavens' (Psalm 2:4), meaning the divine being who is constant and does not change in any way of change, because He said about Himself: 'For I am the Lord, I do not change' (Malachi 3:6). And He expressed by 'heavens' [the idea of] stability and permanence, because the heavens have no change or alteration, meaning: their individual components do not change as the individuals of earthly and decaying things change (as Moses ben Maimon says). And if a thing is great and firm like the globe of the earth, it is compared to the heavens in meaning. And when the earth was corrupted by the flood of Noah, peace be upon him, and the flood settled on the earth, the Torah said: 'The Lord sat enthroned at the flood' (Psalm 29:10), meaning that He is the sole God who did that, and He is the one who controls its affair, no other.

Commentary
This passage interprets the biblical term 'sitting' (جلوس) when attributed to God. It starts by distinguishing the literal meaning (sitting down) from the metaphorical meaning (stability and permanence in the most perfect state). The author then cites two biblical verses: Psalm 2:4 ('He who dwells in the heavens') and Malachi 3:6 ('I do not change'), explaining that 'dwelling' or 'sitting' in heaven is not a physical posture but an expression of God's immutability. He uses the analogy of the heavens, which are unchanging, to reinforce this metaphorical understanding. The author also references Moses Maimonides' view that heavenly bodies do not change like earthly ones. Finally, he interprets the verse 'The Lord sat enthroned at the flood' (Psalm 29:10) as meaning God's sovereign control over the flood, not a physical sitting. This lesson demonstrates the principle of ta'wil to preserve God's transcendence when scripture uses anthropomorphic language.

The Metaphorical Meaning of 'Kursi' (Throne) for God
Defines and interprets 'kursi' metaphorically with Jewish and Islamic sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'kursi' (throne) when applied to God is interpreted metaphorically by both Jewish and Islamic scholars to mean majesty and power, and that the four interpretations of Quran 2:255 by al-Zamakhshari reinforce this metaphorical understanding.

Core Idea
God's throne is not a chair; it is a symbol of His majesty.

This passage from Imam al-Razi's Asas al-Taqdis presents the metaphorical interpretation of the word 'kursi' (throne/chair) as used for God. It begins with a clear distinction between literal and figurative meanings, then cites Jewish exegetes from the Bible, and culminates in a detailed analysis by Imam al-Zamakhshari offering four ways to understand the Quranic verse 'His kursi extends over the heavens and the earth.' This lesson will guide you through these interpretations and show how they safeguard God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal and metaphorical meanings
Read the first part of the passage where the author defines 'kursi' literally as a high seat and metaphorically as majesty and greatness. Note the rational analogy given.
This sets the foundation for all that follows.
2
Explore the Jewish interpretation
Examine how Jewish scholars from the Bible interpret 'throne' as referring to God's existence and greatness, not a physical body. This shows consistency across traditions.
The passage quotes Isaiah 66:1.
3
Analyze al-Zamakhshari's four interpretations
Identify each of the four aspects he gives for 'وسع كرسيه': (1) figurative depiction, (2) knowledge, (3) dominion, (4) a created object. Notice how each avoids a literal physical sitting.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider why it is necessary to interpret such verses metaphorically. What misconception does this passage correct?
The key is to uphold God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكرسى الذي يدل على المكان . على الحقيقة : اسم لموضع جلوس فى مكان عالى . وعلى المجاز : الجلالة والعظمة . لأن الذي ارتفع عن الأرض وجلس على الكرسى، صار عظيماً بالنسبة لمن جلسوا على التراب وقد جاء فى النوراة أن الله له « كرسى » وجاء فيها أن السماء عرش اللــــه » هكذا قال الرب : السماء عرشى » ( أش ٦٦ والمفسرون من علماء بنى اسرائيل بقولون : « هي تدل على وجودى و عظمتی ، كدلالة الكرسى على عظم من أهل له عظم من أهل له . هذا هو الذي يجب أن يعتقده المحققون ، لا أن ثم جسما يرتفع الاله عليه تعالى علوا كبيرا » وهذا التأويل الذي عند علماء بنى اسرائيل فى الكرسى له تأويل يشبهه عند كثيرون من أهل الاسلام . ومنهم الامام فخر الدين الرازي ، يقول بن عمر ، في أنعم الله عليهما والامام محمود بن عمر الكشاف : « الكرسى : ما يجلس عليه ولا يفضل عن مقعد القاعد ، وفي قوله : « وسع كرسيه » أربعة أوجه : أحدها: أن كرسيه لم يضق عن لاسموات والأرض ، لبسطته وسعته ، وما هو الا تصوير وتخييل قط ، ولا كرسى ثمة ولا فعود ولا قاعد . كقوله : « وما قدروا الله حق قدره ، والأرض جميعا قبضته يوم القيامة . والسموات مطويات ببینه » من غير تصور قبضة وطى وبمين ، وانما هو تخبيل لعظمة شأنه وتمثيل حسى ألا ترى الى قوله : ( وما قدروا الله حق قدره » والثاني : وسع . وسمى العلم كرسبا ، تسمية بمكانه الذي هو كرسى العالم . والثالث : وسع ملكه ، تسمية ، بمكانه الذي هو كرسى الملك . والرابع : ما روى أنه خلق كرسبا ، هو بين يدى العرش دونه السموات والأرض . وهو الى العرش كأصغر شيء . وعن الحسن : الكرسى هو العرش » أ . هـ
The 'kursi' which indicates place. In its literal sense: a name for a place of sitting in a high location. In the metaphorical sense: majesty and greatness, because one who rises above the ground and sits on a chair becomes great relative to those who sit on the ground. And it came in the Torah that Allah has a 'kursi', and it came in it that heaven is the throne of Allah: 'Thus says the Lord: Heaven is My throne' (Isaiah 66:1). And the exegetes from the scholars of the Children of Israel say: 'It indicates My existence and My greatness, just as the chair indicates the greatness of one who deserves greatness. This is what the verifiers must believe, not that there is a body upon which the deity ascends, exalted be He greatly.' And this interpretation that the scholars of the Children of Israel have regarding the 'kursi' has a similar interpretation among many of the people of Islam. Among them is Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, may Allah bless them both, and Imam Mahmud b. Umar al-Zamakhshari (author of al-Kashshaf): 'Al-kursi is what one sits on and does not exceed the seat of the sitter. And in His saying: "His kursi extends over the heavens and the earth" (Quran 2:255) there are four aspects: First, that His kursi did not straiten the heavens and the earth due to its expanse and capacity, and it is nothing but a depiction and imagination, and there is no kursi, nor sitting, nor sitter there. Like His saying: "They did not appraise Allah with true appraisal, and the entire earth will be in His grip on the Day of Resurrection, and the heavens will be rolled up in His right hand" (Quran 39:67) without conceiving a grip, rolling, and right hand, but rather it is a representation of the greatness of His affair and a sensory simile. Do you not see His saying: "They did not appraise Allah with true appraisal"? Second, meaning: His knowledge extends; and knowledge was called kursi, as a name for its place which is the chair of the knower. Third, meaning: His dominion extends; as a name for its place which is the chair of the kingdom. Fourth, what is narrated that He created a kursi which is in front of the Throne, below it are the heavens and the earth, and it is to the Throne as the smallest thing. And from al-Hasan: al-kursi is the Throne.' End quote.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and interpretation of the term 'kursi' (chair/throne) when used of God. It begins by giving the literal meaning (a high seat) and the metaphorical meaning (majesty and greatness), with a rational analogy: a person who sits on a high chair is considered great relative to those on the ground. The author then cites Jewish exegetes from the Bible (Isaiah 66:1) who interpret 'throne' and 'chair' as referring to God's existence and greatness, not a physical body. He then connects this to Islamic exegesis, specifically quoting Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and Imam al-Zamakhshari from his tafsir al-Kashshaf. Al-Zamakhshari provides four interpretations of the Quranic verse 'His kursi extends over the heavens and the earth': (1) It is a figurative depiction (takhayyul) of His power, not a real chair; (2) 'kursi' means His knowledge; (3) 'kursi' means His dominion; (4) It is a created object before the Throne (a narrated Tradition). He concludes with a report from al-Hasan al-Basri that 'kursi' is the Throne. The passage thus demonstrates a consistent tradition of metaphorical interpretation across both Jewish and Islamic scholarship to avoid anthropomorphism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'kursi' (throne) of God is a physical chair upon which He sits.
Correction: The passage affirms that 'kursi' is used metaphorically to denote God's majesty and power, not a literal seat. Both Jewish and Islamic scholars interpret it figuratively.

Ascending and Descending as Metaphors for God
Explains 'ascending' and 'descending' as metaphors for change in rank and divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how 'ascending' and 'descending' in scripture are interpreted metaphorically to refer to changes in rank, divine action, and revelation, not physical movement.

Core Idea
'Ascending' and 'descending' are about divine action, not divine location.

This passage concludes Imam al-Razi's treatment of anthropomorphic biblical terms by focusing on 'ascending' and 'descending'. It establishes that these terms, when applied to God, do not imply spatial movement but rather convey changes in status, the communication of revelation, or the sending of divine punishment. The lesson draws on Jewish interpretive tradition and biblical citations to illustrate this metaphorical reading.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal vs. metaphorical distinction
Read the opening lines where the literal meaning (spatial) is contrasted with the metaphorical meaning (rank).
The literal sense is about physical location; the metaphorical sense is about status or action.
2
Examine the human example from Deuteronomy
Look at the verse from Deuteronomy 28:43, where 'rise' and 'fall' are used for status, not movement.
This is a human example that parallels the divine usage.
3
Study the Jewish scholars' interpretation for revelation
Understand how 'descent' of revelation or the Shekhinah is interpreted as God conveying knowledge, not moving spatially.
Descent and ascent refer to the giving and removal of prophecy or divine presence.
4
Analyze the use for divine judgment
Notice how 'descent' is used for calamities or punishment after God examines deeds.
This extends the metaphor to divine retribution.
5
Reflect on the biblical examples
Consider the quotes from Psalm 8:4 and Genesis 11:7 as further illustrations of metaphorical descent.
These reinforce that 'descent' is not spatial but refers to divine attention or action.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و «صعد » و « هبط » يدلان على المكان بالارتفاع والانحطاط على الحقيقة . وعلى المجاز تدل كلمة صعد على علو المنزلة ، وتدل كلمة هبط على انحطاط المنزلة ... فقد قال الله للاسرائيلي الغبي : يستعلى عليك الغريب الذى فيما بينكم متصاعدا ، وأنت منحط متنازلا » ( تث ۲۸ : ٤٣ ) ويقول علماء بنى اسرائيل فى ما ورد فى التوراة عن صعود الله ونزوله : « ولما كنا معشر الآدميين في أسفل السافلين بالموضع وبمرتبة الوجود ، بالاضافة للميحط . وكان هو في أعلى عليين على حقيقة وجود ، وجلالة وعظمة . لا علو مكان . وشاء تعالى : ايصال علم منه ، وافاضة وحى على بعضنا عبر بنزول الوحى على النبي ، أو بحلول سكينة فى موضع : بالنزول . وعبر بارتفاع حالة النبوة ـ تلك الشخص ، أو ازالة السكينة من الموضع : بالرفع . فكل نزلة ورفعة تجدها منسوبة للبارى تعالى : انها أراد بها هذا المعنى . وكذلك اذا نزلت آمة بأمة أو اقليم - بحسب مشيئته القديمة التي تصف الكتب النبوية - قبل أن تصدر تلك النازلة بأن أولئك افتقد الله أعمالهم ، ثم بعد ذلك أنزل بهم العقاب . فانه يكنى عن هذا المعنى أيضا : بالنزول تكون الانسان أقل من أن تفتقد أعماله ، ويعاقب عليها لولا المشيئة . وقد بين ذلك في كتب النبوة . وقبل : ما الانسان حتى تذكره ، وابن البشر حتى تفتقده » ؟ ( مز ۸ : ٥٠ ) يشير الى هذا المعنى . ولذلك كني عن هذا بالنزول . فقال : « هلم نهبط ونبلبل هناك لغتهم » ( تك ۱۱ : ۷ )
And 'ascended' (sa'ada) and 'descended' (habata) in their literal sense indicate place by rising and falling. In the metaphorical sense, the word 'ascended' indicates high rank, and the word 'descended' indicates low rank... For Allah said to the foolish Israelite: 'The foreigner who is among you will rise higher and higher above you, and you will fall lower and lower' (Deuteronomy 28:43). And the scholars of the Children of Israel say regarding what is reported in the Torah about the ascent and descent of Allah: 'Since we, the company of humans, are in the lowest of the low in place and in rank of existence relative to the environment, while He is in the highest of the high in true existence, majesty, and greatness—not highness of place. And He willed, Exalted be He, to convey knowledge from Him and pour out revelation upon some of us, so He expressed it as the descent of revelation upon the prophet, or by the dwelling of the Shekhinah in a place: by descent. And He expressed the elevation of the state of prophethood—that person, or the removal of the Shekhinah from the place: by ascent. So every descent and ascent that you find attributed to the Creator, Exalted be He, He intended this meaning by it. Likewise, when a calamity descends upon a people or a region—according to His eternal will which the prophetic books describe—before that calamity occurs, [it is because] God had examined their deeds, then after that He sent down punishment upon them. For this meaning is also alluded to by descent, since man is too lowly for his deeds to be examined and punished were it not for the will. And this is explained in the books of prophecy. It is said: 'What is man that You are mindful of him, and the son of man that You care for him?' (Psalm 8:4) alluding to this meaning. Therefore, this is alluded to by descent. Thus He said: 'Come, let us go down and confuse their language there' (Genesis 11:7).
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is the final section of the treatment of anthropomorphic biblical terms, focusing on 'ascending' and 'descending'. It begins by stating the literal meaning (spatial rising and falling) and the metaphorical meaning (high or low rank). It cites a biblical verse (Deuteronomy 28:43) where a human example uses the words 'rise' and 'fall' to mean status, not movement. The core of the passage is the Jewish scholars' extended interpretation: humans are in the lowest place and rank, while God is in the highest true existence, not highness of place. When God wishes to communicate knowledge or revelation, it is called 'descent' of revelation or the Shekhinah; the removal of prophecy or Shekhinah is 'ascent'. Every instance of divine 'descent' or 'ascent' carries this meaning. The interpretation extends to divine judgment: when a calamity befalls a people, it is described as 'descending' after God has examined their deeds, but the passage notes that humans are too lowly for their deeds to be examined unless God wills. It quotes Psalm 8:4 ('What is man...') as support. Finally, the Tower of Babel narrative ('Let us go down and confuse their language') is cited as an example of metaphorical descent. The lesson emphasizes that all such language is removed from spatial movement and refers to divine actions or stations.

علو المنزلة
سكينة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'descending' and 'ascending' in scripture imply God physically changes location.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that these terms are used metaphorically for changes in rank, revelation, or divine action, not spatial movement.

Checkpoint

A) God physically descends to earth
B) God conveys knowledge to a prophet
C) God's rank decreases
D) God sits on a throne

Key Takeaway
The essential point: 'Ascending' and 'descending' in scripture are always metaphorical for God, referring to changes in rank, revelation, or action, never physical movement.
