Four Interpretations of the Kursiy Verse
Razi enumerates four interpretations of 'Wasi'a kursiyyuhu'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major interpretations of the Kursiy verse and how they illustrate the Islamic approach to anthropomorphic expressions.

Core Idea
The Kursiy is not a physical throne but a symbol with multiple valid interpretations.

Imam Razi presents four ways to understand the phrase 'His Kursiy encompasses the heavens and the earth' from the Ayat al-Kursi. These interpretations range from pure metaphor to a created object. Grasping them is essential for appreciating how scholars reconcile scriptural language with divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let Razi's own words set the stage before analyzing the interpretations.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أربعة أوجه' – he is clearly enumerating.
2
Identify the four interpretations
Locate each of the four interpretations. Notice that the first is a figurative denial of physicality, the second identifies the Kursiy with knowledge, the third with dominion, and the fourth as a created object before the Throne.
The first interpretation is the longest and includes a comparison to other verses.
3
Understand the first interpretation in depth
The first interpretation is crucial for tanzih (transcendence). Razi uses it to deny any literal sitting or chair, comparing it to verses about 'handful' and 'folded heavens' to show that such expressions are imaginative depictions of God's greatness.
Razi asks 'Do you not see His statement...?' to reinforce the figurative reading.
4
Compare the second and third interpretations
Both second and third interpretations use metaphorical naming: the Kursiy as the seat of knowledge or the seat of kingship. Note how they differ in what they emphasize (knowledge vs. power).
These interpretations are more conceptual than the first.
5
Reflect on the fourth interpretation and al-Hasan's view
The fourth interpretation presents a literal but created Kursiy, smaller than the Throne. The quote from al-Hasan (that the Kursiy is the Throne) shows another opinion. Consider why Razi includes multiple views without endorsing one.
Razi's approach is to present possibilities, not to resolve the ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « وسع كرسيه » أربعة أوجه : أحدها: أن كرسيه لم يضق عن السموات والأرض ، لبسطته وسعته ، وما هو إلا تصوير وتخييل قط ، ولا كرسى ثمة ولا فعود ولا قاعد . كقوله : « وما قدروا الله حق قدره ، والأرض جميعا قبضته يوم القيامة . والسموات مطويات بيمينه » من غير تصور قبضة وطى وبمين ، وإنما هو تخييل لعظمة شأنه وتمثيل حسى ألا ترى الى قوله : ( وما قدروا الله حق قدره » والثاني : وسع . وسمى العلم كرسيا، تسمية بمكانه الذي هو كرسى العالم . والثالث : وسع ملكه ، تسمية ، بمكانه الذي هو كرسى الملك . والرابع : ما روى أنه خلق كرسيا، هو بين يدى العرش دونه السموات والأرض . وهو الى العرش كأصغر شيء . وعن الحسن : الكرسى هو العرش » أ . هـ
His statement: 'His Kursiy encompasses' has four interpretations: One: that His Kursiy is not too narrow for the heavens and the earth, due to its extensiveness and capacity, and it is nothing but a depiction and imagination, and there is no kursiy nor sitting nor sitter there. Like His statement: 'And they did not measure Allah with His true measure, and the earth entirely will be His handful on the Day of Resurrection, and the heavens will be folded in His right hand' without conceiving a handful, folding, and right hand; rather it is an imagination of the greatness of His affair and a sensory representation. Do you not see His statement: 'And they did not measure Allah with His true measure'? The second: He expanded, and knowledge was called kursiy, naming it after its place which is the kursiy of the scholar. The third: He expanded His dominion, naming it after its place which is the kursiy of the king. The fourth: What is narrated that He created a kursiy which is before the Throne, beneath it are the heavens and the earth, and it is in relation to the Throne like the smallest thing. And from al-Hasan: the kursiy is the Throne.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage provides four interpretations of the verse 'Wasi'a kursiyyuhu'. The first interpretation denies any literal physical kursiy, viewing the expression as a figurative depiction of God's greatness, similar to other anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran. The second interpretation identifies the kursiy with knowledge, naming it after its place (the scholar's seat). The third identifies it with dominion, naming it after the king's seat. The fourth is a narrated tradition that there is a created kursiy before the Throne. The passage ends with a quote from al-Hasan that the kursiy is the Throne. The author does not endorse any particular interpretation but presents them as possible understandings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think the Kursiy is a literal physical throne on which God sits.
Correction: Razi's first interpretation explicitly denies any literal kursiy, sitting, or sitter, showing that such terms are figurative. Even the literal interpretations (like the fourth) treat it as a created object, not a divine attribute.

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that the Kursiy in the Qur'an can be understood in multiple ways, from pure metaphor to a created entity, reflecting the interpretive flexibility within Sunni theology.

Metaphorical Meanings of Ascend and Descend
Razi explains the metaphorical meaning of 'su'ud' and 'hoboot' in scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi interprets scriptural language of God's ascending and descending as metaphors for station, revelation, and punishment, not physical movement.

Core Idea
Divine ascent and descent are metaphors for God's action, not location.

Razi confronts a common challenge: scripture describes God as ascending and descending, yet reason demands God is beyond space. In this passage, he shows that such language is metaphorical throughout the Bible and Quran. He explains that 'ascend' metaphorically means high station, and 'descend' means low station, sending revelation, or punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice that Razi begins with literal and metaphorical meanings.
The passage uses examples from the Torah and Psalms.
2
Identify the metaphorical meanings
Find the three metaphorical meanings given for 'ascend' and 'descend': high/low station, sending revelation, and sending punishment.
Razi supports each meaning with scriptural quotes.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this metaphorical interpretation is important for the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).
It avoids attributing spatial movement to God.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And 'ascended' and 'descended' indicate place by elevation and decline in reality. Metaphorically, the word 'ascended' indicates high station, and the word 'descended' indicates low station... For Allah said to the stupid Israelite: 'The stranger who is among you will rise high over you, ascending, while you will descend, descending' (Deut 28:43). And the scholars of the Children of Israel say regarding what is mentioned in the Torah about Allah's ascending and descending: 'When we, the community of humans, are in the lowest of the low in place and rank of existence, in relation to the environment, and He is in the highest of the high in true existence, majesty, and grandeur, not a highness of place. And He wills communication of knowledge from Him and effusion of revelation upon some of us, expressed by the descent of revelation upon the prophet, or by the indwelling of Shekhinah in a place: by descent. And expressed by the elevation of the state of prophethood—that person—or removal of the Shekhinah from the place: by raising. So every descent and ascent you find attributed to the Blessed and Exalted is intended to mean this. And likewise, when a calamity befalls a nation or region—according to His ancient will which the prophetic books describe—before that calamity occurs, those people are deprived of Allah's regard for their deeds, then afterward He sends down punishment upon them. For this meaning is also alluded to by descent: man is too insignificant to have his deeds regarded and be punished for them were it not for the will. And this has been explained in the books of prophecy. And it was said: 'What is man that You remember him, and the son of man that You care for him?' (Psalms 8:5) refers to this meaning. Therefore, this was alluded to by descent. So He said: 'Come, let us go down and confuse their language there' (Gen 11:7), 'So the Lord came down to see' (Gen 11:5), 'I will go down and see' (Gen 18:21). And the whole meaning is about punishment upon the people of the lower region.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage explains that words indicating spatial movement like 'ascend' and 'descend' when applied to God are metaphorical. The literal meaning of 'su'ud' is elevation in place, and 'hoboot' is decline. Metaphorically, 'su'ud' indicates high station and 'hoboot' indicates low station. The author supports this with a quote from Deuteronomy and an explanation from Jewish scholars that divine ascent/descent refer to the sending of revelation, elevation of prophetic state, or sending of punishment. Scriptural examples from Genesis and Psalms are given to show that descent in scripture often refers to divine intervention or punishment. The passage demonstrates that even in earlier scriptures, such language was understood metaphorically.

Seeing God as Intellectual Vision
Razi defines 'ra'a' and 'nazar' as metaphors for intellectual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars interpret words like 'seeing' and 'looking' in divine contexts as intellectual perception rather than physical vision.

Core Idea
Divine vision is always metaphorical—it means knowledge, not eyesight.

This lesson explores a key principle in Islamic theology: when scripture speaks of God seeing or looking, it cannot mean physical vision. Imam Razi guides us through the evidence, showing how both the Quran and the Torah use these terms metaphorically for intellectual perception. The goal is to protect divine transcendence while taking scripture seriously.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two words analyzed
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the words 'ra'a' and 'nazara'.
These two words are the focus of the entire passage.
2
Identify literal vs metaphorical meanings
For each word, Razi gives a literal (physical) meaning and a metaphorical (intellectual) meaning. Find both.
The metaphorical meaning always involves the mind or intellect.
3
Examine the scriptural examples
Razi quotes Quranic verses (Sura al-Fil, Sura al-Isra) and Torah verses (Ecclesiastes, Numbers, Exodus) to show the metaphorical usage. List them.
These examples prove the pattern is consistent across scriptures.
4
Analyze the case of Moses
Moses covered his face fearing to look at God, yet he beheld the likeness of the Lord. Understand the scholars' interpretation: Moses feared God's light, not God Himself, and was rewarded with a vision of a representation.
This resolves the apparent contradiction and reinforces the metaphorical reading.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Why must 'seeing' and 'looking' be metaphorical for God? Because physical vision implies corporeality and limitation. The metaphorical reading preserves divine transcendence.
This principle is fundamental in Islamic theology (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن « رأى » على الحقيقة تدل على رؤية العين . وعلى المجاز تدل على العلم ، وعلى ادراك العقل . ففى القرآن الكريم : ( ألم تر كيف فعل ربك بأصحاب الفيل ؟ أي : ألم تعلم « ألم تر الى ربك كيف مد الظلال ؟ « أى لم تدرك ذلك بعقلك . وفي التوراة : وقلبى رأى كثيرا من الحكمة والعلم » ( جا ا : ١٦ ) فرأى بمعنى أدرك بعقله . وكل ما فى التوراة عن ( رأيت الرب ( ( ۱ ۰ ۲۲ : ۱۹ ) رأوا اله اسرائيل » ( خر ٢٤ : ١٠ ) يحمل على هذا المجاز : أي على الادراك العقلى ، لا على رؤية العين . • أعلم : أن « نظر ( على الحقيقة تدل على الالتفات بالعين للشيء وعلى المجاز تدل على التفات الذهن واقباله على تأمل الشيء . مثل ما جاء فى التوراة أن الله تعالى ( لم ير اثما في يعقوب ) ( عدد ۲۳ : ۲۱ ) لأن الاثم لا يرى بالعين. ومثل قول الله تعالى عن بنى اسرائيل انهم » ينظرون الى موسى » ( حز ۳۳ : ۸ ) أي أنهم يتعقبون أفعاله وأقواله ويتأملونها . ويقول علماء بنى اسرائيل : ان كل لفظة تدل على أن الله ينظر أو نظر أحد اليه ، فانها تدل على التفات الذهن واقباله على تأمل الشي . لا على رؤية العين . وقد جاء في التوراة أن موسى عليه السلام ستر وجهه لأنه خاف أن ينظر الى الله . وجاء فيها أنه عاين صورة الرب . ويفسر علماء التوراة هذين النصين بقولهم : ان موسى استحيا من الله بسبب خوفه نظر نور الله ، لا من النظر الى الله نفسه . ولما رأى الله استحياءه ، أفاض عليه تعالى من جوده وخيره ، ما قدر ، على مشاهدة شبه الرب وذلك جزاء له على أنه ستر وجهه لئلا ينظر الى . وتفسيرهم هذين النصين بهذا المعنى شبيه بقول رئيس الفلاسفة : « انه لا ينبغى للناظر فى كتبه أن ينسبه فيما يبحث عنه لقحة أو تجاسر وتهجم للكلام فى ما لا علم له به . بل ينبغي أن ينسبه المعرص والاجتهاد فى ايجاد وتحصيل اعتقادات صحيحة ، حسب مقدرة الانسان » و شبيه بقول موسى بن میمون: « انه ينبغى للانسان أن لا يتهجم لهذا الرب
And know that 'ra'a' (saw) in reality indicates vision of the eye. Metaphorically, it indicates knowledge and intellectual perception. In the Noble Qur'an: 'Did you not see how your Lord dealt with the companions of the elephant?' i.e., did you not know? 'Did you not see your Lord how He stretched the shade?' i.e., you did not perceive that with your intellect. And in the Torah: 'And my heart saw much wisdom and knowledge' (Ecclesiastes 1:16); 'ra'a' means he perceived with his intellect. And everything in the Torah about 'I saw the Lord' (Isaiah 6:1), 'They saw the God of Israel' (Exodus 24:10) is interpreted metaphorically: i.e., intellectual perception, not vision of the eye. And know that 'nazara' (looked) in reality indicates turning the eye towards something. Metaphorically, it indicates turning the mind and focusing on contemplating something. Like what came in the Torah that Allah 'did not see iniquity in Jacob' (Numbers 23:21), because iniquity cannot be seen with the eye. And like the saying of Allah about the Children of Israel that they 'look at Moses' (Ezekiel 33:8), i.e., they follow his actions and statements and contemplate them. And the scholars of the Children of Israel say: every word that indicates that Allah looks or someone looks at Him indicates turning of the mind and focusing on contemplating the thing, not vision of the eye. And it has come in the Torah that Moses, peace be upon him, covered his face because he feared to look at Allah. And it came in it that he beheld the likeness of the Lord. And the scholars of the Torah interpret these two texts by saying: Moses was shy before Allah because he feared looking at the light of Allah, not from looking at Allah Himself. And when Allah saw his shyness, He effused upon him from His bounty and goodness what enabled him to observe the likeness of the Lord, as a reward for his covering his face so as not to look at Him. And their interpretation of these two texts in this meaning is similar to the saying of the chief of the philosophers: 'It is not befitting for the student of his books to attribute to him impudence or insolence and recklessness in speaking about what he has no knowledge of. Rather, he should be attributed with good intention and effort in finding and acquiring correct beliefs according to human capability.' And similar to the saying of Moses bin Maimon: 'It is befitting for a man not to be reckless towards this Lord...'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exegesis of the words 'ra'a' (to see) and 'nazara' (to look) when used in relation to God. The author first defines 'ra'a': literally vision of the eye, metaphorically knowledge or intellectual perception, quoting Quranic verses and Ecclesiastes. Then he defines 'nazara': literally turning the eye, metaphorically turning the mind to contemplate, with examples from Numbers and Ezekiel. The passage then addresses the Torah accounts of Moses: he covered his face fearing to look at God, yet also beheld the likeness of the Lord. The scholars interpret this as Moses fearing to look at God's light, not God Himself, and his reward was a vision of a representation. The author compares this interpretive approach to that of Aristotle and Maimonides, emphasizing careful interpretation over literalism. The passage ends with a quote from Maimonides warning against recklessness in speaking about God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When the Bible or Quran says someone 'saw God', it means they physically saw Him with their eyes.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that 'saw' and 'looked' in divine contexts always mean intellectual perception, not physical vision, as evidenced by scriptural usage.

Key Takeaway
In divine contexts, 'seeing' and 'looking' must be understood as intellectual perception to preserve God's transcendence.

?

Moses Covering His Face: Modesty and Reward
Imam Razi presents two Torah passages and their scholarly interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's interpretation of Moses covering his face as an act of modesty before God's light, not fear of seeing God's essence.

Core Idea
Moses' modesty before God's light earned him a vision of His likeness.

This passage begins by presenting two Torah passages about Moses: he covered his face and he saw the image of the Lord. Imam Razi then recounts how Torah scholars resolve the apparent contradiction by explaining that Moses' fear was of seeing God's light, not God Himself, and his modesty was rewarded.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah texts
Read the two Torah statements: Moses covered his face fearing to look at God, and he beheld the image of the Lord. Notice the apparent contradiction.
2
Understand the scholarly interpretation
The scholars say Moses felt shy because of fear of seeing God's light, not God's essence. This resolves the contradiction.
3
Identify the reward for modesty
God rewarded Moses' modesty by enabling him to observe a likeness of the Lord.
This maintains divine transcendence.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this interpretation upholds the principle that God cannot be seen with physical eyes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جاء في التوراة أن موسى عليه السلام ستر وجهه لأنه خاف أن ينظر الى الله . وجاء فيها أنه عاين صورة الرب . ويفسر علماء النوراة هذين النصين بقولهم : ان موسى استحيا من الله بسبب خوفه نظر نور الله ، لا من النظر الى الله نفسه . ولما رأى الله استحياءه ، أفاض عليه تعالى من جوده وخيره ، ما قدر ، على مشاهدة شبه الرب وذلك جزاء له على أنه ستر وجهه لئلا ينظر الى . وتفسيرهم هذين النصين بهذا المعنى شبيه بقول رئيس الفلاسفة : « انه لا ينبغى للناظر فى كتبه أن ينسبه فيما يبحث عنه لقحة أو تجاسر وتهجم للكلام فى ما لا علم له به . بل ينبغي أن ينسبه المعرص والاجتهاد فى ايجاد وتحصيل اعتقادات صحيحة ، حسب مقدرة الانسان » و شبيه بقول موسى بن میمون: « انه ينبغى للانسان أن لا يتهجم لهذا الأمر العظيم الجليل من أول وهلة ، دون أن يروض نفسه فى العلوم والمعارف ويهذب أخلاقه حق التهذيب ويقتل شهواته وتشوقاته الخيالية ، فاذا حصل مقدمات يقينية وعلمها وعلم قوانين القياس والاستدلال وعلم وجوه التحفظ من أغاليط الذهن ، حينئذ يقدم للبحث في هذا المعنى ، ولا يقطع بأول رأى يقع له ولا يمد أفكاره أولا ويسلطها نحو ادراك الاله بل يستحي ويكف ويقف حتى يستنهض أولا أولا . وبعد هذا الكلام مباشرة يقول موسى بن میمون : « وعن هذا المعنى قيل : فسر موسى وجهه اذ خاف أن ينظر الى الله » ( خر ۳ : ٦ ) مضافا الى ما يدل عليه الظاهر من خوفه من نظر النور المتجلى لا أن الاله تدركه الأعين تعالى عن كل نقص علوا كبيرا – وحمد له عليه السلام ذلك ، وأفاض عليه تعالى من جوده وخيره ما أوجب له أن قبل فيه — أخبرا : « صورة الرب يعاين » ( عدا : ٨) وذكر الحكماء عليهم السلام أن ذلك لكونه ساترا وجهه أولا لئلا ينظر الى الرب . أما مختارى بنى اسرائيل ( خر ٢٤ : ١١ ) فانهم تهجموا ومدوا أفكارهم وأدركوا لكن ادراكا ليس بكامل ولذلك قال عنهم : « رأوا اله اسرائيل وتحت رجليه ( خر ٢٤ : ١٠ ) ولم يقل : « رأوا اله اسرائيل » فقط . اذ معرض القول انما هو الانتقاد عليهم رؤيتهم لا في وصف كيف رأوا . وهو انما انتقد عليهم صورة ادراكهم التي ضمنوها والتي أوجبت عليهم تهجمهم قبل كمالهم من الجسمانية ما ضمنوها فاستحقوا الهلاك )
And it has come in the Torah that Moses, peace be upon him, covered his face because he feared looking at God. And it came in it that he beheld the image of the Lord. And the scholars of the Torah interpret these two texts by their saying: Moses felt shy before God because of his fear of seeing the light of God, not from looking at God Himself. And when God saw his modesty, He poured upon him from His bounty and goodness what enabled him to observe the likeness of the Lord, and that was a reward for his covering his face so as not to look at Him. And their interpretation of these two texts in this sense is similar to the saying of the chief of the philosophers: 'It is not proper for the reader of his books to attribute to him impudence or audacity and brashness in speaking about what he has no knowledge of. Rather, one should attribute to him exertion and striving in seeking and acquiring correct beliefs according to human capacity.' And similar to the saying of Moses ben Maimon: 'It is proper for a person not to rush into this great and sublime matter at first glance, without disciplining himself in the sciences and knowledges, refining his morals properly, killing his passions and imaginary desires. When he has acquired certain premises and knows them, and knows the laws of syllogism and inference, and knows the ways of protecting against intellectual fallacies, then he may proceed to investigate this meaning, and he should not decide on the first opinion that occurs to him, nor extend his thoughts and direct them towards comprehending the divine, but rather he should show modesty, refrain, and pause until he gradually awakens.' And immediately after this speech, Moses ben Maimon says: 'And concerning this meaning, it was said: Moses covered his face when he feared looking at God' (Ex 3:6), in addition to what the apparent meaning indicates of his fear of seeing the manifested light, not that the divine is perceived by eyes—exalted is He above every deficiency, greatly—and God praised him, peace be upon him, for that, and poured upon him from His bounty and goodness what necessitated that he be accepted in it—He informed: 'The image of the Lord he beholds' (Num 8:8). And the sages, peace be upon them, mentioned that that was because he covered his face first so as not to look at the Lord. As for the chosen ones of the Children of Israel (Ex 24:11), they rushed and extended their thoughts and perceived, but with an incomplete perception. Therefore He said about them: 'They saw the God of Israel and under His feet' (Ex 24:10), and He did not say 'They saw the God of Israel' only. For the object of the statement is the criticism of their vision, not in describing how they saw. Rather, He criticized them for the form of their perception which they included and which necessitated their rushing before their perfection, from the corporeality that they included, so they deserved destruction.)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory and argumentative passage. The author begins by recounting two Torah passages about Moses: he covered his face out of fear of looking at God, and he beheld the image of the Lord. The Torah scholars interpret these as referring to the light of God, not God's essence, and that Moses' modesty was rewarded with a vision of a likeness. The author then parallels this interpretation with the saying of 'the chief of the philosophers' who advocates for modesty and striving in acquiring correct beliefs without brashness. Then he quotes Maimonides at length, who advises against rushing into divine matters without proper intellectual and moral preparation. Maimonides then explicitly links this to Moses covering his face, explaining that the apparent meaning of fearing to look at God refers to the manifested light, not God's essence. Finally, the author contrasts Moses with the nobles of Israel who rushed and attempted to perceive God with an incomplete understanding, leading to criticism and near destruction. The passage emphasizes the proper method of studying God: with reverence, preparation, and metaphorical interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions.

Key Terms
ستر وجهه (covering his face)
نظر نور الله (seeing the light of God)
شبه الرب (likeness of the Lord)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Torah scholars quoted by Imam Razi, why did Moses cover his face?
A) He was ashamed of his own body
B) He feared seeing the light of God
C) He did not want to be seen by God
D) He was preparing to pray

Key Takeaway
Moses' covering his face was an act of modesty before God's light, not a fear of seeing God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt genuine shyness before God, not fear of punishment but awe of His presence? What would it take for you to 'cover your face' in your daily worship?
?
Moses' modesty earned him a reward. Is there an area in your life where your lack of humility might be blocking you from deeper spiritual insight?
?
Do you sometimes confuse God's blessings (His 'light') with God Himself? How can you cultivate a clearer distinction in your heart this week?

Divine Transcendence

Maimonides' Method for Studying the Divine
Imam Razi compares the Torah scholars' interpretation to the methodological advice of the chief of philosophers and Maimonides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maimonides' method for approaching divine matters with intellectual and moral preparation, as quoted by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Do not rush into divine matters; prepare through knowledge, ethics, and logical training.

Imam Razi draws a parallel between the Torah scholars' interpretation and the methodological advice of philosophers, particularly Maimonides. This passage quotes Maimonides at length, emphasizing the need for proper preparation before investigating the divine.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the parallel
Notice that the Torah scholars' interpretation is similar to the advice of the chief of philosophers and Maimonides.
2
Read Maimonides' advice
Read the extended quote from Maimonides carefully. He lists steps: discipline in sciences, morals, premises, logic, and then gradual approach with modesty.
3
Identify key ingredients
Identify the key components: self-discipline, certainty, logical skills, and humility.
4
Connect to Moses
Reflect on how this method mirrors Moses' own modesty and preparation before receiving divine vision.

Source Text

The Nobles of Israel: A Warning Against Rashness
Imam Razi applies Maimonides' advice to the story of Moses and contrasts it with the nobles of Israel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi contrasts Moses' reverent approach with the nobles of Israel's rashness, and the consequences.

Core Idea
Rushing to perceive God leads to incomplete understanding and destruction.

After quoting Maimonides, Imam Razi directly applies the lesson to the biblical narrative: Moses' modesty was rewarded, while the nobles of Israel who rushed were criticized and nearly destroyed. This reinforces the necessity of proper method.

Guided Path
1
Read Maimonides' link to Moses
Notice how Maimonides explicitly connects his advice to Moses covering his face, interpreting it as modesty before God's light.
2
Understand the contrast with the nobles
The nobles of Israel rushed and attempted to perceive God, but their perception was incomplete and included corporeal concepts.
3
Analyze the criticism
The Quranic verse criticizes them not for seeing, but for the form of their perception which involved corporeality due to rushing.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Because of their rashness, they were criticized and deserved destruction.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the nobles of Israel had a true vision of God.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that their perception was incomplete and included corporeal concepts, leading to criticism.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why were the nobles of Israel criticized?
A) They saw God's feet
B) Their perception was incomplete and included corporeality
C) They did not believe in God
D) They worshipped idols

Key Takeaway
Rushing to perceive God leads to incomplete, corporeal perceptions that incur divine displeasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rushed to make a judgment about God or religion without proper knowledge? What might be the consequence of such rashness in your own faith?
?
When you think of God, do you sometimes imagine Him in limited, human-like terms? How does this passage challenge you to purify your conception of Him?
?
Consider your own spiritual state: Are you more like Moses, who covered his face in reverence and fear, or like the nobles who rushed forward? What step can you take today to cultivate more awe and patience in your relationship with Allah?

Critique of Visibility-Direction Argument
Razi refutes the visibility-to-direction argument with a dilemma and an inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's two-pronged critique of the argument that God's visibility in the afterlife entails His occupying a direction.

Core Idea
If the premise is self-evident, the argument is redundant; if inferential, it is unproven and self-contradictory.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi was a master of dialectical theology. Here he takes aim at an opponent's attempt to prove that God occupies a direction based on His visibility. Razi does not merely reject the conclusion; he dissects the logical structure of the argument, exposing its weaknesses whether its key premise is taken as self-evident or as requiring proof. Let's watch how he turns the opponent's own admissions against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flavor of Razi's style. Then read the full English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the shift from 'if this is self-evident' to 'if it is inferential'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Razi presents two scenarios: either the premise 'every visible is in a direction' is self-evident, or it is inferential. Find where he discusses each and note the consequences he draws.
In the first horn, the argument is redundant. In the second, it lacks proof.
3
Analyze the inconsistency argument
Razi points out that the opponent admits God, when seen, lacks size, extension, and parts—unlike visible things in this world. Ask yourself: why does this undermine their analogy?
The opponent's own concession about dissimilarity weakens their case.
4
Summarize Razi's conclusion
After working through the passage, try to state Razi's core objection in your own words. What makes the visibility argument for direction invalid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو مختص بالجهة وثبت أن البارى تعالى قائم بالنفس فوجب القطع بأنه مختص بالجهة لأن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد وجب أن يكون في الغائب كذلك. فإذا كان هذا الوجه حاصلا في إثبات كونه تعالى في الجهة ، كان إثبات كونه تعالى في الجهة بكونه مرئياً ، ثم إثبات أن كل ما كان مرئياً فهو مختص بالجهة ، تطويل من غير فائدة ، ومن غير مزيد شرح وبيان . وأما إن قلنا : إن قولنا إن كل مرئي فهو مختص بالجهة : ليست مقدمة بديهية ، بل هي مقدمة استدلالية . فحينئذ ما لم يذكروا على صحتها دليلا ، لا تصير هذه المقدمة يقينية وأيضاً إنا كما لا نعقل مرئياً في الشاهد، إلا إذا كان مقابلا أو في حكم المقابل للرائى . فكذلك لا نعقل مرئياً إلا إذا كان صغيراً وكبيراً ، أو ممتداً في الجهات ، أو مؤتلفاً من الأجزاء . وهم يقولون : إنه تعالى يرى ، لا صغيراً ولا كبيرا ، ولا ممتداً في الجهات والجوانب والأحياز. فإذا جاز لكم أن تحكموا بأن الغائب مخالف للشاهد في هذا الباب ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن المرئي في الشاهد - وإن وجب كونه مقابلا للرائى إلا أن المرئي في الغائب - لا يجوز أن يكون كذلك؟
He is specifically associated with direction. And it is established that the Creator, exalted, is self-subsistent. So it is necessary to decisively conclude that He is specifically associated with direction, because necessary knowledge obtains that everything established in the witnessed (shāhid) must be likewise in the unseen (ghāʾib). If this approach is used to prove that He, exalted, is in a direction, then proving that He is in a direction by virtue of being visible, and then proving that everything visible is specifically associated with direction, is a lengthy process without benefit and without additional explanation and clarification. But if we say: our statement 'every visible is specifically associated with direction' is not a self-evident premise, but rather an inferential premise, then unless they provide proof for its validity, this premise does not become certain. Also, just as we do not conceive a visible in the witnessed except if it is facing or in the ruling of facing the viewer, so too we do not conceive a visible except if it is small or large, or extended in directions, or composed of parts. Yet they say: He, exalted, is seen, neither small nor large, nor extended in directions, sides, and spaces. So if you are allowed to rule that the unseen is contrary to the witnessed in this regard, then why is it not also allowed that the visible in the witnessed—even though it must be facing the viewer—that the visible in the unseen is not allowed to be like that?
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that claims God must be in a direction because He is visible (in the afterlife). The opponent argues: everything visible in the witnessed world occupies a direction; God is visible; therefore God occupies a direction. Razi first points out that if the premise 'every visible is in a direction' is taken as self-evident based on the analogy from witnessed to unseen, then the proof is redundant and adds nothing. Alternatively, if it is not self-evident but inferential, then it requires proof, which has not been provided. Moreover, the opponent undermines their own analogy: in the witnessed world, a visible object must be facing the viewer, have size, extension, and parts. But they admit that God, when seen, has none of these attributes. If they allow that the unseen can differ from the witnessed in these respects, then why cannot the attribute of 'occupying a direction' also differ? Thus the argument is inconsistent.

Key Terms
مرئي
مختص بالجهة
شاهد
غائب
مقدمة بديهية
مقدمة استدلالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must be in a direction because He will be seen in the afterlife, and everything visible is in a direction.
Correction: Razi shows that the premise is either redundant or unproven, and the opponent's own admissions about God's unlike-ness undermine the analogy from the witnessed world.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the problem with the premise 'every visible is specifically associated with direction'?
A) It is self-evident but false.
B) It is either not self-evident and unproven, or if self-evident, it is contradicted by the opponent's own admission that God does not resemble visible things.
C) It is a necessary truth from the witnessed world.
D) It is only true for non-divine beings.

Key Takeaway
Razi's critique shows that the visibility argument for God's direction is either redundant or unproven, and the opponent's own admissions defeat their analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah in the afterlife, do you unconsciously picture Him as having a direction or shape? How does Razi's critique challenge that mental image, and what will you do to correct it?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly logical argument can be flawed if its premises are unexamined. Think of a belief you hold strongly about Allah. What is the basis for that belief? Are you willing to test it with the same rigor Razi applies here?
?
The opponent admitted that God, when seen, lacks size, extension, and parts, yet they insisted on direction. Where in your own thinking do you accept a similar inconsistency? Ask Allah to show you any hidden contradictions in your faith, and commit to resolving them with knowledge.

The Fourth Doubt and Its Refutation by Prostration
Imam Razi states the fourth shubha and gives the first part of his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth shubha (doubt) regarding raising hands to heaven and Imam Razi's initial refutation using the analogy of prostration.

Core Idea
If prostration implied God is below, then raising hands implies He is above – a false inference.

Imam Razi presents the fourth objection raised by those who claim God is spatially above. He then provides a powerful counter-argument: the same logic would require that God is beneath us when we prostrate, which is absurd. This lesson examines both the doubt and its initial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the shubha and first refutation
Read the Arabic passage describing the doubt and Razi's first answer. Note the structure: objection then response.
2
Identify the logical flaw
Notice that Razi uses the practice of prostration to show the inconsistency. Ask yourself: why is this a valid refutation?
3
Connect to broader theme
Understand that this refutation is part of a larger argument that universal practices do not prove theological truths.
This lesson sets up the need for alternative explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : تمسكوا برفع الأيدى إلى السماء . قالوا : وهذا شيء يفعله أرباب النحل . فدل على أنه تقرر في جميع عقول الخلق كون الإله في جهة فوق . الجواب : إن هذا معارض بما تقرر في جميع عقول الخلق : أنهم عند تعظيم خالق العالم يضعون جباههم على الأرض . ولما لم يدل هذا على كون خالق العالم في الأرض ، لم يدل ما ذكروه على أنه في السماء . وأيضاً : فالخلق إنما يقدمون على رفع الأيدى إلى السماء ، لوجوه أخرى وراء اعتقادهم أن خالق العالم في السماء : فالأول : إن معظم الأشياء فيفها للخلق ، ظهور الأنوار . وأنها إنما تظهر من جانب السموات . والثاني : إن مبنى حياة الخلق على استنشاق النفس . وليس ذلك الاستنشاق إلا من الهواء. والهواء ليس إلا موجودا فوق الأرض فلهذا السبب كان فوق الأرض أشرف مما تحت الأرض . الثالث : إن نزول الغيث من جهة الفوق . ولما كانت هذه الأشياء التي هي منافع الخلق ، إنما تنزل من جانب السموات ، لا جرم كان ذلك الجانب عندهم أشرف . وتعلق الخاطر بالأشرف أقوى من تعلقه بالأخص . وهذا هو السبب في رفع الأيدى إلى السماء . وأيضا : إنه تعالى جعل العرش قبلة لدعائنا ، كما جعل الكعبة قبلة لصلاتنا . وأيضا : إنه تعالى جعل الملائكة وسائط في مصالح هذا العالم . قال تعالى : فالمديرات أمر ، وقال تعالى : فالمقسمات أمر
The fourth doubt: They clung to the raising of hands to the sky. They said: This is something done by the people of the religions. So it indicates that it is established in all the minds of creation that the deity is in a direction above. The answer: This is countered by what is established in all the minds of creation: that when they magnify the Creator of the world, they place their foreheads on the ground. And since this does not indicate that the Creator of the world is on the ground, what they mentioned does not indicate that He is in the sky. Also, creation only proceed to raise hands to the sky for other reasons beyond their belief that the Creator is in the sky: First, that most things beneficial to creation are the appearance of lights, and they only appear from the direction of the heavens. Second, that the basis of life for creation is the inhalation of air, and that inhalation is only from the air, and air is only found above the earth. For this reason, what is above the earth is more noble than what is under the earth. Third, that rain descends from above. Since these things which are benefits to creation only descend from the side of the heavens, inevitably that side is considered more noble by them. And the attachment of the heart to the more noble is stronger than its attachment to the lower. This is the reason for raising hands to the sky. Also, He, exalted, made the Throne the qibla of our supplication, just as He made the Kaaba the qibla of our prayer. Also, He, exalted, made the angels intermediaries in the interests of this world. He said: 'By those who direct affairs' (79:5), and He said: 'By those who distribute affairs' (51:4).
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection (shubha) and its refutation. The objection argues that the universal human practice of raising hands towards the sky in supplication and worship proves that God is located in a direction (above). Razi's answer is multi-pronged: First, he points out a counter-practice: the same people prostrate by placing their foreheads on the ground, which would analogously imply that God is on the ground. This shows the argument from practice is inconsistent. Second, he offers natural and psychological explanations for why people raise their hands upward: (1) beneficial lights come from the heavens, (2) air (breath) comes from above, (3) rain falls from above—all these make the 'above' direction seem more noble and thus attract the heart. Third, he gives theological reasons: God made the Throne a qibla for supplication, just as the Kaaba is a qibla for prayer, and angels are intermediaries from above. These explain the practice without requiring God to be spatially above.

Key Terms
رفع الأيدى
جهة فوق
تعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universal human practice of raising hands to heaven proves that God is spatially above.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the same logic would require that prostration proves God is below, which is contradictory. Therefore, such practices do not constitute valid theological evidence.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's primary counter-argument to the claim that raising hands to the sky proves God is above?
A) Raising hands is not a universal practice among all people.
B) The same people also prostrate on the ground, which would analogously imply God is on the ground.
C) The practice is only done by the people of the books, not all religions.
D) The sky is not a literal direction.

Key Takeaway
Universal practices like raising hands do not by themselves prove theological truths, as shown by the contradictory implication of prostration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a common Muslim practice, like raising hands in dua, proves something about God that you later found to be incorrect? How does this lesson change how you view your own actions?
?
When you prostrate, do you consider the theological implication that some might draw from it? Will you now be more careful about inferring God's attributes from human actions?
?
Are there deeply held beliefs you have that are based on 'what everyone does' rather than on clear evidence? Will you critically examine them after learning how Razi refuted the objectors?

Natural Explanations for Raising Hands to Heaven
Imam Razi gives three natural reasons why people raise hands to heaven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three natural explanations for why people raise their hands to heaven, beyond any belief about God's location.

Core Idea
Lights, breath, and rain come from above, making the sky naturally seem nobler.

Imam Razi continues his refutation by offering alternative, natural reasons why people raise their hands upward. These reasons are based on common benefits: beneficial lights come from the heavens, we breathe air that is above the earth, and rain descends from above. These natural factors explain the practice without requiring a theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the three natural reasons
Read the Arabic text that lists the first, second, and third reasons. Notice how each reason is presented and linked to a benefit.
2
Understand the logic
Razi argues that because these benefits come from above, people naturally associate that direction with nobility and thus raise their hands in supplication.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Realize that Razi is providing an alternative explanation for the practice, undermining the opponent's claim that the practice is evidence for God's location.
This shows the opponent's argument is not the only possible explanation.

Theological Reasons: The Throne as Qibla and Angels
Imam Razi gives two theological reasons for raising hands: the Throne as qibla and angels as intermediaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's theological explanations for why people raise their hands to heaven, specifically the Throne as a qibla for supplication and the role of angels as intermediaries.

Core Idea
God made the Throne a qibla for supplication and angels intermediaries, explaining the upward gesture.

Imam Razi concludes his refutation by providing two theological reasons that account for the practice of raising hands: first, God designated the Throne as the direction (qibla) for supplication, just as the Kaaba is for prayer; second, angels, who are in heaven, act as intermediaries in worldly affairs. These reasons give a positive explanation for the practice, rooted in divine command and angelic mediation.

Guided Path
1
Read the theological reasons
Read the final part of the passage where Razi mentions the Throne as qibla and the angels. Notice the Quranic verses quoted.
2
Understand the concept of qibla
Recognize that a qibla is a direction for worship, but does not imply God's location. Similarly, the Throne is a direction for supplication without spatializing God.
3
Link to the refutation
Realize that Razi is providing alternative explanations that do not require God being spatially above; instead, they are based on divine wisdom and angelic roles.

So it indicates that it is established in all the minds of creation that the deity is in a direction above. The answer: This is countered by what is established in all the minds of creation: that when they magnify the Creator of the world, they place their foreheads on the ground. And since this does not indicate that the Creator of the world is on the ground, what they mentioned does not indicate that He is in the sky. Also, creation only proceed to raise hands to the sky for other reasons beyond their belief that the Creator is in the sky: First, that most things beneficial to creation are the appearance of lights, and they only appear from the direction of the heavens. Second, that the basis of life for creation is the inhalation of air, and that inhalation is only from the air, and air is only found above the earth. For this reason, what is above the earth is more noble than what is under the earth. Third, that rain descends from above. Since these things which are benefits to creation only descend from the side of the heavens, inevitably that side is considered more noble by them. And the attachment of the heart to the more noble is stronger than its attachment to the lower. This is the reason for raising hands to the sky. Also, He, exalted, made the Throne the qibla of our supplication, just as He made the Kaaba the qibla of our prayer. Also, He, exalted, made the angels intermediaries in the interests of this world. He said: 'By those who direct affairs' (79:5), and He said: 'By those who distribute affairs' (51:4).
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection (shubha) and its refutation. The objection argues that the universal human practice of raising hands towards the sky in supplication and worship proves that God is located in a direction (above). Razi's answer is multi-pronged: First, he points out a counter-practice: the same people prostrate by placing their foreheads on the ground, which would analogously imply that God is on the ground. This shows the argument from practice is inconsistent. Second, he offers natural and psychological explanations for why people raise their hands upward: (1) beneficial lights come from the heavens, (2) air (breath) comes from above, (3) rain falls from above—all these make the 'above' direction seem more noble and thus attract the heart. Third, he gives theological reasons: God made the Throne a qibla for supplication, just as the Kaaba is a qibla for prayer, and angels are intermediaries from above. These explain the practice without requiring God to be spatially above.

What does Razi compare the Throne to in this passage?
A) The Kaaba

Key Takeaway
Razi provides theological explanations (Throne as qibla and angels as intermediaries) that account for raising hands without implying God's spatial location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in dua, is your heart truly oriented towards the Throne as a qibla, or is it merely a habitual gesture? What would it mean for you to make your supplication as intentional as your prayer facing the Kaaba?
?
If angels are only intermediaries appointed by Allah, do you ever find yourself attributing your provision or answered prayers directly to an angel rather than to Allah? How can you train your heart to see the angels as mere instruments and Allah as the sole Disposer of affairs?
?
The Throne is a direction for dua, yet Allah is not confined to it. Does your mind still imagine Allah 'up there' when you pray? What would change in your worship if you truly internalised that Allah is beyond all directions and yet answers every sincere turn of the heart?

Reasons for Raising Hands to the Sky Beyond Spatial Deity
Razi enumerates alternative reasons for raising hands to the sky, refuting anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative reasons Imam al-Razi provides for the practice of raising hands to the sky, and how these reasons refute anthropomorphic interpretations.

Core Idea
Raising hands to heaven has multiple explanations that do not require believing God is there.

Imam al-Razi counters the claim that raising hands to the sky proves God's location in heaven. He offers several alternative explanations rooted in human psychology, natural benefits, ritual practice, and angelic intermediaries. This passage clarifies that the physical act of supplication does not imply spatial deity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the overall flow: Razi begins by rejecting the opponent's evidence, then lists alternative reasons.
The passage is a continuous argument; try to see how each reason builds on the previous.
2
Identify the three practical reasons
Find the three reasons Razi gives that are based on observable benefits: lights appearing from the sky, air above the earth, and rain descending from above.
These reasons show that the sky is associated with benefits, making it noble in people's minds.
3
Understand the psychological reasoning
Razi explains that because these benefits come from the heavens, people consider that direction nobler, and the heart attaches more strongly to the nobler. This is why hands are raised upward.
This connects the practical reasons to the action of raising hands.
4
Consider the ritual and angelic reasons
Razi adds two more reasons: the Throne (al-arsh) is the qibla for supplication, and angels are intermediaries for benefits, so raising hands may be directed to them.
These reasons show that the act has ritual and intercessory dimensions.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Think about how all these reasons together refute the claim that raising hands implies God is in heaven. Razi shows multiple plausible explanations without needing spatial deity.
This lesson protects divine transcendence (tanzih) from anthropomorphic interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدل هذا على كون خالق العالم في الأرض ، لم يدل ما ذكروه على أنه في السماء . وأيضاً : فالخلق إنما يقدمون على رفع الأيدى إلى السماء ، لوجوه أخرى وراء اعتقادهم أن خالق العالم في السماء :
فالأول : إن معظم الأشياء التي فيها ظهور الأنوار ، وإنما تظهر من جانب السموات .
والثاني : إن مبنى حياة الخلق على استنشاق النفس . وليس ذلك الاستنشاق إلا من الهواء. والهواء ليس إلا موجودا فوق الأرض ، فلهذا السبب كان فوق الأرض أشرف مما تحت الأرض .
والثالث : إن نزول الغيث من جهة الفوق .
ولما كانت هذه الأشياء التى هى منافع الخلق ، إنما تنزل من جانب السموات ، لا جرم كان ذلك الجانب عندهم أشرف . وتعلق الخاطر بالأشرف أقوى من تعلقه بالأخص . وهذا هو السبب في رفع الأيدى إلى السماء .
وأيضا : إنه تعالى جعل العرش قبلة لدعائنا ، كما جعل الكعبة قبلة لصلاتنا .
وأيضا : إنه تعالى جعل الملائكة وسائط فى مصالح هذا العالم .
قال تعالى : فالمديرات أمر ، وقال تعالى : فالمقسمات أمر . وأجمعوا على أن جبريل عليه السلام ملك الوحي والتنزيل والنبوة ، وميكائيل ملك الأرزاق، وملك الموت ملك الوفاة . وكذا القول في سائر الأمور . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يبعد أن يكون الغرض من رفع الأيدى إلى السماء : رفع الأيدى إلى الملائكة . وبالله التوفيق.
This indicates that the Creator of the world is in the earth; what they mentioned does not indicate that He is in the sky. Also: people only proceed to raise their hands towards the sky for other reasons beyond their belief that the Creator of the world is in the sky:
First: Most things in which lights appear only appear from the direction of the heavens.
Second: The basis of people's life is breathing air, and that breathing is only from the air, and air exists only above the earth, so for this reason what is above the earth is nobler than what is beneath the earth.
Third: Rain descends from the direction of above.
And since these things, which are benefits for people, only descend from the direction of the heavens, necessarily that direction is nobler in their view. Attachment of the heart to the nobler is stronger than attachment to the lesser. This is the reason for raising hands towards the sky.
Also: God Most High made the Throne (al-arsh) the direction (qibla) for our supplication, just as He made the Kaaba the direction for our prayer.
Also: God Most High made the angels intermediaries in the affairs of this world. He said, Exalted is He: 'By those who direct affairs' (al-Nazi'at 5), and He said: 'And by those who distribute affairs' (al-Dhariyat 4). And they agreed unanimously that Gabriel, peace be upon him, is the angel of revelation, scripture, and prophethood; Michael is the angel of provisions; and the Angel of Death is the angel of death. And the same is said regarding all other matters. And if the matter is so, it is not far-fetched that the purpose of raising hands towards the sky is raising hands towards the angels. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is part of a refutation of an anthropomorphic argument that claims God is spatially located in heaven because people raise their hands towards the sky in supplication. The author, Imam al-Razi, first states that the opponent's evidence does not prove their claim—the fact that people raise hands to heaven does not indicate that the Creator is there. He then provides three alternative reasons for this practice, all grounded in observable benefits and human psychology.

First reason: Most luminous phenomena (like light and stars) appear from the sky, so the sky is associated with light and goodness.
Second reason: Humans depend on breathing air, which exists above the earth, making the upper region nobler than the lower.
Third reason: Rain, a vital benefit, descends from above.

Since these benefits come from the heavens, people naturally consider that direction as nobler. The human heart attaches more strongly to what is noble, which explains raising hands upward.

Razi then adds two further arguments: 1) God made the Throne (al-arsh) the direction for supplication, similar to the Kaaba for prayer, so turning to the Throne is a ritual act, not spatial belief. 2) Angels are intermediaries for worldly affairs—Gabriel for revelation, Michael for provisions, the Angel of Death for death—so raising hands may be directed towards the angels as a request for their intercession in these benefits.

Thus, the practice of raising hands has multiple plausible explanations that do not require believing God is in heaven. The passage ends with 'And with God is success,' a typical pious closing.

?

?
Razi lists reasons for raising hands that do not require limiting God. Reflect on your own prayer: Are there any actions you perform that you might be associating with a limited understanding of God? How can you purify your worship from such subtle anthropomorphism?
?
The angels are intermediaries for worldly benefits. But you ask God directly. Do you truly see God as the direct provider, or do you unconsciously depend on means and forget the Creator? What would change if you internalized that no angel or direction holds power except by His will?

The Karramiyyah's Definition of Body as Self-Subsistent
Razi introduces the Karramiyyah's redefinition and calls it a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyyah's claim that Allah is a body defined as self-subsistent and why al-Razi initially characterizes the dispute as merely verbal.

Core Idea
When definitions differ, disputes can become verbal – but Razi shows the deeper problem.

The Karramiyyah sect attempted to reconcile their anthropomorphic tendencies with divine transcendence by redefining the term 'body'. They claimed Allah is a body, but by that they meant self-subsistent and independent of location – not composed of parts. Al-Razi begins his refutation by noting that if this is all they mean, the disagreement becomes merely about words. However, he then exposes the logical impossibility behind their definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Razi states the Karramiyyah view, then his initial observation.
The passage begins with 'اعلم' which signals important information.
2
Identify the Karramiyyah definition
Find the phrase where the Karramiyyah define what they mean by 'body'. They explicitly deny composition and affirm self-subsistence.
They say: 'لا نريد به كونه تعالى مؤلفا من الأجزاء، ومركبا من الأبعاض. بل تريد به كونه تعالى غنيا عن المحل، قائما بالنفس.'
3
Understand the verbal dispute
Razi says that on this definition the dispute becomes a 'verbal dispute' (نزاع لفظي). Ask yourself: why would changing a definition make a disagreement merely verbal?
If both sides agree that Allah is not composite, the only difference is the label 'body' – which is a matter of words, not reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في الرد على الكرامية القائلين بأنه تعالى جسم بمعنى كونه تعالى غنيا عن المحل قائما بالنفس
اعلم : أن المشهور عن قدماء الكرامية : إطلاق لفظ الجسم على الله تعالى . إلا أنهم يقولون : لا نريد به كونه تعالى مؤلفا من الأجزاء ، ومركبا من الأبعاض . بل تريد به كونه تعالى غنيا عن المحل، قائما بالنفس. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإنه يصير النزاع فى أنه تعالى جسم أو لا؟ : نزاعا لفظيا. أولا؟ هذا حاصل ما قيل فى هذا الباب . إلا أنا نقول : كل ما كان مختصا بحيز أو جهة ، ويمكن أن يشار إليه بالحس . فذلك المشار إليه إما أن لا يبقى منه شيء في جوانبه الست ، وإما أن يبقى . فإن لم يبق منه شيء في جوانبه الست ، فهذا يكون كالجوهر الفرد، وكالنقطة التي لا تتجزأ ، ويكون في غاية الصغر والحقارة . ولا أظن أن عاقلا يرضى أن يقول : إن إله العالم كذلك . وأما إن بقى شيء في جوانبه الست ، أو في أحد هذه الجوانب . فهذا يقتضى كونه مؤلفا مركبا من جزئين وأكثر . وأقصى ما فى الباب : أن يقول قائل : إن تلك الأجزاء لا تقبل التفرق والانحلال . إلا أن هذا لا يمنع من كونه في نفسه مركبا مؤلفا ، كما أن الفيلسوف يقول : « الفلك جسم ، إلا أنه لا يقبل الخرق والالتئام .
The Sixth Chapter: On the Refutation of the Karramiyyah who say that He, Exalted, is a body in the sense of being independent of location, self-subsistent. Know that the well-known [view] from the ancient Karramiyyah is the use of the term 'body' (jism) for Allah, Exalted. Except that they say: we do not mean by it that He, Exalted, is composed of parts and made up of limbs. Rather, we mean by it that He, Exalted, is independent of location, self-subsistent. On this supposition, the dispute concerning whether He, Exalted, is a body or not becomes a verbal dispute. Or not? This is the gist of what is said on this topic. However, we say: everything that is specific to a space or direction and can be pointed to by the senses, that which is pointed to either has nothing remaining in its six directions, or something remains. If nothing remains in its six directions, then it is like the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) and like the point that cannot be divided, and it is extreme smallness and contemptibility. And I do not think a rational person would be satisfied to say that the God of the world is like that. But if something remains in its six directions or in one of these directions, then this necessitates it being composed and compounded of two or more parts. And the utmost extent of the matter is that someone might say: those parts do not admit of separation and dissolution. But this does not prevent it from being in itself compounded and composed, just as the philosopher says: 'The celestial sphere is a body, but it does not admit of tearing and rejoining.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyyah sect, who assert that Allah is a body (jism) but define 'body' not as a composite of parts but as something self-subsistent (qā'imun bi nafsihī) and independent of location (ghanī 'an al-maḥall). Razi begins by stating the well-known view of the ancient Karramiyyah: they apply the term 'body' to Allah, but they clarify that they do not mean composition from parts or limbs; rather they mean 'self-subsistent' and 'independent of location'. Razi notes that on this interpretation the dispute becomes merely verbal—if they mean by 'body' something that is neither divisible nor occupying space, then there is no real disagreement. However, Razi then provides a logical argument to show that their definition is untenable. He argues: anything that occupies a specific space or direction and is perceptible by the senses must be subject to spatial considerations. Such an entity either has no extension in any of its six directions (like an indivisible atom) or has extension in some or all directions. If it has no extension (i.e., occupies no space), then it is like an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) or a mathematical point—extremely small and contemptible. No rational person would accept that the Creator of the universe is such a minute, negligible thing. If, on the other hand, it does have extension in one or more directions, then that implies it is composed of parts and is therefore a compound (murakkab). The opponent might retort that those parts are indivisible and cannot separate. But Razi counters: even if the parts are inseparable, the entity is still composite in itself, just as the philosophers say the celestial sphere is a body that cannot be torn or rejoined—yet it is still a body composed of parts. Thus, the Karramiyyah's position either leads to absurd diminution or to composition, both of which are incompatible with divine transcendence.

Key Terms
جسم
غني عن المحل
قائم بالنفس
نزاع لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that redefining 'body' to mean 'self-subsistent' resolves the problem of anthropomorphism.
Correction: Razi later shows that even with this redefinition, the claim that Allah is a body leads to logical absurdities, so the dispute is not merely verbal.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what makes the dispute with the Karramiyyah potentially verbal?
A) Because both sides agree that Allah is not a body.
B) Because they define 'body' differently from the usual meaning.
C) Because the Karramiyyah deny God's existence.
D) Because Razi refuses to argue with them.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyyah redefined 'body' to avoid composition, but Razi shows that even this redefinition does not escape logical problems.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah, do you ever unknowingly adopt definitions that come from your own assumptions rather than from revelation? How can this lesson push you to examine every word you use to describe Him?
?
The Karramiyyah clung to the word 'body' even after redefining it. What words or labels in your own faith—like 'power', 'mercy', or 'control'—might you be holding onto, redefining them to fit your comfort rather than submitting to their true meaning as taught by the scholars?
?
Razi points out that a verbal dispute can still carry deep consequences. How often do you dismiss theological disagreements as 'just words' instead of digging into the underlying beliefs? What would it take for you to take theological precision seriously in your own heart?

Razi's Refutation: The Spatial Argument
Razi presents a spatial argument disproving the Karramiyyah's corporeal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's logical argument that any entity occupying space must be either an indivisible point (too small for God) or a composite (too limited), thus refuting the Karramiyyah's definition.

Core Idea
The six directions prove that any spatial being must be either tiny or composite – neither befits Allah.

Having noted the Karramiyyah's redefinition, Razi now provides a rigorous argument to show that their position is incoherent. He examines the implications of associating Allah with spatial location and direction. Using the concept of the 'six directions' (front, back, up, down, left, right), he demonstrates that any being that can be pointed to must either have no extension (like an indivisible atom) or have extension (implying composition). Both alternatives are incompatible with divine majesty. He also refutes the potential objection that the parts might be inseparable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the logical structure: Razi presents a dichotomy and examines both horns.
The passage begins with 'إلا أنا نقول' marking the start of the refutation.
2
Understand the two possibilities
Razi says anything occupying space either has no remainder in its six directions (like a point) or has some remainder (implying parts). Identify these two options.
The six directions are the standard spatial axes.
3
Analyze each option
First option: no extension leads to an indivisible atom – absurdly small for God. Second option: extension implies composition – even if inseparable, still composite.
Razi uses the celestial sphere analogy to show that inseparability does not negate composition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God has a body with inseparable parts, that avoids composition.
Correction: Razi shows that inseparable parts still make a composite entity; using the celestial sphere example, he argues that inseparability does not negate composition.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the problem with an entity that has no extension in its six directions?
A) It would be infinite.
B) It would be like an indivisible atom, too small and contemptible for God.
C) It would be composed of parts.
D) It would not exist.

Key Takeaway
Razi's spatial argument proves that any corporeal conception of God leads either to an unworthy smallness or to composition, negating divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously pictured Allah as sitting on a throne or being 'above' the heavens? After reading Razi's argument, are you willing to abandon that mental image and affirm that He is beyond all spatial categories, even if it feels abstract?
?
If you were to describe Allah to someone, would you be tempted to use spatial terms to make Him more 'relatable'? Does this lesson convict you that such attempts actually diminish His transcendence?
?
Razi says 'no rational person would be pleased to say the Creator of the universe is like an indivisible atom.' Are you rational enough to reject any description of Allah that implies smallness or composition, even if it comes from a respected tradition or your own imagination?

Razi's Refutation of the Karamiyya: Implicit Corporealism
Razi refutes the Karamiyya by showing their belief in divine dimensions implies composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi exposes the Karamiyya's implicit belief in divine composition despite their verbal denial.

Core Idea
Believing God occupies space inevitably implies He has parts.

In this passage, Imam Razi demonstrates that a sect's theological commitments can force them into a position they verbally reject. The Karamiyya sect denies calling God a 'body', but Razi shows their belief in God's spatial location and perceptibility logically entails that they do believe in divine composition. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow of Razi's argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify key premises
Identify the two premises: (1) The Karamiyya believe God is in a direction and perceptible by senses. (2) They believe God is not like an indivisible atom. From these, Razi infers that they must believe God is extended (long, wide, deep).
Extension in any direction implies spatial dimensions.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi argues that extension implies composition (tarkīb). Therefore, the Karamiyya must believe God is composite, even if they avoid the word. Their denial is mere dissimulation (taqiyya).
This reveals the inconsistency in their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ذلك لا يمنعه من اعتقاد كونه جسماً طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً. فثبت أن هؤلاء الكرامية لما اعتقدوا كونه تعالى مختصاً بالحيز والجهة ومشاراً إليه بحسب الحس، واعتقدوا أنه تعالى ليس في الصغر والحقارة مثل الجوهر الفرد والنقطة التي لا تتجزأ: وجب أن يكونوا قد اعتقدوا أنه تعالى ممتد في الجوانب، أو في بعض الجوانب. ومن قال ذلك فقد اعتقد كونه مركباً مؤلفاً، فكان امتناعه عن إطلاق لفظ المؤلف والمركب، امتناعاً عن مجرد هذا اللفظ، مع كونه معتقداً المعناه. فثبت: أنهم إنما أطلقوا عليه لفظ الجسم: لأجل أنهم اعتقدوا كونه تعالى طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً ممتداً في الجهات. فثبت: أن امتناعهم عن هذا الكلام المحض التقية والخوف، وإلا فهم يعتقدون كونه تعالى مركباً مؤلفاً. فهذا تمام الكلام في القسم الأول من هذا الكتاب) وهذا هو (٤)) القسم المشتمل على الوجوه العقلية، وبالله التوفيق.
For that does not prevent him from believing that He is a body long, wide, deep. So it is established that these Karamiyya, since they believed that He, exalted, is specific to space and direction and pointed to by sense perception, and they believed that He, exalted, is not in smallness and baseness like the indivisible atom and the point that is not divisible, it necessarily follows that they believed that He, exalted, is extended in the sides, or in some sides. And whoever said that has believed that He is composite and composed. So his abstention from uttering the term 'composed' and 'composite' is an abstention from the mere wording while he believes its meaning. So it is established that they only applied to Him the term 'body' because they believed that He, exalted, is long, wide, deep, extended in directions. So it is established that their abstention from this speech is pure dissimulation and fear; otherwise, they believe that He, exalted, is composite and composed. So this is the completion of the discourse in the first section of this book, and this is (4) the section containing the intellectual proofs. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karamiyya sect. Razi argues that their belief in God's occupation of space (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha) and that He is perceptible by the senses necessarily entails that He has dimensions (length, width, depth). Having dimensions implies composition (tarkīb) into parts. Therefore, even if the Karamiyya verbally deny using the terms 'composite' (mu'allaf) and 'body' (jism), they are committed to the meaning of those terms. Their refusal to utter them is mere dissimulation (taqiyya) and fear, not a genuine denial of corporeality. Razi concludes by noting this ends the first section of the book, which contains intellectual proofs.

الجسم
الحيز
الجهة
المركب

Correction: Razi shows that their theological commitments (spatial location, perceptibility, and not being like an atom) force them to believe in extension and composition, regardless of the words they use.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue is the necessary consequence of the Karamiyya's belief that God is in a direction and perceptible by senses, and that He is not like an indivisible atom?
A) They must believe God is composed of parts.
B) They must believe God is beyond space and time.
C) They must believe God is a simple indivisible entity.
D) They must believe God is nothing but an atom.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the Karamiyya's belief in God's spatiality forces them to affirm divine composition, despite their verbal avoidance of 'body'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the Karamiyya's verbal denial of 'body' is insincere because their underlying beliefs force them to accept the meaning. Is there any area of your own belief about Allah where you might be verbally affirming a correct term but your actual concept of Him still carries subtle, unexamined spatial or bodily assumptions? How will you purify that today?
?
This passage shows that even if a group avoids certain words, their logical commitments can make their position contradictory. In your own understanding of tawhid, are you fully consistent with what you say you believe, or are there hidden contradictions you've avoided facing? What specific step will you take to examine your beliefs against the clear texts and rational proofs?
?
When you stand before Allah in prayer, do you ever catch yourself imagining Him in a specific direction or location, even subconsciously? If so, what does this lesson demand you change about how you direct your heart and mind?
