The Necessity of Ta'wil: The Absurdity of Literalism about Allah's Attributes
Razi's first argument against literalism of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taking Qur'anic descriptions of Allah's face, eyes, side, and hands literally leads to an absurd and unworthy image, proving the necessity of ta'wil.

Core Idea
Literal readings of divine anthropomorphic terms lead to absurdity, proving ta'wil is essential.

Razi begins his case for ta'wil by presenting a vivid example: if we take the verses mentioning Allah's face, eyes, side, and hands at face value, we end up with a grotesque and irrational picture. This first argument sets the stage for all subsequent proofs, showing that even the most basic rational intuition rejects a literal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction and first argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi frames the discussion as a consensus among Islamic sects.
The introduction states that all Muslims agree ta'wil is needed.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
List the attributes mentioned (face, eyes, side, hands, leg) and see how combining them literally creates a bizarre being.
Razi says no rational person would accept this description of God.
3
Reflect on the necessity of ta'wil
Ask yourself: what alternative interpretations could avoid this absurdity? Razi implies that ta'wil is the only way.
This argument forces the reader to accept ta'wil for these verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة
في
بيان أن جميع فرق الإسلام مقرون بأنه لابد من التأويل في بعض ظواهر القرآن والأخبار
أما في القرآن فبيانه من وجوه:
الأول: هو أنه ورد في القرآن ذكر الوجه، وذكر العين، وذكر الجنب الواحد، وذكر الأيدي، وذكر الساق الواحدة، فلو أخذنا بالظاهر يلزمنا إثبات شخص له وجه واحد وعلى ذلك الوجه أعين كثيرة وله جنب واحد وعليه أيد كثيرة وله ساق واحدة ولا نرى في الدنيا شخصا أقبح صورة من هذه الصورة المتخيلة، ولا أعتقد أن عاقلا يرضى بأن يصف ربه بهذه الصفة.
الثاني: إنه ورد في القرآن أنه تعالى نور السموات والأرض، وأن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهية أن إله العالم ليس هو هذا الشيء المنبسط على الجدران والحيطان، وليس هو هذا النور الفائض من جرم الشمس والقمر والنار، فلا بد لكل واحد منا من أن يفسر قوله تعالى: «الله نور السموات والأرض» بأنه منور السموات والأرض أو بأنه هادي لأهل السموات والأرض، أو بأنه مصلح السموات والأرض، وكل ذلك تأويل.
الثالث: قال الله تعالى: «وأنزلنا الحديد فيه بأس شديد»، ومعلوم أن الحديد ما نزل جرمه من السماء إلى الأرض. وقال: «وأنزل لكم من الأنعام ثمانية أزواج»، ومعلوم أن الأنعام ما نزلت من السماء إلى الأرض.
الرابع: قوله تعالى: «وهو معكم أينما كنتم»، وقوله تعالى: «ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد»، وقوله تعالى: «وما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم»، وكل عاقل يعلم أن المراد منه القرب بالعلم والقدرة الإلهية.
الخامس: قوله تعالى: «واسجد واقترب»، فإن هذا القرب ليس إلا بالطاعة والعبودية، فأما القرب بالجهة فمعلوم بالضرورة أنه لا يحصل بسبب السجود.
السادس: قوله تعالى: «فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله»، وقال تعالى: «ونحن أقرب إليه منكم ولكن لا تبصرون».
السابع: قال تعالى: «من ذا الذي يقرض الله قرضا حسنا؟»، ولا شك أنه لا بد فيه من التأويل.
الثامن: قوله تعالى: «فأتى الله بنيانهم من القواعد»، ولا بد فيه من التأويل.
Introduction: On the statement that all Islamic sects affirm that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary for some apparent meanings (zawahir) of the Qur'an and reports. As for the Qur'an, its explanation is through aspects: First: It occurs in the Qur'an the mention of the face, the eyes, the one side, the hands, and the one leg. If we take the apparent, it would necessitate affirming a person having one face, and upon that face many eyes, and one side, and upon it many hands, and one leg. We do not see in the world a person more ugly in form than this imagined form, and I do not think a rational person would be pleased to describe his Lord with this description. Second: It occurs in the Qur'an that He, exalted, is the light of the heavens and the earth. Every rational person knows intuitively that the Lord of the world is not this thing spread upon walls and ceilings, and is not this light emanating from the body of the sun, moon, and fire. Therefore, each one of us must interpret His saying 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' as that He is the illuminator of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the guide for the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the reformer of the heavens and the earth. All of that is interpretation (ta'wil). Third: Allah said, 'And We sent down iron, in which is severe might.' It is known that iron, its body did not descend from the heaven to the earth. And He said, 'And We sent down for you of the cattle eight pairs.' It is known that cattle did not descend from the heaven to the earth. Fourth: His saying, 'And He is with you wherever you are,' and His saying, 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein,' and His saying, 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is their fourth.' Every rational person knows that what is meant by it is closeness in knowledge and divine power. Fifth: His saying, 'And prostrate and draw near.' This nearness is only through obedience and worship. As for physical closeness, it is known necessarily that it does not occur by prostration. Sixth: His saying, 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah,' and He said, 'And We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' Seventh: He said, 'Who is it that will lend Allah a goodly loan?' There is no doubt that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in it. Eighth: His saying, 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations,' and interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in it.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a list of eight arguments (wujuh) proving that interpretation (ta'wil) is unavoidable for certain Qur'anic verses. Each argument presents a verse that, if taken literally, leads to an absurd or impossible conclusion, thereby demonstrating the necessity of ta'wil.

1. The first argument cites verses mentioning Allah's face, eyes, side, hands, and leg. Taken literally, this would depict a being with one face, many eyes, one side, many hands, and one leg—an absurd and ugly image no rational person would attribute to God. Hence, ta'wil is required.

2. The second argument considers the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' Light as commonly understood is a physical phenomenon. Every rational person knows God is not physical light. Therefore, 'light' must be interpreted metaphorically, e.g., as the illuminator, guide, or reformer of the heavens and earth. All such interpretations are ta'wil.

3. The third argument notes verses that say 'We sent down iron' and 'We sent down cattle.' Literally, 'sent down' implies descent from heaven. But iron and cattle are not physically sent down; they originate on earth. Thus, 'sent down' must be interpreted (e.g., created or provided), proving ta'wil necessary.

4. The fourth argument presents verses about God's nearness ('He is with you,' 'nearer than the jugular vein,' 'He is the fourth of three'). Physical nearness in space is impossible for God. The intended meaning is closeness in knowledge and power, not spatial proximity. This requires ta'wil.

5. The fifth argument addresses the command 'prostrate and draw near.' If taken literally, prostration would cause physical nearness, which is impossible. Instead, it means spiritual nearness through obedience and worship. Thus, ta'wil is needed.

6. The sixth argument refers to 'Wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' and 'We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' The 'face' cannot be a physical face, and nearness cannot be spatial. These must be interpreted figuratively.

7. The seventh argument cites 'Who will lend Allah a goodly loan?' Lending implies borrowing, which is inappropriate for God. The verse is metaphorical, encouraging charity. Ta'wil is clearly needed.

8. The eighth argument cites 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations.' Literal 'coming' would imply movement and physical location for God, which is impossible. This requires ta'wil to understand it as divine punishment or intervention.

Through these examples, Razi demonstrates that all Islamic sects agree on the necessity of ta'wil for certain scriptural passages, as literalism would lead to absurdity or impossibility.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
ظواهر (zawahir)
وجه (wajh)
عين (ayn)
جنب (janb)
يد (yad)
ساق (saq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, what would be the consequence of taking verses mentioning Allah's face, eyes, side, hands, and leg literally?
A) It would confirm God's true form as described.
B) It would lead to an absurd and ugly depiction unworthy of God.
C) It would be the correct understanding without need for ta'wil.
D) It would show that these verses are not from the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The first argument proves that ta'wil is necessary to avoid attributing an absurd physical form to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear verses about Allah's face, hands, or eyes, do you ever catch yourself forming a mental image? How does Razi's argument expose the danger of that habit, and what must you actively do to correct it?
?
If you were to insist on a literal reading of these verses, what would your mental picture of Allah look like? Does that image match the majesty and perfection you believe in? What does that discrepancy reveal about your need for ta'wil?
?
How willing are you to accept that your own understanding of some Quranic verses might be flawed and require reinterpretation by scholars? What resistance do you feel, and is it rooted in piety or in attachment to a particular reading? Commit to one action you will take to deepen your trust in sound scholarly interpretation.

The Necessity of Ta'wil: Allah as Light
Razi's second argument on the metaphorical nature of light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' cannot be taken literally and must be interpreted metaphorically.

Core Idea
Allah is not physical light; the verse must be understood through ta'wil.

The second argument tackles the well-known verse describing Allah as light. Since no rational person would identify the Creator with physical light, Razi argues that ta'wil is inevitable. He offers several plausible interpretations, showing that the meaning must be figurative.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the verse and Razi's reasoning.
2
Understand why literalism fails
Recognize that physical light is a created entity and cannot be the Creator. Razi lists examples: light on walls, from sun, etc.
This is intuitively known (badihiyya).
3
Explore possible interpretations
Razi suggests 'illuminator', 'guide', or 'reformer'. These are all ta'wil.
The multiplicity of interpretations shows non-literal meaning.

Introduction: On the statement that all Islamic sects affirm that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary for some apparent meanings (zawahir) of the Qur'an and reports. As for the Qur'an, its explanation is through aspects: First: It occurs in the Qur'an the mention of the face, the eyes, the one side, the hands, and the one leg. Sixth: His saying, 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah,' and He said, 'And We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' Commentary
This passage is a list of eight arguments (wujuh) proving that interpretation (ta'wil) is unavoidable for certain Qur'anic verses. Each argument presents a verse that, if taken literally, leads to an absurd or impossible conclusion, thereby demonstrating the necessity of ta'wil.

1. The first argument cites verses mentioning Allah's face, eyes, side, hands, and leg. Taken literally, this would depict a being with one face, many eyes, one side, many hands, and one leg—an absurd and ugly image no rational person would attribute to God. Hence, ta'wil is required.

2. The second argument considers the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' Light as commonly understood is a physical phenomenon. Every rational person knows God is not physical light. Therefore, 'light' must be interpreted metaphorically, e.g., as the illuminator, guide, or reformer of the heavens and earth. All such interpretations are ta'wil.

3. The third argument notes verses that say 'We sent down iron' and 'We sent down cattle.' Literally, 'sent down' implies descent from heaven. But iron and cattle are not physically sent down; they originate on earth. Thus, 'sent down' must be interpreted (e.g., created or provided), proving ta'wil necessary.

4. The fourth argument presents verses about God's nearness ('He is with you,' 'nearer than the jugular vein,' 'He is the fourth of three'). Physical nearness in space is impossible for God. The intended meaning is closeness in knowledge and power, not spatial proximity. This requires ta'wil.

5. The fifth argument addresses the command 'prostrate and draw near.' If taken literally, prostration would cause physical nearness, which is impossible. Instead, it means spiritual nearness through obedience and worship. Thus, ta'wil is needed.

6. The sixth argument refers to 'Wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' and 'We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' The 'face' cannot be a physical face, and nearness cannot be spatial. These must be interpreted figuratively.

7. The seventh argument cites 'Who will lend Allah a goodly loan?' Lending implies borrowing, which is inappropriate for God. The verse is metaphorical, encouraging charity. Ta'wil is clearly needed.

8. The eighth argument cites 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations.' Literal 'coming' would imply movement and physical location for God, which is impossible. This requires ta'wil to understand it as divine punishment or intervention.

Through these examples, Razi demonstrates that all Islamic sects agree on the necessity of ta'wil for certain scriptural passages, as literalism would lead to absurdity or impossibility.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
نور (nur)
بديهية (badihiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what do rational people intuitively know about the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth'?
A) It means Allah is a physical light.
B) It must be interpreted metaphorically because God is not physical light.
C) It refers to the light of the sun and moon.
D) It requires no interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The verse about Allah as light must be interpreted figuratively, as literalism contradicts intuitive reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse of the Qur'an and felt that its literal meaning could not possibly apply to Allah? Did you dismiss it or did you seek a deeper understanding? What specific step will you take today to learn proper ta'wil?
?
Razi uses intuitive reason (badihiyya) to reject a literal interpretation. Are you using the intellect Allah gave you when you read the Qur'an, or do you rely solely on what you've been told? Challenge yourself to think deeply about a verse you've glossed over.
?
If you had to choose one of Razi's interpretations—illuminator, guide, or reformer—to describe how Allah's light manifests in your own life, which would it be? And how will that interpretation change your actions before the day ends?

The Necessity of Ta'wil: 'Sent Down' Iron and Cattle
Razi's third argument against literal 'sending down'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic phrase 'sent down' used for iron and cattle cannot be literal and requires ta'wil.

Core Idea
Not everything 'sent down' literally descends from the sky; ta'wil clarifies the intended meaning.

Razi's third argument points to verses where 'sent down' (anzala) is used for iron and cattle. Since these items are not physically sent from heaven, the term must be interpreted metaphorically — as 'created' or 'provided' — demonstrating again the need for ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two examples: iron and cattle.
2
Analyze the literal impossibility
Iron and cattle are known to originate on earth, not descend from the sky. Literalism contradicts common knowledge.
3
Conclude the need for ta'wil
Since literal 'sending down' is false, the word must be interpreted figuratively, e.g., 'created' or 'provided'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة
في
بيان أن جميع فرق الإسلام مقرون بأنه لابد من التأويل في بعض ظواهر القرآن والأخبار
أما في القرآن فبيانه من وجوه:
الأول: هو أنه ورد في القرآن ذكر الوجه، وذكر العين، وذكر الجنب الواحد، وذكر الأيدي، وذكر الساق الواحدة، فلو أخذنا بالظاهر يلزمنا إثبات شخص له وجه واحد وعلى ذلك الوجه أعين كثيرة وله جنب واحد وعليه أيد كثيرة وله ساق واحدة ولا نرى في الدنيا شخصا أقبح صورة من هذه الصورة المتخيلة، ولا أعتقد أن عاقلا يرضى بأن يصف ربه بهذه الصفة.
الثاني: إنه ورد في القرآن أنه تعالى نور السموات والأرض، وأن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهية أن إله العالم ليس هو هذا الشيء المنبسط على الجدران والحيطان، وليس هو هذا النور الفائض من جرم الشمس والقمر والنار، فلا بد لكل واحد منا من أن يفسر قوله تعالى: «الله نور السموات والأرض» بأنه منور السموات والأرض أو بأنه هادي لأهل السموات والأرض، أو بأنه مصلح السموات والأرض، وكل ذلك تأويل.
الثالث: قال الله تعالى: «وأنزلنا الحديد فيه بأس شديد»، ومعلوم أن الحديد ما نزل جرمه من السماء إلى الأرض. وقال: «وأنزل لكم من الأنعام ثمانية أزواج»، ومعلوم أن الأنعام ما نزلت من السماء إلى الأرض.
الرابع: قوله تعالى: «وهو معكم أينما كنتم»، وقوله تعالى: «ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد»، وقوله تعالى: «وما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم»، وكل عاقل يعلم أن المراد منه القرب بالعلم والقدرة الإلهية.
الخامس: قوله تعالى: «واسجد واقترب»، فإن هذا القرب ليس إلا بالطاعة والعبودية، فأما القرب بالجهة فمعلوم بالضرورة أنه لا يحصل بسبب السجود.
السادس: قوله تعالى: «فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله»، وقال تعالى: «ونحن أقرب إليه منكم ولكن لا تبصرون».
السابع: قال تعالى: «من ذا الذي يقرض الله قرضا حسنا؟»، ولا شك أنه لا بد فيه من التأويل.
الثامن: قوله تعالى: «فأتى الله بنيانهم من القواعد»، ولا بد فيه من التأويل.
Introduction: On the statement that all Islamic sects affirm that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary for some apparent meanings (zawahir) of the Qur'an and reports. As for the Qur'an, its explanation is through aspects: First: It occurs in the Qur'an the mention of the face, the eyes, the one side, the hands, and the one leg. If we take the apparent, it would necessitate affirming a person having one face, and upon that face many eyes, and one side, and upon it many hands, and one leg. We do not see in the world a person more ugly in form than this imagined form, and I do not think a rational person would be pleased to describe his Lord with this description. Second: It occurs in the Qur'an that He, exalted, is the light of the heavens and the earth. Every rational person knows intuitively that the Lord of the world is not this thing spread upon walls and ceilings, and is not this light emanating from the body of the sun, moon, and fire. Therefore, each one of us must interpret His saying 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' as that He is the illuminator of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the guide for the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the reformer of the heavens and the earth. All of that is interpretation (ta'wil). Third: Allah said, 'And We sent down iron, in which is severe might.' It is known that iron, its body did not descend from the heaven to the earth. And He said, 'And We sent down for you of the cattle eight pairs.' It is known that cattle did not descend from the heaven to the earth. Fourth: His saying, 'And He is with you wherever you are,' and His saying, 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein,' and His saying, 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is their fourth.' Every rational person knows that what is meant by it is closeness in knowledge and divine power. Fifth: His saying, 'And prostrate and draw near.' This nearness is only through obedience and worship. As for physical closeness, it is known necessarily that it does not occur by prostration. Sixth: His saying, 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah,' and He said, 'And We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' Seventh: He said, 'Who is it that will lend Allah a goodly loan?' There is no doubt that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in it. Eighth: His saying, 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations,' and interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in it.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a list of eight arguments (wujuh) proving that interpretation (ta'wil) is unavoidable for certain Qur'anic verses. Each argument presents a verse that, if taken literally, leads to an absurd or impossible conclusion, thereby demonstrating the necessity of ta'wil.

1. The first argument cites verses mentioning Allah's face, eyes, side, hands, and leg. Taken literally, this would depict a being with one face, many eyes, one side, many hands, and one leg—an absurd and ugly image no rational person would attribute to God. Hence, ta'wil is required.

2. The second argument considers the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' Light as commonly understood is a physical phenomenon. Every rational person knows God is not physical light. Therefore, 'light' must be interpreted metaphorically, e.g., as the illuminator, guide, or reformer of the heavens and earth. All such interpretations are ta'wil.

3. The third argument notes verses that say 'We sent down iron' and 'We sent down cattle.' Literally, 'sent down' implies descent from heaven. But iron and cattle are not physically sent down; they originate on earth. Thus, 'sent down' must be interpreted (e.g., created or provided), proving ta'wil necessary.

4. The fourth argument presents verses about God's nearness ('He is with you,' 'nearer than the jugular vein,' 'He is the fourth of three'). Physical nearness in space is impossible for God. The intended meaning is closeness in knowledge and power, not spatial proximity. This requires ta'wil.

5. The fifth argument addresses the command 'prostrate and draw near.' If taken literally, prostration would cause physical nearness, which is impossible. Instead, it means spiritual nearness through obedience and worship. Thus, ta'wil is needed.

6. The sixth argument refers to 'Wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' and 'We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' The 'face' cannot be a physical face, and nearness cannot be spatial. These must be interpreted figuratively.

7. The seventh argument cites 'Who will lend Allah a goodly loan?' Lending implies borrowing, which is inappropriate for God. The verse is metaphorical, encouraging charity. Ta'wil is clearly needed.

8. The eighth argument cites 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations.' Literal 'coming' would imply movement and physical location for God, which is impossible. This requires ta'wil to understand it as divine punishment or intervention.

Through these examples, Razi demonstrates that all Islamic sects agree on the necessity of ta'wil for certain scriptural passages, as literalism would lead to absurdity or impossibility.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
إنزال (inzal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the verse 'We sent down iron' cannot be taken literally?
A) Because iron is heavy and cannot fall.
B) Because iron is known not to have descended from the sky.
C) Because iron is not mentioned in the Qur'an.
D) Because the word 'anzala' only means 'created'.

Key Takeaway
The word 'sent down' in the Qur'an sometimes requires ta'wil to avoid contradiction with reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read a verse like 'We sent down iron,' do you feel any discomfort because of what science tells you? How does this lesson change the way you reconcile faith and reason?
?
Razi uses undeniable facts to argue for ta'wil. Are you willing to let your intellect and observation guide your understanding of the Quran, even when it challenges a literal reading? What might that cost you?
?

The Necessity of Ta'wil: Divine Nearness and Prostration
Razi's fourth and fifth arguments on literal vs spiritual nearness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verses about God's nearness and the command to 'draw near' through prostration cannot be literal and must be interpreted spiritually.

Core Idea
God's nearness is not spatial but relational; ta'wil transforms absurd literalism into meaningful doctrine.

The fourth and fifth arguments deal with verses that speak of God's nearness — 'He is with you,' 'nearer than the jugular vein,' 'He is the fourth of three,' and the command 'prostrate and draw near.' Razi shows that literal spatial nearness is impossible for God, so these verses must be interpreted as closeness in knowledge, power, or obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth and fifth arguments
Read the combined Arabic and translation. Note the multiple verses cited.
2
Identify the literal absurdity
If nearness is spatial, God would be physically located, which is impossible. Similarly, prostration cannot cause physical proximity.
3
Understand the correct interpretation
Razi clarifies that nearness means knowledge and power (fourth) and that drawing near through prostration is spiritual (fifth).

The Necessity of Ta'wil: Face, Loan, and Divine Intervention
Razi's final three arguments for ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verses about the 'face of Allah,' 'lending to Allah,' and 'Allah came to their buildings' must be interpreted metaphorically.

Core Idea
Even seemingly straightforward verses about divine actions and attributes require ta'wil to avoid absurd literalism.

Razi concludes his series with three more arguments: the verse 'wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah,' 'who will lend Allah a goodly loan,' and 'Allah came to their buildings from the foundations.' Each presents a clear case for ta'wil, as literal readings would imply physicality or borrowing, which are inappropriate for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth, seventh, and eighth arguments
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the variety of verses: face, loan, and coming.
2
Analyze each argument
For the sixth: 'face' cannot be physical. Seventh: lending implies need, which God does not have. Eighth: 'coming' implies movement and location.
3
Synthesize the overall lesson
All eight arguments collectively show that ta'wil is unavoidable for a rational understanding of the Qur'an.

الرابع: قوله تعالى: «وهو معكم أينما كنتم»، وقوله تعالى: «ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد»، وقوله تعالى: «وما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم»، وكل عاقل يعلم أن المراد منه القرب بالعلم والقدرة الإلهية.
الخامس: قوله تعالى: «واسجد واقترب»، فإن هذا القرب ليس إلا بالطاعة والعبودية، فأما القرب بالجهة فمعلوم بالضرورة أنه لا يحصل بسبب السجود.
السادس: قوله تعالى: «فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله»، وقال تعالى: «ونحن أقرب إليه منكم ولكن لا تبصرون».
السابع: قال تعالى: «من ذا الذي يقرض الله قرضا حسنا؟»، ولا شك أنه لا بد فيه من التأويل.
الثامن: قوله تعالى: «فأتى الله بنيانهم من القواعد»، ولا بد فيه من التأويل.
Introduction: On the statement that all Islamic sects affirm that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary for some apparent meanings (zawahir) of the Qur'an and reports. As for the Qur'an, its explanation is through aspects: First: It occurs in the Qur'an the mention of the face, the eyes, the one side, the hands, and the one leg. If we take the apparent, it would necessitate affirming a person having one face, and upon that face many eyes, and one side, and upon it many hands, and one leg. We do not see in the world a person more ugly in form than this imagined form, and I do not think a rational person would be pleased to describe his Lord with this description. Second: It occurs in the Qur'an that He, exalted, is the light of the heavens and the earth. Every rational person knows intuitively that the Lord of the world is not this thing spread upon walls and ceilings, and is not this light emanating from the body of the sun, moon, and fire. Therefore, each one of us must interpret His saying 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' as that He is the illuminator of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the guide for the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the reformer of the heavens and the earth. All of that is interpretation (ta'wil). Third: Allah said, 'And We sent down iron, in which is severe might.' It is known that iron, its body did not descend from the heaven to the earth. And He said, 'And We sent down for you of the cattle eight pairs.' It is known that cattle did not descend from the heaven to the earth. Fourth: His saying, 'And He is with you wherever you are,' and His saying, 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein,' and His saying, 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is their fourth.' Every rational person knows that what is meant by it is closeness in knowledge and divine power. Fifth: His saying, 'And prostrate and draw near.' This nearness is only through obedience and worship. As for physical closeness, it is known necessarily that it does not occur by prostration. Sixth: His saying, 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah,' and He said, 'And We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' Seventh: He said, 'Who is it that will lend Allah a goodly loan?' There is no doubt that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in it. Eighth: His saying, 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations,' and interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in it.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a list of eight arguments (wujuh) proving that interpretation (ta'wil) is unavoidable for certain Qur'anic verses. Each argument presents a verse that, if taken literally, leads to an absurd or impossible conclusion, thereby demonstrating the necessity of ta'wil.

1. The first argument cites verses mentioning Allah's face, eyes, side, hands, and leg. Taken literally, this would depict a being with one face, many eyes, one side, many hands, and one leg—an absurd and ugly image no rational person would attribute to God. Hence, ta'wil is required.

2. The second argument considers the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' Light as commonly understood is a physical phenomenon. Every rational person knows God is not physical light. Therefore, 'light' must be interpreted metaphorically, e.g., as the illuminator, guide, or reformer of the heavens and earth. All such interpretations are ta'wil.

3. The third argument notes verses that say 'We sent down iron' and 'We sent down cattle.' Literally, 'sent down' implies descent from heaven. But iron and cattle are not physically sent down; they originate on earth. Thus, 'sent down' must be interpreted (e.g., created or provided), proving ta'wil necessary.

4. The fourth argument presents verses about God's nearness ('He is with you,' 'nearer than the jugular vein,' 'He is the fourth of three'). Physical nearness in space is impossible for God. The intended meaning is closeness in knowledge and power, not spatial proximity. This requires ta'wil.

5. The fifth argument addresses the command 'prostrate and draw near.' If taken literally, prostration would cause physical nearness, which is impossible. Instead, it means spiritual nearness through obedience and worship. Thus, ta'wil is needed.

6. The sixth argument refers to 'Wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' and 'We are nearer to him than you, but you do not perceive.' The 'face' cannot be a physical face, and nearness cannot be spatial. These must be interpreted figuratively.

7. The seventh argument cites 'Who will lend Allah a goodly loan?' Lending implies borrowing, which is inappropriate for God. The verse is metaphorical, encouraging charity. Ta'wil is clearly needed.

8. The eighth argument cites 'So Allah came to their buildings from the foundations.' Literal 'coming' would imply movement and physical location for God, which is impossible. This requires ta'wil to understand it as divine punishment or intervention.

Through these examples, Razi demonstrates that all Islamic sects agree on the necessity of ta'wil for certain scriptural passages, as literalism would lead to absurdity or impossibility.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
وجه (wajh)
قرض (qard)
إتيان (ityan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Razi's ruling on the verse 'Who is it that will lend Allah a goodly loan?' regarding ta'wil?
A) It should be taken literally.
B) There is no doubt that ta'wil is necessary.
C) It applies only to certain interpretations.
D) It does not require ta'wil.

Key Takeaway
These remaining arguments confirm that ta'wil is essential for a wide range of Qur'anic expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله' (so wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah), does your mind immediately picture a direction or a face? If so, what does that reveal about your hidden assumptions regarding Allah? How can you train yourself to immediately think of Allah's transcendental presence rather than a physical entity?
?
The verse about lending to Allah (القرض) is clearly a metaphor, yet many people subconsciously treat their worship as a transaction: 'I do this, so Allah owes me that.' In what areas of your life have you viewed your relationship with Allah as a loan instead of humble servitude? What practical step will you take today to break that transactional mindset and replace it with pure devotion?
?
Razi argues that 'فأتى الله بنيانهم من القواعد' (Allah came to their buildings) requires ta'wil because it cannot be literal movement. When you hear sermons or read stories about divine intervention, do you ever mentally picture Allah 'coming down' or 'acting' in a human-like way? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your mental image of Allah's actions, and what specific discipline will you adopt to guard against imagining Allah in human terms?

Hadith Examples Requiring Ta'wil
Imam Razi gives hadiths that must be interpreted metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain hadiths require ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation) and how even traditionalist scholars like Ahmad ibn Hanbal accepted this method.

Core Idea
Even the most literalist scholars accepted ta'wil for some hadiths.

Imam Razi now turns from Qur'anic verses to hadiths. He presents several examples where the literal meaning would imply anthropomorphism, so they must be interpreted metaphorically. He even cites Ahmad ibn Hanbal, a pillar of traditionalism, to show that ta'wil is not a rationalist innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the three examples given.
2
Understand the first two examples
These are hadith qudsi where God speaks of being ill, hungry, and coming quickly. Why can't they be literal? Reflect on divine transcendence.
The author states that no rational person doubts these are metaphorical.
3
Analyze the report from Ahmad ibn Hanbal
Al-Ghazali reports that Ahmad ibn Hanbal affirmed ta'wil for three hadiths: the Black Stone as God's right hand, the breath of the Most Merciful from Yemen, and God as the companion of those who remember Him.
This shows ta'wil was accepted by a leading traditionalist.
4
Reflect on the significance
What does this tell us about the acceptance of ta'wil among early scholars? How does it counter the claim that ta'wil is a rationalist innovation?

أما الأخبار : فهذا النوع فيه كثرة . فالأول : قوله - عليه السلام - حكاية عن الله سبحانه وتعالى: مرضت فلم تعدنى ، استطعمتك فيما أطعمتنى ، استسقيتك فما أسقيتني .. ولا يشك عاقل : أن المراد منه : التمثيل فقط . الثاني : قوله ( حكاية عن ربه ) ( من أتاني يمشى ، أتيته هرولة ، ولا يشك عاقل فى أن المراد منه : التمثيل والتصوير . الثالث : نقل الشيخ الغزالي رحمه الله عن أحمد بن حنبل رحمه الله - أنه أقر بالتأويل في ثلاثة أحاديث : أحدهما : قوله عليه السلام : « الحجر الأسود : يمين الله في الأرض. وثانيها : قوله عليه السلام : . إني لأجد نفس الرحمن من قبل اليمن وثالثها : قوله عليه السلام (حكاية عن الله عز وجل : أنا جليس من ذكرني .
As for the hadiths, this type is abundant. The first: His saying (peace be upon him) narrating from Allah, 'I fell ill and you did not visit Me; I asked you for food and you did not feed Me; I asked you for drink and you did not give Me to drink...' No rational person doubts that what is meant is mere representation. The second: His saying (narrating from his Lord), 'Whoever comes to Me walking, I come to him running.' No rational person doubts that what is meant is representation and imagery. The third: Al-Ghazali (may Allah have mercy on him) reported from Ahmad ibn Hanbal (may Allah have mercy on him) that he affirmed ta'wil in three hadiths: One of them is his saying (peace be upon him): 'The Black Stone is the right hand of Allah on earth.' The second: his saying: 'I find the breath of the Most Merciful from the direction of Yemen.' And the third: his saying (narrating from Allah): 'I am the companion of whoever remembers Me.'

Commentary
This passage shifts from Qur'anic verses to hadiths. The author first notes that hadiths requiring ta'wil are numerous. He then gives two explicit examples. The first is a divine saying (hadith qudsi) where God speaks about being ill, hungry, and thirsty—clearly metaphorical because God is transcendent. The second is another divine saying about God coming quickly to the one who approaches Him slowly; again, this cannot be literal. Then he cites al-Ghazali reporting from Ahmad ibn Hanbal that even the staunch traditionalist Ahmad acknowledged ta'wil in three hadiths: (1) the Black Stone being called 'the right hand of Allah,' (2) 'the breath of the Most Merciful' coming from Yemen, and (3) 'I am the companion of whoever remembers Me.' By citing Ahmad ibn Hanbal, the author demonstrates that ta'wil is not a rationalist innovation but accepted by leading hadith scholars.

The Ninth Example: The Verse of Ma'iyya and Its Ta'wil
Imam Razi presents the ninth verse example requiring ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic expression 'I am with you' must be interpreted figuratively, not literally.

Core Idea
Allah's 'with-ness' means His preservation, knowledge, and mercy, not spatial location.

Imam Razi continues his series of Qur'anic verses that cannot be taken literally. Here he examines the verse where Allah tells Prophet Moses and Aaron, 'Indeed, I am with you, I hear and I see.' At first glance, this might suggest Allah is present in a physical sense, but that contradicts divine transcendence. Razi explains the true meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the key term
Focus on the word 'ma'iyya' and Razi's definition of it as preservation, knowledge, and mercy.
3
Understand the necessity of ta'wil
Reflect on why a rational person must interpret such verses figuratively.
4
Connect to broader methodology
This verse is an example of a general rule: any verse implying corporeality requires ta'wil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع: قال تعالى لموسى وهارون: «إني معكما أسمع وأرى». هذه المعية ليست إلا بالحفظ والعلم والرحمة. فهذه وأمثالها من الأمور التي لا بد لكل عاقل الاعتراف بحملها على التأويل. وبالله التوفيق.
The ninth: He, exalted, said to Moses and Aaron: 'Indeed, I am with you, I hear and I see.' This 'with-ness' is nothing but preservation, knowledge, and mercy. So these and their likes are among the matters that every rational person must acknowledge are to be interpreted figuratively. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth example from the Quran requiring ta'wil. The verse is from Surah Taha (20:46) where Allah says to Moses and Aaron, 'Indeed, I am with you, I hear and I see.' Imam Razi argues that the word 'معية' (with-ness) here cannot be taken literally as spatial accompaniment, because that would attribute to Allah physical location, which is impossible. Instead, it must be interpreted figuratively (ta'wil) to mean Allah's preservation ('حفظ'), knowledge ('علم'), and mercy ('رحمة') towards them. He then generalizes: all such expressions that imply corporeality must be understood figuratively by every rational person. The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms

الحفظ
العلم
الرحمة
التأويل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah being 'with' someone means He is physically present in that location.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that 'ma'iyya' here means preservation, knowledge, and mercy, not spatial accompaniment.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic expression 'I am with you' must be understood as Allah's preservation, knowledge, and mercy, not as literal presence.

Nine Hadiths Requiring Ta'wil
Imam Razi lists nine hadiths requiring ta'wil, each with a brief explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi demonstrates that many hadiths, like Quranic verses, necessitate ta'wil for divine transcendence, and that even traditionalist scholars like Ahmad ibn Hanbal employed it.

Core Idea
Ta'wil is not just for the Quran; even traditionalists use it for hadiths to uphold God's transcendence.

Imam Razi has already established the necessity of ta'wil for Quranic verses. He now turns to the hadith literature, showing that such traditions are abundant. In this passage, he lists nine specific hadiths and briefly explains why each one cannot be taken literally, citing even the endorsement of Ahmad ibn Hanbal for some.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage overview
Scan the list of nine hadiths. Notice that each is followed by a brief reason why literal reading is impossible.
The passage is a numbered enumeration.
2
Examine the first two hadiths
The hadiths 'I was sick' and 'I come running' are clear representations (tamthil). Understand why no rational person takes them literally.
3
Study the three hadiths from Ahmad ibn Hanbal
Al-Ghazali reports that Ibn Hanbal, a model of traditionalism, accepted ta'wil for the Black Stone, the breath of the Merciful, and God as companion.
This shows ta'wil is not a marginal tool.
4
Analyze the remaining hadiths (4–9)
Each hadith presents a specific problem: surahs like clouds, womb attachment, mosque contraction, heart between fingers, God's 'with-ness', and the cloak of pride. Note the rational arguments against literalism.
The heart-between-fingers hadith is especially clear: we know our chests lack literal fingers.
5
Reflect on the overall message
Ta'wil is a necessary method for preserving tanzih (transcendence) when scriptural texts contain anthropomorphic expressions.
Razi uses rational necessity ('ilm daruri) as the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأخبار: فهذا النوع فيه كثرة. فالأول: قوله عليه السلام حكاية عن الله سبحانه وتعالى: مرضت فلم تعدني، استطعمتك فلم تطعمني، استسقيتك فلم تسقني. ولا يشك عاقل أن المراد منه: التمثيل فقط. الثاني: قوله عليه السلام حكاية عن ربه: من أتاني يمشي أتيته هرولة. ولا يشك عاقل في أن المراد منه: التمثيل والتصوير. الثالث: نقل الشيخ الغزالي رحمه الله عن أحمد بن حنبل رحمه الله أنه أقر بالتأويل في ثلاثة أحاديث: أحدها: قوله عليه السلام: الحجر الأسود يمين الله في الأرض. وثانيها: قوله عليه السلام: إني لأجد نفس الرحمن من قبل اليمن. وثالثها: قوله عليه السلام حكاية عن الله عز وجل: أنا جليس من ذكرني. الرابع: حكي أن المعتزلة تمسكوا في خلق القرآن بما روي عنه عليه السلام: أنه تأتي سورة البقرة وآل عمران كذا وكذا يوم القيامة كأنهما غمامتان. فأجاب أحمد بن حنبل رحمه الله وقال: يعني ثواب قارئهما، وهذا تصريح منه بالتأويل. الخامس: قوله عليه السلام: إن الرحم يتعلق بحقوقي الرحمن، فيقول سبحانه وتعالى: أصل من وصلك، وهذا لابد له من التأويل. السادس: قال عليه السلام: إن المسجد لينزوي من النخامة كما تنزوي الجلدة من النار، ولا بد فيه من التأويل. السابع: قال عليه السلام: قلب المؤمن بين إصبعين من أصابع الرحمن، وهذا لا بد فيه من التأويل، لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أنه ليس في صدورنا إصبعان بينهما قلوبنا. الثامن: قوله عليه السلام حكاية عن الله تعالى: أنا عند المنكسرة قلوبهم، وليست هذه العندية إلا بالرحمة. وأيضا: قال حكاية عن الله تعالى في صفة الأولياء: فإذا أحببته كنت سمعه الذي يسمع به وبصره الذي يبصر به، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن القوة الباصرة التي بها يرى الأشياء ليست هي الله سبحانه وتعالى. التاسع: قال عليه السلام حكاية عن الله سبحانه وتعالى: الكبرياء ردائي والعظمة إزاري، والعاقل لا يثبت الله تعالى إزارا ورداء.
As for the reports (hadiths): this type is abundant. The first: His saying, peace be upon him, narrating from God glorified and exalted: 'I was sick and you did not visit Me; I asked you for food and you did not feed Me; I asked you for drink and you did not give Me to drink.' No rational person doubts that what is meant is representation only. The second: His saying, peace be upon him, narrating from his Lord: 'Whoever comes to Me walking, I come to him running.' No rational person doubts that what is meant is representation and depiction. The third: Al-Ghazali narrated from Ahmad ibn Hanbal that he affirmed ta'wil in three hadiths: one: his saying: 'The Black Stone is the right hand of God on earth'; second: his saying: 'I find the breath of the Merciful from the direction of Yemen'; third: his saying narrating from God: 'I am the companion of whoever remembers Me.' The fourth: It is narrated that the Mu'tazila argued for the creation of the Qur'an by what is transmitted from him: 'Surah al-Baqarah and Al Imran will come on the Day of Resurrection like two clouds.' Ahmad ibn Hanbal answered and said: 'He means the reward of their reciters,' and this is a clear statement from him of ta'wil. The fifth: His saying: 'The womb attaches to the rights of the Merciful, and He says: I join whoever joins you.' This necessarily requires ta'wil. The sixth: He said: 'The mosque contracts from sputum just as skin contracts from fire,' and it necessarily requires ta'wil. The seventh: He said: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Merciful,' and this necessarily requires ta'wil, because we know necessarily that there are not in our chests two fingers between which our hearts are. The eighth: His saying narrating from God: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken,' and this 'with-ness' is only by mercy. Also: He said narrating from God describing the saints: 'When I love him, I become his hearing by which he hears and his sight by which he sees,' and it is known necessarily that the power of sight by which things are seen is not God Himself. The ninth: He said narrating from God: 'Pride is My cloak and greatness My garment,' and a rational person does not affirm for God a garment and a cloak.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of nine hadiths (prophetic traditions) that Imam Razi presents as evidence for the necessity of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) when dealing with anthropomorphic expressions. The author has already argued for ta'wil regarding Quranic verses, and now turns to the hadith literature, claiming that such traditions are abundant. He then lists nine examples, each followed by a brief explanation showing why a literal reading is impossible and why ta'wil is required.

First hadith: The famous sacred hadith about God saying 'I was sick...' — this cannot be taken literally because God does not get sick; it is a representation (تمثيل) to encourage care for the needy.

Second: 'Whoever comes to Me walking, I come to him running' — again, God does not move; it is depiction (تصوير) of divine response.

Third: Imam al-Ghazali reports that Ahmad ibn Hanbal, the great traditionalist, accepted ta'wil for three hadiths: the Black Stone as God's hand, the 'breath of the Merciful' from Yemen, and God being the companion of the one who remembers Him. This is significant because Ibn Hanbal is often associated with literalism.

Fourth: The Mu'tazila used the hadith about Surahs coming like clouds to argue for the creation of the Qur'an (implying that the Qur'an is a created thing like the clouds). Ibn Hanbal responded that 'means the reward of their reciters,' thus interpreting the hadith figuratively.

Fifth: The hadith about the womb attaching to God's rights implies God speaking, but since God does not have body parts or literal attachment, it must be interpreted.

Sixth: The mosque contracting from sputum is a metaphor; a mosque is inanimate.

Seventh: The heart between two fingers — we know our chests do not contain literal fingers, so it must be a metaphor for God's power.

Eighth: 'I am with the broken-hearted' — 'with-ness' is mercy, not physical accompaniment. Also the hadith about God becoming the hearing and sight of the saint — that cannot mean literal identity; the power of sight is a created attribute, not God Himself.

Ninth: 'Pride is My cloak' — God does not wear clothes; it is a metaphor for divine attributes.

Throughout, Imam Razi emphasizes that rational necessity (علم ضروري) forces these interpretations.

Key Terms
التأويل
التمثيل
التصوير
الحجر الأسود
نفس الرحمن
الأصابع
يمين الله
عندية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only the Mu'tazila or rationalists use ta'wil; traditionalists reject it entirely.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that even Ahmad ibn Hanbal, a pillar of traditionalism, accepted ta'wil for specific hadiths, demonstrating its widespread necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Ahmad ibn Hanbal say about the hadith that Surah al-Baqarah and Al Imran will come like clouds?
A) He accepted it literally
B) He said it means the reward of their reciters
C) He rejected the hadith as weak
D) He said the clouds are the actual Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that ta'wil is an essential method for understanding anthropomorphic hadiths, endorsed even by traditionist scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Imam Razi shows that even Ahmad ibn Hanbal used ta'wil. Is there a hadith you have been reluctant to interpret figuratively because you thought literalism was more pious? What will you do to overcome that reluctance and embrace the method of the scholars?
?
The nine hadiths here challenge you to examine your own heart: Do you truly believe in God's absolute transcendence, or do you sometimes let your imagination limit Him? What concrete step will you take today to deepen your understanding of tanzih?

Ninth Example: Pride and Majesty as Garments
Imam Razi quotes a divine saying and states it requires ta'wil for rational people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'Pride is My cloak, greatness is My garment' requires ta'wil to avoid anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Attributing garments to Allah is impossible; the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically.

Imam Razi presents the ninth of his hadith examples that illustrate the necessity of allegorical interpretation. This divine saying ascribes 'cloak' and 'garment' to Allah. A rational person immediately sees that such words cannot be taken literally, for Allah does not have a body. This lesson explains the rational argument for ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith
Read the Arabic text of the ninth example. Notice the phrase 'الكبرياء ردائي والعظمة إزاري'. This is a hadith Qudsi.
The hadith is well-known.
2
Understand the author's point
Imam Razi says 'والعاقل لا يثبت الله تعالى إزارا ورداء' - a rational person does not affirm that Allah has a garment. Why? Because garments are physical and limited.
This is the key argument.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on how this hadith, if taken literally, would imply a body for Allah. Ta'wil saves the transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : قال عليه السلام - حكاية حكاية عن الله سبحانه وتعالى الكبرياء ردائي ، والعظمة إزارى ، والعاقل لا يثبت الله تعالى إزارا ورداء . العاشر : قال عليه السلام لأبي بن كعب : « يا أبا المنذر . أية آية في كتاب الله تعالى أعظم ؟ فتردد فيه مرتين . ثم قال في الثالثة : آية الكرسي . فضرب يده - عليه السلام - على صدره ، وقال : « أصبت . والذي نفسي بيده . إن لها لسانا يقدس الله تعالى عند العرش ، ولا بد فيه من التأويل .
The ninth: He said, peace be upon him—narrating from Allah glorified and exalted: 'Pride is My cloak, and greatness is My garment,' and a rational person does not affirm that Allah possesses a garment and a cloak. The tenth: He said, peace be upon him to Ubayy bin Ka'b: 'O Abu al-Mundhir, which verse in the Book of Allah is greatest?' He hesitated twice. Then he said the third time: 'Ayat al-Kursi.' He struck his chest, peace be upon him, and said: 'You are correct. By the One in whose hand is my soul, it has a tongue that glorifies Allah at the Throne,' and ta'wil is necessary in it.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage provides the ninth and tenth examples in the series of hadiths requiring ta'wil. The ninth hadith is a divine saying: 'Pride is My cloak, and greatness is My garment.' The author comments that no rational person would affirm that Allah literally has a garment (izar) and cloak (rida'). Therefore, ta'wil is necessary—these attributes are to be understood metaphorically to mean that pride and greatness are exclusively Allah's, not that He wears clothing. The tenth hadith is longer: the Prophet asked Ubayy bin Ka'b about the greatest verse in the Quran. Ubayy eventually said Ayat al-Kursi. The Prophet affirmed and said it has a tongue that glorifies Allah at the Throne. The attribution of a literal tongue to the verse or to a non-corporeal entity requires ta'wil; it cannot be taken literally because the Quran is not a physical being. Thus both examples underscore the need for allegorical interpretation to safeguard divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الكبرياء
العظمة
الإزار
الرداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because Allah is described with 'cloak' and 'garment', He has a body or wears clothing.
Correction: The author explicitly states that a rational person does not affirm that Allah has garments, as that would be anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint

A) The hadith is weak
B) A rational person does not affirm that Allah has a garment and cloak
C) The Prophet was speaking figuratively
D) The hadith is a parable

Key Takeaway
The hadith of pride and greatness as garments must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve Allah's transcendence.

Imam Razi relates a hadith about Ayat al-Kursi's tongue and declares ta'wil necessary.

Analyzing the Pronoun in the Hadith of Creation in God's Image
Imam Razi introduces the hadith and sets up the pronoun analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses grammatical analysis of the pronoun in the hadith 'created Adam upon His image' to demonstrate that the hadith does not force a literal anthropomorphic interpretation.

Core Idea
The ambiguous pronoun shows the hadith does not require attributing a literal image to Allah.

This lesson introduces a key hadith often cited by anthropomorphists. Imam Razi begins by noting that the word 'image' (sura) does not appear in the Quran but only in transmitted reports. He then presents two versions of the hadith and reveals his method: a grammatical analysis of the pronoun (ha') in 'upon His image', which yields three possible referents, thereby undermining any forced literal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Note the absence of 'sura' in the Quran
Imam Razi starts by stating that the word 'image' does not occur in the Quran, only in hadith. This is important because it shows the issue is from transmitted reports, not the foundational text.
2
Read the two narrated hadiths
Read the two hadiths: one from the Prophet about creation upon His image, and another from Ibn Khuzayma forbidding cursing someone's face because of this fact. Understand their wording and context.
The second hadith reinforces the first by linking the prohibition to the creation account.
3
Analyze the three possible referents of the pronoun
Imam Razi identifies three possibilities for the pronoun: referring to something other than Adam and Allah, referring to Adam himself, or referring to Allah. Examine each and see how they affect interpretation.
This grammatical opening is the foundation for Razi's subsequent arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في إثبات الصورة : اعلم أن هذه اللفظة ما وردت في القرآن لكنها واردة في الأخبار . الخبر الأول : ما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : إن الله تعالى خلق آدم على صورته . وروى ابن خزيمة عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : لا يقولن أحدكم لعبده : قبح الله وجهك ، ووجه من أشبه وجهك ، فإن الله خلق آدم على صورته . والجواب : اعلم أن الهاء في قوله « على صورته » يحتمل أن يكون عائدا إلى شيء غير صورة آدم عليه السلام وغير الله تعالى ، ويحتمل أن يكون عائدا إلى آدم ، ويحتمل أن يكون عائدا إلى الله تعالى . فهذه طرق ثلاثة .
The first chapter: On affirming the image (sura). Know that this word does not occur in the Quran, but it does occur in traditions. The first tradition: what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Indeed Allah the Exalted created Adam upon His image.' And Ibn Khuzayma narrated from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Let none of you say to his slave: May Allah disfigure your face, and the face of whoever resembles your face, for indeed Allah created Adam upon His image.' The response: Know that the pronoun (ha') in His statement 'upon His image' could possibly refer to something other than the image of Adam, peace be upon him, and other than Allah the Exalted; and it could possibly refer to Adam; and it could possibly refer to Allah the Exalted. So these are three ways.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the first chapter, which addresses the issue of 'image' (sura) as found in hadith. The author first notes that the word 'sura' does not appear in the Quran at all, but it is found in the transmitted reports. He then presents two hadith: the first is the well-known statement that Allah created Adam upon His image; the second forbids cursing someone's face because of this fact. After stating these reports, the author gives the theological response (al-jawab) by analyzing the ambiguous pronoun 'ha' in the phrase 'upon His image'. He identifies three possible referents: 1) something other than Adam's image and other than God (e.g., a human being like another prophet); 2) Adam himself (meaning 'upon his own image', i.e., Adam's image as intended); 3) Allah the Exalted (meaning 'upon God's image', which would require interpretation to avoid anthropomorphism). He does not yet choose one; he simply lists the three possibilities, setting up the subsequent discussion.

الصورة
الهاء
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the hadith must be taken literally as affirming a divine image for Allah.
Correction: Imam Razi shows the pronoun is ambiguous, so literalism is not forced; there are multiple possible interpretations.

Checkpoint

B) Three

Key Takeaway

Interpreting the Hadith on Insulting Adam's Face: The First Way
Razi presents two interpretations of a hadith to avoid shatting Adam and anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets a hadith that could imply anthropomorphism or insult to Adam, specifically through the first way which redirects the pronoun away from God and Adam.

Core Idea
The hadith's pronoun refers to someone else, avoiding both anthropomorphism and insult to Adam.

Imam Razi presents a delicate interpretive exercise: a hadith that, if taken literally, could either ascribe a human form to God or insult Prophet Adam. To safeguard divine transcendence and prophetic dignity, he offers a nuanced reading. This lesson explores the first of two major ways, which reassigns the pronoun in the hadith and unfolds two interpretations that protect both God's transcendence and Adam's honor.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the Problem
Understand the theological stakes: the hadith could lead to anthropomorphism (tashbih) or insult to Adam.
The hadith mentions 'face' which could be misconstrued.
2
Identify the First Way
Notice how Razi redirects the pronoun away from God and Adam, grounding the interpretation in linguistic analysis.
3
Explore the First Interpretation
Cursing a person's face indirectly insults Adam because all human faces share Adam's form.
This explanation shows why the Prophet forbade such cursing.
4
Examine the Second Interpretation
The hadith refutes exaggerated claims about Adam's size or form, affirming his normal human form.
This part is incomplete in the passage but the intent is clear.
5
Reflect on the Method
See how careful interpretation preserves doctrine while honoring the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الأول : أن يكون هذا الضمير عائداً إلى غير آدم ، وإلى غير الله تعالى . وعلى هذا التقدير فني تأويل الخبر وجهان : الأول : هو أن من قال لإنسان : قبح الله وجهك ، ووجه من أشبه وجهك، فهذا يكون شتما لآدم عليه السلام ، فإنه لما كانت صورة هذا الإنسان مشابهة الصورة آدم ، كان قوله : قبح الله وجهك، ووجه من أشبه وجهك: شتما لآدم عليه السلام ، ولجميع الأنبياء - عليهم السلام ـ وذلك غير جائز ، فلا جرم نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن ذلك . وإنما خص آدم بالذكر ، لأنه عليه السلام هو الذى ابتدنت خلقه وجهه على هذه الصورة [؟] [هذه] (5) [؟] الثاني : إن المراد منه : إبطال قول من يقول : إن آدم كان على صورة أخرى . مثل مايقال : إنه كان عظيم الجثة ، طويل القامة ، بحيث يكون
The first way: that this pronoun refers to other than Adam and other than God. On this assumption, the interpretation of the hadith has two aspects: First: that if someone says to a person, 'May God disfigure your face and the face of those who resemble your face,' this would be an insult to Adam, peace be upon him, for since the form of this person resembles the form of Adam, his saying, 'May God disfigure your face and the face of those who resemble your face,' is an insult to Adam and to all the prophets—peace be upon them—and this is impermissible, so the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, forbade that. And he specifically mentioned Adam because he, peace be upon him, was the one whose creation began with his face in this form [؟] [this] (5) [؟] Second: the intended meaning is to nullify the statement of those who say that Adam was in another form, such as it is said that he was huge in body, tall in stature, so that he would be…
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents two interpretative ways to understand a prophetic hadith that might otherwise imply anthropomorphism or insult to Adam. The author first states the first way: the pronoun in the hadith is taken to refer to someone other than Adam and other than God. Within this assumption, there are two interpretations. The first interpretation explains that if someone curses another person's face and the face of those resembling him, it indirectly insults Adam (peace be upon him) because all human faces share Adam's form. Thus the Prophet forbade such cursing to protect Adam's dignity. Adam was singled out because he is the original pattern. The second interpretation (within the first way) aims to refute claims that Adam had an exaggerated form (like enormous size). The author argues that such beliefs are not intended by the hadith. The passage is cut off at the end of the second interpretation due to page break.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The hadith actually discourages cursing faces because it indirectly insults Adam, and the pronoun refers to someone else, safeguarding divine transcendence.

Key Takeaway

The True Meaning of 'Surah' (Image) according to Maimonides
Maimonides defines 'surah' as natural form (intellect) and distinguishes it from shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maimonides interprets the word 'surah' (image) in scripture as the intellectual essence (essential form) of a thing, not its physical shape.

Core Idea
'Surah' is intellectual essence, not physical shape.

Maimonides tackles a common misunderstanding that leads to anthropomorphism: the belief that 'image of God' implies a physical form. Using Hebrew philology and philosophical reasoning, he shows that 'surah' refers to the natural form that makes a thing what it is—in humans, the intellect. This lesson explores his argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'surah'
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Note how Maimonides distinguishes the common meaning from the true meaning.
Pay attention to the Hebrew distinction between 'surah' and 'to'ar'.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'surah'
Find where Maimonides explains that the common meaning is shape (called 'to'ar'), while the true meaning is natural form (essential form), which in humans is intellectual apprehension.
The key verse is 'He created him in the image of God'—this refers to the intellect, not the body.
3
Understand the examples
Maimonides gives scriptural examples where 'surah' refers to meaning, not shape. Consider the idols and the 'images of hemorrhoids'.
These examples reinforce that 'surah' is about meaning, not outline.

Source Text

يقول موسى بن ميمون فى دلالة الحائرين . ما نصه : قد ظن الناس : أن الصوره فى اللسان العبراني تدل على شكل الشيء وتخطيطه . فأدى ذلك الى التجسيم المحض . لقوله : « لنصنع الانسان على صورتنا كمتالنا » ( تك ١ : ٢٦ ) وظنوا : أن الله على صورة انسان أعنى شكله وتخطيطه فلزمهم التجسيم المحض ، فاعتقدوه ، ورأوا أنهم أن فارقوا هذا الاعتقاد ، كذبوا النص ، بل يعدمون الاله ان لم يكن جسما ذا وجه ويسد منلهم في الشكل والتخطيط لكنه أكبر وأبهى بزعمهم ومادنه أيضا ليست بدم ولحم غاية ما رأوا أنه يكون تنزيها فى حق الله . أما ما ينبغي أن يقال في نفي الجسمانية ، واثبات الوحدانية الحقيقية التي لا حقيقة لها الا بدفع الجسمانية ، فستعرف برهان ذلك كله من هذه المقالة ، وانما التنبيه هنا في هذا الفصل على تبيين معنى الصورة والمثال . فأقول : أن الصورة المشهورة عند الجمهور التى هى شكل الشيء وتخطيطه ، اسمها الخصيص بها فى اللسان العبراني ) صفة » قال : « حسن الهيئة ، جميل المنظر » ( نك ٣٩ : ٦ ( « ما هى هيئته ( ؟ ) ۱ مل ۲۸ : ١٤ ) « هيئة أبناء الملوك ( ) قض ٨ : ۱۸ ( وقيل فى الصورة الصناعية : « وبسويه بالمنحت ويرسمه بالبركار » ( أش ٤٤ : ١٣ ) وهذه اسمية لم تقع على الاله قط . وحاشا وكلا - أما الصورة . فهي تقع على الصوره الطبعية أعنى على المعنى الذى به تجوهر الشيء وصار ما هو؟ وهو حقيقه من حيث هو ذلك الموجود الذى ذلك المعنى في الانسان هو الذي عنه يكون الادراك الانساني . ومن أجل هذا الادراك العقلي قبل فيه : « على صورة الله خلقه » ( تك ۱ : ۲۷ ) ولذلك قيل : ) نصقر خيالهم » ) مز ۷۲ : ٦٠ ) لأن الاحتقار لاحق للنفس التي هي الصورة النوعية ، لا لأشكال الأعضاء وتخطيطها . وكذلك أقول : ان العلة في سمية الأصنام صورا : كون المطلوب منها : معناها المظنون به ، لا شكلها ونخيطها . وكذلك أقول فى منال : « تماثيل به اسبركم » صدم 1: ٥ ( لأنه كان المراد منها : معنى دفع أذية البواسير ، لا شكل البواسير . فان لم يكن بد من أن تكون ( صور بواسيركم » من أجل الشكل والتخطيط ، فتكون الصورة اسما مشتركا ، أو مشككا بقال على الصورة النوعية ، وعلى الصورة الصناعية ، وما ماثلها من اتكال الأجسام الطبيعية وتخاطيطها ) ويكون المراد به في قوله : نخلق آدم على صورتنا » : الصورة النوعية ، التي هي الادراك العقلي ، لا الشكل والتخطيط قد بينا لك الفرق بين الصورة والهيئة ، وبينا معنى الصورة : أما المثال : فهو اسم من مثل : وهو أيضا : شبه في المعنى . لأن قوله : « شابهت قوق البرية ( ) مزمور ۱۰۱ : ۷ ( لبس أنه شابه أجنحتها ورشها ، به شابه حزنه حزنها . وكذلك كل شجرة فى جنة الله ، لم يماثلها في بهجنه ، شبه في معنى الحسن ( مناله ) : « لهم حمة مثل حمة الحية » ( مز ٥٧ : ٥ ) « مثله كالأسد الذي يقوم الى الفريسة » ) مز ١٦ : ١٢ ) كلها شبه فى المعنى ، لا فى الشكل والتخطيط . وكذلك قبل : « شبه العرش : شبه عرش » ( مز ۱ : ۲۷ ( شبه في معنى الرفعة والجلالة ، لا فى تربيعه وغلظه وطول رجليه - كما يظن المساكين وكذلك شبه الصوانات . فلما خص الانسان بمعنى فيه غريب جدا ليس في شيء من الموجودات من لدن فلك القمر هو الادراك العقلى ، الذي لا تتصرف فيه حاسة ولا جارحة ولا جانحة ، شبه بادراك الاله الذي ليس هو بآلة . وان كان لا شبه فى الحقيقة - لكن على بادى الرأي . وقيل فى الانسان : من أجل هذا المعنى - أعنى : من أجل العقل الالهي المتصل به أنه على صورة الله وشاكلته لا أن الله - تعالى - جسم فيكون ذا شكل » ( دلالة الحائرين ج ١ ص ٢٨٢٦ ) .
Moses Maimonides says in The Guide of the Perplexed, verbatim: People thought that the word 'surah' in the Hebrew language indicates the shape of a thing and its outline. This led to pure anthropomorphism because of His saying, 'Let us make man in our image, after our likeness' (Genesis 1:26). They thought that God is in the image of a man, meaning his shape and outline, so they fell into pure anthropomorphism and believed it. They thought that if they departed from this belief, they would falsify the text, nay, they would annihilate God if He were not a body with a face and limbs having shape and outline, but larger and more splendid in their view, and that His material is not flesh and blood—the utmost they saw as transcendence in respect of God. As for what should be said concerning the negation of corporeality and the affirmation of true unity, which has no reality except by repudiating corporeality, you will know the proof of all that from this treatise. And here only the warning in this chapter is to clarify the meaning of 'surah' and 'mithal'. So I say: The common 'surah' among the public, which is the shape of a thing and its outline, has a specific name in the Hebrew language: 'to'ar'. He said: 'Fair of form, goodly of appearance' (Genesis 39:6); 'What is his appearance?' (1 Kings 28:14); 'The appearance of the sons of kings' (Judges 8:18). And it was said concerning artificial form: 'He shapes it with a plane and marks it with a compass' (Isaiah 44:13). These names have never been applied to God—far be it! Rather 'surah' applies to the natural form, meaning the meaning by which a thing becomes a substance and becomes what it is, and its essence in terms of that being. That meaning in man is from which originates human apprehension. And because of this intellectual apprehension, it was said of him: 'He created him in the image of God' (Genesis 1:27). Therefore it was said: 'They consume his image' (Psalms 73:20), because contempt attaches to the soul which is the specific form, not to the shapes of limbs and their outline. Likewise I say: The reason for calling idols 'images' is that what is sought from them is their supposed meaning, not their shape and outline. Likewise I say concerning the phrase: 'Images of your hemorrhoids' (1 Samuel 5:5?) because what was intended was the meaning of repelling the harm of hemorrhoids, not the shape of hemorrhoids. If it must be that 'images of your hemorrhoids' is for shape and outline, then 'surah' would be a homonym or analogical term applied to the specific form and to the artificial form and what resembles them of the outlines of natural bodies and their configurations. And what is meant by His saying 'Let us create Adam in our image' is the specific form, which is intellectual apprehension, not shape and outline. We have shown you the difference between 'surah' and 'hay'a', and we have shown the meaning of 'surah'. As for 'mithal' (likeness), it is a noun derived from 'maththala', and it also means similitude in meaning. Because His saying: 'I resemble the pelican of the wilderness' (Psalm 102:7) is not that he resembled its wings and feathers, but that he resembled its grief in his grief. Likewise every tree in the garden of God, none resembled it in its splendour—similitude in the meaning of beauty. (His saying): 'They have venom like the venom of a serpent' (Psalm 58:5), 'He is like a lion that rises to prey' (Psalm 17:12)—all are similitude in meaning, not in shape and outline. Likewise it was said: 'The likeness of the throne: the likeness of the throne' (Ezekiel 1:26) —similitude in the meaning of loftiness and majesty, not in its squaring, thickness, or length of legs, as the poor wretches think. Likewise the likeness of the cherubim. Since man was distinguished by a meaning very extraordinary, not found in any of the existents from the sphere of the moon downwards—namely intellectual apprehension, which no sense, organ, or bodily part operates—he was compared to the apprehension of God, which is not by an instrument. Even though there is no likeness in reality, but according to first glance. And it was said concerning man, because of this meaning—I mean because of the divine intellect connected to him—that he is in the image of God and His likeness, not that God—Exalted is He—is a body such that He has a shape.' (The Guide of the Perplexed, vol. 1, p. 282?).
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Maimonides' Guide of the Perplexed, included by Razi to explain the proper interpretation of 'image of God' in scripture to avoid anthropomorphism. Maimonides begins by noting that people mistakenly think the Hebrew word 'surah' means shape or outline, leading to pure anthropomorphism (tajsim) because of verses like 'Let us make man in our image.' They believe God has a human form, though larger and more splendid, and think denying this would falsify the text or even negate God's existence. Maimonides then states his intent: to clarify the true meanings of 'surah' and 'mithal'. He distinguishes between the common meaning of 'surah' as shape (called 'to'ar' in Hebrew) and the philosophical meaning of 'surah' as the natural form or essence (al-surah al-tabi'iyya) that makes a thing what it is and is its true reality. In humans, this essence is intellectual apprehension (al-idrak al-'aqli). Thus the verse 'He created him in the image of God' refers to the intellect, not any physical form. Maimonides supports this with scriptural examples: Psalm 73:20 says 'they consume his image' meaning they destroy his dignity attached to the soul, not bodily shape. He extends this to idols and the biblical 'images of hemorrhoids' (1 Samuel 5:5?) as examples where 'image' means intended meaning, not shape. If forced, 'surah' could be considered homonymous between essential form and artificial shape. Then he turns to 'mithal' (likeness), showing from various Psalms that it always means likeness in meaning or quality, not in shape. For example, the throne of God is described in terms of majesty, not dimensions. Ultimately, man's unique intellectual apprehension is compared to God's knowledge (without instrument) as a first-glance analogy, not a real likeness. Thus man is said to be in God's image because of the intellect connected to him, not because God has a body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'surah' (image) in the Bible always means physical shape or outline.
Correction: Maimonides shows that 'surah' refers to the essential form (intellect) and only the word 'to'ar' means shape; 'surah' in divine context never means shape.

Checkpoint
According to Maimonides, what does the word 'surah' refer to in Genesis 1:27 ('He created him in the image of God')?
A) The physical shape of the human body.
B) The human soul's intellectual apprehension.
C) The external appearance of the person.
D) The size of the person's body.

Key Takeaway
Maimonides redefines 'surah' as intellectual form to refute anthropomorphism and affirm divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture God in your mind, do you unconsciously imagine a shape or form? How does Maimonides' teaching confront that habit? What will you do today to retrain your imagination to think of God as beyond any physical attribute?
?
Your intellect is the 'image of God' within you. Are you feeding it with truth and worship, or neglecting it? If you truly believed this, what would change in how you spend your time?
?
The common error was to reduce God to a human-like figure. Have you ever felt more comfortable with a 'manageable' God? What would it cost you to fully embrace a God whose essence is utterly transcendent? Will you accept that cost?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
The True Meaning of 'Mithal' (Likeness) according to Maimonides
Maimonides defines 'mithal' as similitude in meaning and refutes physical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maimonides interprets 'mithal' (likeness) as similitude in meaning or quality, not in physical shape, using scriptural examples to support divine transcendence.

Core Idea
'Mithal' means similarity in meaning, not in shape.

Having clarified 'surah', Maimonides turns to 'mithal' (likeness), another term often misunderstood anthropomorphically. He demonstrates through multiple biblical examples that 'mithal' always indicates a resemblance in meaning, quality, or function, never in physical form. This lesson examines his argument and its role in upholding tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'mithal'
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on Maimonides' definition and examples of 'mithal'.
Compare his examples—pelican, lion, throne—all rely on meaning, not shape.
2
Identify the key principle
Maimonides states that 'mithal' is a noun derived from 'maththala' meaning similitude in meaning. Find this statement.
The principle: likeness is always in meaning, not in outline.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: the pelican simile, the lion simile, the throne 'likeness'. Notice how each is about qualities like grief, ferocity, majesty, not physical form.
The throne is described in terms of loftiness, not dimensions.
4
Understand the application to God
Maimonides applies this to the likeness between God and man: it is based on intellect, a non-physical quality, not a body.
This preserves divine transcendence.

This led to pure anthropomorphism because of His saying, 'Let us make man in our image, after our likeness' (Genesis 1:26). They thought that God is in the image of a man, meaning his shape and outline, so they fell into pure anthropomorphism and believed it. And here only the warning in this chapter is to clarify the meaning of 'surah' and 'mithal'. So I say: The common 'surah' among the public, which is the shape of a thing and its outline, has a specific name in the Hebrew language: 'to'ar'.
