If it must be that 'images of your hemorrhoids' is for shape and outline, then 'surah' would be a homonym or analogical term applied to the specific form and to the artificial form and what resembles them of the outlines of natural bodies and their configurations. And what is meant by His saying 'Let us create Adam in our image' is the specific form, which is intellectual apprehension, not shape and outline. We have shown you the difference between 'surah' and 'hay'a', and we have shown the meaning of 'surah'. As for 'mithal' (likeness), it is a noun derived from 'maththala', and it also means similitude in meaning. Because His saying: 'I resemble the pelican of the wilderness' (Psalm 102:7) is not that he resembled its wings and feathers, but that he resembled its grief in his grief. Likewise every tree in the garden of God, none resembled it in its splendour—similitude in the meaning of beauty. (His saying): 'They have venom like the venom of a serpent' (Psalm 58:5), 'He is like a lion that rises to prey' (Psalm 17:12)—all are similitude in meaning, not in shape and outline. Likewise it was said: 'The likeness of the throne: the likeness of the throne' (Ezekiel 1:26) —similitude in the meaning of loftiness and majesty, not in its squaring, thickness, or length of legs, as the poor wretches think. And it was said concerning man, because of this meaning—I mean because of the divine intellect connected to him—that he is in the image of God and His likeness, not that God—Exalted is He—is a body such that He has a shape.' Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Maimonides' Guide of the Perplexed, included by Razi to explain the proper interpretation of 'image of God' in scripture to avoid anthropomorphism. Maimonides begins by noting that people mistakenly think the Hebrew word 'surah' means shape or outline, leading to pure anthropomorphism (tajsim) because of verses like 'Let us make man in our image.' They believe God has a human form, though larger and more splendid, and think denying this would falsify the text or even negate God's existence. Maimonides then states his intent: to clarify the true meanings of 'surah' and 'mithal'. He distinguishes between the common meaning of 'surah' as shape (called 'to'ar' in Hebrew) and the philosophical meaning of 'surah' as the natural form or essence (al-surah al-tabi'iyya) that makes a thing what it is and is its true reality. In humans, this essence is intellectual apprehension (al-idrak al-'aqli). Thus the verse 'He created him in the image of God' refers to the intellect, not any physical form. Maimonides supports this with scriptural examples: Psalm 73:20 says 'they consume his image' meaning they destroy his dignity attached to the soul, not bodily shape. He extends this to idols and the biblical 'images of hemorrhoids' (1 Samuel 5:5?) as examples where 'image' means intended meaning, not shape. If forced, 'surah' could be considered homonymous between essential form and artificial shape. Then he turns to 'mithal' (likeness), showing from various Psalms that it always means likeness in meaning or quality, not in shape. For example, the throne of God is described in terms of majesty, not dimensions. Ultimately, man's unique intellectual apprehension is compared to God's knowledge (without instrument) as a first-glance analogy, not a real likeness. Thus man is said to be in God's image because of the intellect connected to him, not because God has a body.

Key Terms
المثال
الشبه
التنزيه
الإدراك العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When the Bible says 'likeness of God' or 'likeness of the throne', it implies a physical shape or form similar to created objects.
Correction: Maimonides shows that 'mithal' always refers to likeness in meaning, quality, or function—never in physical shape—thus preserving God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does Maimonides say about the word 'mithal' (likeness) in the Bible?
A) It means a physical copy or image.
B) It means similitude in meaning, not in shape.
C) It is only used for idols.
D) It refers to the shape of the throne.

Key Takeaway
Maimonides teaches that 'mithal' means similarity in meaning, not shape, safeguarding divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'knowing' or 'majestic', do you ever subconsciously imagine a version of your own knowledge or majesty, just magnified? How does Maimonides' insistence that likeness is only in meaning, never in form, challenge that? What must you strip away in your understanding of God to truly worship Him as transcendent?
?
This lesson shows that even the prophets used analogies from creation to speak about God. Have you ever taken a scriptural description of God too literally, forming a mental image? How can you train yourself to read such passages through the lens of tanzih? What practical step will you take this week to purify your concept of God?
?
You claim that God is unique and incomparable, yet you inevitably use human categories to think about Him. Is that a contradiction? Maimonides says it is a 'first-glance analogy'. Are you content to leave it at that, or does his explanation push you to a deeper awareness of God's otherness? How does this affect your prayer and reliance on Him today?

The True Meaning of 'Surah' in 'Created in God's Image'
Razi refutes the claim that Adam had a different physical form and defines 'surah' as rational perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the word 'surah' in the hadith 'God created Adam in His image' refers to the rational soul, not physical form.

Core Idea
God's image is not a shape but the gift of intellect.

Imam Razi begins by stating that the second purpose of the hadith is to refute those who claim Adam had a different physical form. He then defines 'surah' as the specific form (al-surah al-naw'iyya), which is the rational perception (al-idrak al-aqli). This lesson unpacks that definition and shows why it matters for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation. Notice how Razi moves from refutation to definition to examples.
The passage includes biblical citations; focus on Razi's argument.
2
Identify the key claim being refuted
Find where Razi states what he is refuting: that Adam had a huge body or different physique. Understand why this is problematic.
3
Understand the definition of 'surah'
Razi defines 'surah' as the specific form by which a thing is what it is—here, the rational soul. Note how he links it to human perception.
This is the core doctrinal point.
4
Examine the supporting examples
Look at the examples of idols and the hemorrhoids. They show that 'surah' often refers to meaning or function, not physical shape.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that when the hadith says 'created in Our image', it means the specific form of rational perception, not shape or outline.
This safeguards tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إن المراد منه: إبطال قول من يقول: إن آدم كان على صورة أخرى. مثل ما يقال: إنه كان عظيم الجثة، طويل القامة، بحيث يكون الصورة الطبعية أعني على المعنى الذي به تجوهر الشيء وصار ما هو؟ وهو حقيقته من حيث هو ذلك الموجود. ذلك المعنى في الإنسان هو الذي عنه يكون الإدراك الإنساني. ومن أجل هذا الإدراك العقلي قيل فيه: «على صورة الله خلقه» (تكوين 1:27) ولذلك قيل: «نصقر خيالهم» (مزمور 72:60) لأن الاحتقار لاحق للنفس التي هي الصورة النوعية، لا لأشكال الأعضاء وتخطيطها. وكذلك أقول: إن العلة في تسمية الأصنام صورا: كون المطلوب منها: معناها المظنون به، لا شكلها وتخطيطها. وكذلك أقول في مثال: «تماثيل به أسفاركم» (صم 1:5) لأنه كان المراد منها: معنى دفع أذية البواسير، لا شكل البواسير. فإن لم يكن بد من أن تكون «صور بواسيركم» من أجل الشكل والتخطيط، فتكون الصورة اسما مشتركا، أو مشككا يقال على الصورة النوعية، وعلى الصورة الصناعية، وما ماثلها من أشكال الأجسام الطبيعية وتخطيطها. ويكون المراد به في قوله: «خلق آدم على صورتنا»: الصورة النوعية، التي هي الإدراك العقلي، لا الشكل والتخطيط. قد بينا لك الفرق بين الصورة والهيئة، وبينا معنى الصورة: أما المثال: فهو اسم من مثل: وهو أيضا: شبه في المعنى. لأن قوله: «شابهت قوق البرية» (مزمور 101:7) ليس أنه شابه أجنحتها وريشها، بل شابه حزنه حزنها. وكذلك كل شجرة في جنة الله، لم يماثلها في بهجته، شبه في معنى الحسن. «لهم حمة مثل حمة الحية» (مزمور 57:5) «مثله كالأسد الذي يقوم إلى الفريسة» (مزمور 16:12) كلها شبه في المعنى، لا في الشكل والتخطيط. وكذلك قيل: «شبه العرش: شبه عرش» (مزمور 1:27) شبه في معنى الرفعة والجلالة، لا في تربيعه وغلظه وطول رجليه - كما يظن المساكين. وكذلك شبه الصور. فلما خص الإنسان بمعنى فيه غريب جدا ليس في شيء من الموجودات من لدن فلك القمر، وهو الإدراك العقلي، الذي لا تتصرف فيه حاسة ولا جارحة ولا جانحة، شبه بإدراك الإله الذي ليس هو بآلة. وإن كان لا شبه في الحقيقة - لكن على بادي الرأي. وقيل في الإنسان: من أجل هذا المعنى - أعني: من أجل العقل الإلهي المتصل به أنه على صورة الله وشاكلته لا أن الله - تعالى - جسم فيكون ذا شكل» (دلالة الحائرين ج 1 ص 2826).
The second: it is intended by it: refuting the statement of whoever says: that Adam was upon a different form. Like what is said: that he was huge of body, tall of stature, such that the natural form exists, meaning by that meaning by which a thing is substanced and became what it is? And it is its reality in terms of that existence. That meaning in the human is that from which human perception comes. On account of this rational perception it was said concerning him: 'He created him in the image of God' (Gen. 1:27) and therefore it was said: 'they contempt his image' (Ps. 72:60) because contempt attaches to the soul which is the specific form, not to the shapes of limbs and their outlines. Likewise I say: the cause for calling idols 'images' is that what is sought from them is their assumed meaning, not their shape and outline. Likewise I say in the example: 'Images of your hemorrhoids' (1 Sam. 5:?) because what was intended by them was the meaning of repelling the harm of hemorrhoids, not the shape of hemorrhoids. If there is no alternative but that 'images of your hemorrhoids' be for shape and outline, then the word 'image' is a homonym, or amphibolous, applied to the specific form, and to the artificial form, and to what resembles them of shapes of natural bodies and their outlines. And what is intended by His saying: 'We created Adam in Our image' is the specific form, which is rational perception, not shape and outline. We have already explained to you the difference between image and form, and explained the meaning of image: As for the likeness, it is a noun derived from 'like', and it is also a resemblance in meaning. Because His saying: 'it resembled the wild ox' (Ps. 101:7) is not that it resembled its wings and its feathers, but rather its grief resembled its grief. Likewise every tree in the garden of God was not equaled by it in its splendor, a resemblance in the meaning of beauty. 'They have venom like the venom of a serpent' (Ps. 57:5) 'He is like a lion that arises to prey' (Ps. 16:12) all are resemblances in meaning, not in shape and outline. Likewise it was said: 'The likeness of the throne: a likeness of a throne' (Ps. 1:27) a resemblance in the meaning of exaltation and majesty, not in its squareness and thickness and length of its legs - as the poor fellows suppose. Likewise the resemblance of images. So when the human was distinguished by a meaning exceedingly strange which is not in anything of existents from the sphere of the moon downwards, that is rational perception, into which no sense nor limb nor impulse enters, he was likened to the perception of the God who is not subject to instrumentality. And if there is no resemblance in reality - but at first glance. And it was said concerning the human: on account of this meaning - I mean: because of the divine intellect connected to him that he is in the image of God and His likeness, not that God - Exalted - is a body such that He has a shape' (Guide for the Perplexed vol. 1 p. 2826).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the second meaning of the hadith 'God created Adam in His image'. Imam Razi states that the purpose is to refute the claim that Adam had a different physical form than humans today, such as being huge or tall. He then defines 'surah' (image) not as physical shape but as the specific form (al-surah al-naw'iyya) which is the rational soul (al-idrak al-aqli). He argues that when Scripture says humans are created in God's image, it refers to this intellectual perception, not bodily form. He supports this with examples from the Bible and from the usage of 'image' for idols and other objects, showing that what is intended is the meaning or function, not the physical outline. He distinguishes between 'surah' (image) and 'hay'a' (form), explaining that 'mithal' (likeness) also refers to resemblance in meaning, not in shape. He gives several biblical examples where likeness is in meaning (e.g., 'resembles the wild ox' refers to grief, not wings). He concludes that the human is distinguished by rational perception, which is unlike anything in the lower world, and is likened to God's perception, which is not through instruments. He cites Maimonides' Guide for the Perplexed to reinforce that this does not imply God is a body.

Key Terms
صورة
الصورة النوعية
الإدراك العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'image of God' means God has a physical form or that Adam's body was physically different from humans today.
Correction: Razi clarifies that 'image' refers to the rational soul, which is non-physical, and thereby upholds God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what does the word 'surah' (image) primarily refer to when it is said that God created Adam in His image?
A) Adam's physical body and shape
B) Adam's stature and height
C) The specific form of rational perception (al-idrak al-aqli)
D) The likeness of God's physical appearance

Key Takeaway
The word 'surah' in 'created in God's image' means rational perception, not physical form, preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of being created 'in God's image', do you ever feel pride in your physical appearance? How does Razi's redefinition challenge that pride and redirect your focus to your intellect? Will you today thank God for your mind, not just your mirror?
?
Razi says contempt attaches to the soul, not the body. Think of a time you felt disrespected. Was it truly about your body, or about your intellect and character? How can you shift your focus to honor the 'surah' of rational perception in others, starting with someone you find easy to overlook?
?
If your rational perception is your true 'image', are you cultivating it? What one change can you make today to exercise your God-given intellect in seeking truth, rather than passively consuming information?

Distinguishing 'Surah', 'Hay'a', and 'Mithal' in Scriptural Language
Razi explains the differences between image, form, and likeness, providing examples of resemblance in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi distinguishes between 'surah', 'hay'a', and 'mithal', and how scriptural likenesses refer to meaning, not physical appearance.

Core Idea
Likeness in scripture is about meaning, not shape.

Imam Razi continues his discussion by clarifying the differences between key terms used in scripture: image (surah), form (hay'a), and likeness (mithal). He provides multiple biblical examples to demonstrate that when God is described using these terms, the resemblance is in meaning—such as majesty or grief—not in physical outline. This lesson delves into those distinctions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the passage from 'قد بينا لك الفرق' to the end. Notice the sequence of examples.
2
Identify the three terms and their differences
Razi distinguishes between 'surah' (image), 'hay'a' (form), and 'mithal' (likeness). Focus on how he defines each.
He already explained 'surah' earlier; now he focuses on 'mithal'.
3
Analyze the biblical examples
Look at the examples: 'resembles the wild ox' (grief), 'like a lion' (aggressiveness), 'likeness of the throne' (exaltation). Note that all refer to meaning, not shape.
4
Understand the connection to human intellect
Razi argues that humans are distinguished by rational perception, which is likened to God's perception (without instruments). This is why humans are called 'in God's image'.
This is the theological climax.
5
Reflect on the conclusion from Maimonides
Razi cites the Guide for the Perplexed to reinforce that God is not a body. The likeness is in the intellect, not physical shape.

First Interpretation: The Prophet Pointed to a Specific Person
Razi presents the first interpretation of the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of the hadith 'God created Adam upon his image' as explained by Imam Razi, specifically that the Prophet gestured to a particular man and meant that Adam's form was exactly like that man's form.

Core Idea
Adam's image was identical to a specific human form, not God's.

Imam Razi begins his discussion of the hadith about Adam's creation by offering a straightforward interpretation. He explains that when the Prophet said 'upon his image,' he was pointing to a particular man present at that time, indicating that Adam's form was exactly like that man's. This interpretation avoids any implication that the 'image' refers to God, thus preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation. Notice that Razi starts mid-sentence describing a man with his head near the sky.
2
Identify the key claim
The Prophet pointed to a specific man and said Adam was created upon his image, meaning Adam's form was identical to that man's.
3
Understand the refutation
Recognize that this interpretation refutes the misconception that Adam had a different, non-human form.
This guards against imagining Adam as a giant or non-human.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأسه قريبا من السماء . فالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أشار إلى إنسان معين ، وقال : إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي كان شكل آدم ، مثل شكل هذا الإنسان ، من غير تفاوت البتة . فأبطل هذا البيان : وهم من توهم أن آدم - عليه السلام - كان على صورة أخرى ، غير هذه الصورة .
His head was close to the sky. So the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, pointed to a specific man and said: 'Indeed, God created Adam upon his image,' meaning the form of Adam was like the form of this man, with no difference whatsoever. So this statement refuted the misconception of whoever imagined that Adam, peace be upon him, was upon another form, other than this form.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the first interpretation of the hadith 'God created Adam upon his image.' Razi begins from the middle of a sentence describing that the Prophet pointed to a specific man who was tall such that his head was near the sky. He then reports that the Prophet said, 'God created Adam upon his image,' explaining that this means Adam's form was exactly like the form of that man, with no difference at all. The purpose of this explanation is to refute the misunderstanding of those who thought that Adam had a different form, perhaps a giant or non-human form. Thus, the first interpretation keeps the meaning of 'image' tied to a human being, avoiding any implication of God's image.

Key Terms
صورة
آدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adam was created with a gigantic or non-human form different from present-day humans.
Correction: The Prophet pointed to a specific man and said Adam's form was exactly like that man's form, with no difference.

Checkpoint
What does the first interpretation say about the phrase 'upon his image'?
A) It means Adam was created in God's image.
B) It means Adam's form was identical to a specific man the Prophet pointed to.
C) It means Adam was created without any form.
D) It means Adam's form changed over time.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation of 'upon his image' is that the pronoun refers to a human being, not God, thus safeguarding divine transcendence.

Second Interpretation: The Pronoun Refers to Adam
Razi invokes the grammatical rule of pronoun referent proximity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the pronoun in the hadith 'his image' refers to Adam, not God, based on the grammatical rule of pronoun referent proximity.

Core Idea
The pronoun must refer to the nearest noun; that noun is Adam, not God.

Imam Razi presents a straightforward grammatical argument to protect divine transcendence. By applying the rule that a pronoun returns to the nearest antecedent, he shows that the disputed pronoun in 'his image' refers to Adam, not Allah. This interpretation removes any anthropomorphic implication from the hadith. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how concise Razi's argument is.
2
Identify the grammatical rule
Razi invokes the rule that a pronoun must return to the nearest mentioned noun. Find this rule in the text.
The Arabic phrase 'أقرب المذكورات' is key.
3
Apply the rule to the hadith
Confirm that Adam is the nearest mentioned noun in the hadith. Ask yourself how this protects God from having an image.
This connects to the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني : أن يكون الضمير عائداً إلى آدم - عليه السلام - وهذا أولى الوجوه الثلاثة . لأن عود الضمير إلى أقرب المذكورات واجب. وفى هذا الحديث : أقرب الأشياء المذكورة هو آدم عليه السلام ، فكان عود الضمير إليه أولى .
The second way: that the pronoun refers back to Adam, peace be upon him, and this is the most appropriate of the three aspects. Because the return of the pronoun to the nearest mentioned thing is obligatory. In this hadith, the nearest mentioned thing is Adam, peace be upon him, so the return of the pronoun to him is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and logical argument for interpreting the pronoun in 'his image' as referring to Adam. Razi calls this the 'second way' (presumably after a first way that interpreted the pronoun as referring to God, which is rejected). He states that the pronoun should return to the nearest antecedent mentioned in the text, which is a grammatical rule. In the hadith, the nearest mentioned entity is Adam, so the pronoun refers to him. This is the most appropriate of the three possible referents (the other two being God or the Prophet). This interpretation removes any possibility of God having an image, thus preserving divine transcendence.

Key Terms
ضمير
عود الضمير
أقرب المذكورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pronoun in 'his image' refers to God, implying God has an image.
Correction: Razi shows the pronoun must refer to the nearest noun, which is Adam, not God.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the pronoun in 'his image' refers to Adam?
A) Because Adam is the most important person.
B) Because the pronoun must refer to the nearest mentioned noun.
C) Because the Prophet said so.
D) Because there is no other option.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses the grammatical rule of pronoun referent proximity to argue that the image belongs to Adam, not to Allah, thus preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Razi's argument protects Allah from being compared to creation. Have you ever casually described Allah with human-like attributes without thinking about the implications? How can you ensure that your words about Allah are always grounded in textual precision, not cultural habit?

Preservation from Disfigurement: The First Interpretation
Razi presents the first sub-interpretation: preservation from disfigurement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith 'God created Adam upon His image' as a preservation of Adam's form from change, distinguishing it from a previous interpretation.

Core Idea
Adam's sin did not change his form; God preserved him from disfigurement.

Imam Razi offers a nuanced explanation of the well-known hadith about Adam's creation. In this first sub-interpretation, he focuses on the honor God bestowed upon Adam by not punishing him with a change in form, unlike other creatures. This lesson explores how Razi uses the concept of 'preservation from muskh' to clarify the meaning of the prophetic statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from Razi
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi sets up the context of Adam's honor and his slip.
Pay attention to the comparison with the serpent and peacock.
2
Understand the key claim
Identify the main point: God did not change Adam's form, leaving him in his original creation as an honor.
3
Compare with the previous interpretation
Razi explicitly contrasts this interpretation with the one before it. Note the difference: the previous one only stated continuity of form; this one adds the reason of preservation from punishment.
This helps see the progression of Razi's argument.
4
Reflect on the broader theological point
Consider why this matters for divine transcendence and prophethood. It shows God's special treatment of Adam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه تعالى لما عظم أمر آدم ، يجعله مسجود الملائكة . ثم إنه أتى بتلك الزلة . فالله تعالى لم يعاقبه بمثل ما عاقب به غيره ، فإنه نقل : أن الله تعالى أخرجه من الجنة، وأخرج معه الحية والطاووس ، وغير تعالى خلقهما مع أنه لم يغير خلقة آدم عليه السلام ، بل تركه على الخلقة الأولى إكراما له وصونا له عن عذاب المسخ . فقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « إن الله تعالى خلق آدم على صورته ، معناه خلق آدم على هذه الصورة التى هى الآن باقية من غير وقوع التبديل فيها . والفرق بين هذا الجواب ، والذي قبله : أن المقصود من هذا : بيان أنه عليه السلام كان مصونا عن المسخ . والجواب الأول ليس فيه ، إلا بيان أن هذه الصورة الموجودة ، ليست إلا هى التى كانت موجودة من قبل ، من غير تعرض لبيان أنه جعل مصونا عن المسخ ، بسبب زلته ، أن غيره صار محسوخا .
First: When God Almighty magnified the affair of Adam, making him one to whom the angels prostrate, then he committed that slip. So God Almighty did not punish him as He punished others. For it is reported that God Almighty expelled him from Paradise, and expelled with him the serpent and the peacock, and He changed their forms while He did not change the form of Adam, peace be upon him, but left him in the first form as an honor for him and a preservation for him from the punishment of disfigurement. So his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed God Almighty created Adam upon his image' means He created Adam upon this image which now remains with no alteration occurring in it. And the difference between this answer and the one before it is that the intent of this is to show that he, peace be upon him, was preserved from disfigurement. And the first answer contains only the statement that this existing image is exactly what existed before, without addressing that he was made immune to disfigurement because of his slip, while others became disfigured.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the first sub-interpretation under the second way, explaining the hadith as indicating that Adam’s form was preserved from change. Razi begins by noting that God honored Adam by making the angels prostrate to him, but then Adam committed a sin (the slip). Unlike other beings that were punished by having their forms changed (like the serpent and peacock that were transformed), God did not change Adam’s form; He left him in his original creation as an honor and to protect him from the punishment of disfigurement (maskh). Therefore, the Prophet’s statement means that God created Adam upon the same image that remains today, without alteration. Razi then distinguishes this interpretation from the previous one (the first interpretation from page 115): the first only stated that the existing form is the same as the original, while this one adds the idea that Adam was specifically preserved from disfigurement as a consequence of his sin, whereas others were disfigured.

Key Terms
مسخ
خلقة
إكرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adam's sin caused his form to change.
Correction: According to Razi's first sub-interpretation, God preserved Adam's form unchanged as an honor, unlike others.

Checkpoint
What does this interpretation say about Adam's form after his sin?
A) It was changed into a different form.
B) It was preserved as the original form.
C) It became like the serpent.
D) It became spiritual.

Key Takeaway
Adam's physical form remained unchanged after his sin because God honored him by preserving him from the punishment of disfigurement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you expect God to disfigure you or change your form? How does Adam's preservation from physical disfigurement challenge your view of God's mercy in the face of your own mistakes? Will you trust that He can preserve your original dignity despite your slips?
?
The serpent and peacock were disfigured—their outward form was changed. Are there ways you have allowed sin to 'disfigure' your soul, altering the pure nature God gave you? What would it mean for God to restore you to your original form, as He did for Adam?
?
Adam’s slip led to expulsion from Paradise, yet his form was unchanged. Do you believe that your own spiritual failures have permanently marred you in God's sight? Or can you cling to the honor God originally granted you, knowing that He preserves those He loves? What must you do today to reclaim that honor?

Refuting Materialists: Adam's Direct Creation
Razi refutes materialists using Adam's direct creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith about Adam's creation refutes materialist beliefs that human generation requires sperm and menstrual blood.

Core Idea
Adam's direct creation denies materialist claims of necessary biological generation.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the second sub-interpretation of the hadith, which directly challenges the materialists (al-dahriyya). These materialists argued that humans can only be generated through sperm and menstrual blood. The Prophet's statement that God created Adam 'upon his image' from the very beginning, without any prior biological stages, serves as a refutation of that view.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and interpretation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi introduces the second sub-interpretation.
The passage is concise; focus on the key contrast.
2
Identify the materialist claim
The materialists claim humans are generated only through sperm and menstrual blood. Find this in the Arabic.
3
Understand the refutation
The Prophet's statement that Adam was created 'upon his image' directly, without sperm, clot, or chewed lump, refutes the materialist position.
This shows creation is not bound by natural biological processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى : المراد منه : إبطال قول الدهرية . الذين يقولون : «إن الإنسان لا يتولد إلا بواسطة النطفة ، ودم الطمث » فقال عليه السلام : إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، ابتداء من غير تقدم نطفة وعلقة ومضغة
Second: The intended meaning is the refutation of the statement of the materialists (al-dahriyya) who say: 'Man is not generated except through the intermediary of sperm and menstrual blood.' So he, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed, God created Adam upon his image, beginning without the prior existence of sperm, a clot, or a chewed lump.'
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the second sub-interpretation, which uses the hadith to refute the materialists (al-dahriyya), who held that human generation only occurs through biological processes involving sperm and menstrual blood. The Prophet’s statement that God created Adam ‘upon his image’ is interpreted as meaning that Adam was created directly from the start, without any prior stages of sperm (nutfa), a clot of blood (‘alaqa), or a chewed lump (mudgha). Thus, the hadith affirms supernatural creation, denying materialistic naturalism.

Key Terms
الدهرية
نطفة
علقة
مضغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith is only about the form or shape of Adam.
Correction: Here, it is about refuting the materialist claim that humans are generated only through biological processes, by pointing to Adam's direct creation without those stages.

Third Sub-Interpretation: Refuting the Philosophers
Razi explains the hadith refutes philosophers who claim creation requires time and celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses a hadith to refute the philosophical claim that human creation requires intermediaries and time.

Core Idea
Direct creation refutes philosophical intermediaries.

Imam Razi presents the third sub-interpretation of the hadith, targeting the philosophers who argued that human formation inherently requires long periods and mediation by celestial spheres and elements. The hadith's statement that God created Adam 'upon His image' is interpreted to mean direct creation, thereby refuting the necessitarian causality of the philosophers. This lesson examines that key argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the philosophical claim, then the hadith quote, then the interpretation.
2
Understand the philosophical view being refuted
Philosophers claimed that humans come into existence only after a long time through the mediation of celestial spheres and elements. Identify this view in the passage.
3
Analyze Razi's use of the hadith
Razi interprets the hadith phrase 'upon His image' as meaning 'without these intermediaries'. This directly counters the philosophical position. Consider why this is a powerful refutation.
The hadith affirms direct creation, denying any necessary mediated causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الإنسان لا يتكون إلا فى مدة طويلة ، وزمان مديد ، بواسطة الأفلاك والعناصر . فقال عليه السلام : « إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي من غير هذه الوسائط . والمقصود منه : الرد على الفلاسفة.
Third: Man is not formed except in a long period and an extended time, through the intermediary of the celestial spheres and elements. So he, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed, God created Adam upon his image,' i.e., without these intermediaries. And the intended purpose is refutation of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the third sub-interpretation, which addresses the philosophers (falasifa) who believed that human beings come into existence only after a long duration and through the mediation of celestial spheres (aflak) and elements (anasir). The hadith, by stating that God created Adam ‘upon his image,’ means that God created him directly without these intermediaries. Thus, the hadith refutes the philosophical doctrine of emanation and natural causation.

Image Means Attributes: Fifth Interpretation
Razi argues 'image' means 'attributes' in the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the word 'image' (sura) in the hadith as referring to Adam's complete attributes rather than a physical form.

Core Idea
The 'image' in the hadith points to Adam's perfect attributes, not a physical shape.

Imam Razi offers a fifth interpretation of the problematic hadith about God creating Adam 'upon His image.' Instead of literal anthropomorphism, he explains that 'image' here idiomatically means 'attribute' or 'description.' This lesson explores how linguistic usage and Adam's unique perfection support this reading, safeguarding divine transcendence while honoring the Prophet's words.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting how Razi uses linguistic examples to establish his interpretation.
2
Understand the linguistic point
Razi cites common phrases like 'I explained to him the image of this event' where 'image' means 'description.' Recognize this idiomatic usage.
This is key to the interpretation.
3
Connect to Adam's creation
See how Razi applies this to the hadith: 'upon his image' means 'upon the totality of his attributes and states.'
4
Grasp the perfection argument
Razi contrasts normal human development (ignorance to knowledge) with Adam's immediate perfection, showing why the hadith emphasizes Adam's special status.
This resolves any problematic reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قد تذكر الصورة ويراد بها الصفة . يقال : شرحت له صورة هذه الواقعة ، وذكرت له صورة هذه المسألة . والمراد من الصورة فى كل هذه المواضع : الصفة . فقوله عليه السلام : إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي على جملة صفاته وأحواله . وذلك لأن الإنسان حين يحدث ، يكون في غاية الجهل والعجز . ثم لا يزال يزداد علمه وقدرته ، إلى أن يصل إلى حد الكمال . فبين النبي أن آدم خلق من أول الأمر كاملا
Fifth: Sometimes 'image' (sura) is mentioned and by it is meant 'attribute' (sifa). One says: 'I explained to him the image of this event' and 'I mentioned to him the image of this issue.' And what is meant by 'image' in all these contexts is the attribute. So his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed God created Adam upon his image' means upon the totality of his attributes and states. This is because when a human comes into existence, he is in the utmost ignorance and incapacity. Then he continues increasing in knowledge and power until he reaches the limit of perfection. So the Prophet made clear that Adam was created from the very beginning perfect.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth sub-interpretation, which takes the word 'image' (sura) to mean 'attribute' (sifa). Razi gives linguistic evidence: one says 'I explained to him the image of this event' meaning the description or account. So in the hadith, 'upon his image' means upon the totality of Adam's attributes and states. Razi then explains that normally humans begin in ignorance and weakness and gradually increase in knowledge and power until they reach perfection. But the Prophet indicates that Adam was created perfect from the start, possessing full knowledge and power. Thus, the hadith is about Adam's original perfection, not a physical shape.

Key Terms
صورة
صفة
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the hadith implies God has a physical form or that Adam was created in God's literal image.
Correction: Razi shows that 'image' here idiomatically means 'attributes,' so the hadith refers to Adam's perfect attributes, not a physical likeness to God.

Checkpoint

A) Physical shape
B) Attribute (sifa)
C) Model
D) Spirit

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'God created Adam upon His image' means Adam was created with perfect attributes, as the word 'image' here denotes 'description' or 'attribute.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This interpretation redefines 'image' as 'attribute'. In what areas of your life have you mistaken outer forms for inner qualities? How can you shift your focus to attributes rather than appearances, especially in your understanding of God?

Refuting the Materialists: Adam's Direct Creation
Imam Razi presents the second interpretation refuting materialist views on human generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second interpretation of the hadith refutes materialist claims that human generation depends solely on biological processes.

Core Idea
Adam's direct creation refutes the idea that humans arise only from sperm and blood.

Imam Razi now presents a second interpretation of the hadith 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image.' This interpretation is directed against the Dahriyya (materialists) who argue that human reproduction is purely natural. The hadith affirms that Adam was created directly, without the usual biological precursors, thereby undermining their entire framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation

Pay attention to the concise style of Imam Razi.

Identify the materialist claim

Adam is a counterexample to their claim.
4

Consider why this refutation is important for the doctrine of divine transcendence and the ability of Allah to create directly.
This reinforces tawhid and Allah's absolute power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى : المراد منه : إبطال قول الدهرية . الذين يقولون : «إن الإنسان لا يتولد إلا بواسطة النطفة ، ودم الطمث » فقال عليه السلام : إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، ابتداء من غير تقدم نطفة وعلقة ومضغة

The hadith negates this by stating that Adam was created directly by Allah without these precursors such as sperm-drop (nutfa), clot ('alaqa), and chewed lump (mudgha). Thus, it affirms divine creation ex nihilo and refutes naturalistic accounts of human origin.

B) To refute the materialists who deny direct creation
C) To explain that Adam was created in God's physical image
D) To discuss the medical process of human generation

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation of the hadith refutes materialism by affirming Adam's direct creation without biological precursors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What area of your life are you treating as if it must follow natural processes, forgetting that Allah can create from nothing? Will you trust Him for a breakthrough that defies your expectations starting today?
?
The materialists of the past claimed that human life only comes from sperm and blood. What 'materialist' assumptions do you hold about your own heart—that it can only change through certain methods or circumstances? Do you believe Allah can directly transform you without any prior steps? Commit to one specific way you will rely on His direct power this week.
?
Adam was created without any prior cause. Does your daily trust reflect that same belief in Allah's absolute creative power, or do you secretly think that material causes are necessary? When you face an obstacle, do you first look to material means, or do you first turn to Allah who creates from nothing? Pledge a small act of tawakkul that bypasses your usual material thinking.

Refuting the Philosophers on Creation
Imam Razi gives the third interpretation refuting philosophers who require lengthy cosmic processes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'Allah created Adam upon His image' is interpreted by Imam Razi to refute the philosophical view that human formation requires lengthy cosmic mediation.

Core Idea
Adam's direct creation without intermediaries refutes naturalistic causation.

Imam Razi offers a third interpretation of the well-known hadith, one that directly challenges the philosophers of his time. They held that human beings can only arise after immense cosmic cycles involving celestial spheres and elements. The Prophet's words, however, affirm that Adam was created directly by Allah, without such intermediaries, serving as a powerful theological counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophical view
The philosophers claim that human formation takes a long time and requires the mediation of celestial spheres and elements. Recognize this as a naturalistic explanation that diminishes direct divine action.
The term 'philosophers' here refers to those influenced by Greek thought.
2
Read the hadith and its interpretation
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the hadith is directly invoked to counter the philosophical position: 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image' is explained as 'without these intermediaries'.
3
Appreciate the refutation
The refutation is not a scientific argument but a theological one: Allah's power is not bound by natural processes. This interpretation upholds divine transcendence and the possibility of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الإنسان لا يتكون إلا فى مدة طويلة ، وزمان مديد ، بواسطة الأفلاك والعناصر . فقال عليه السلام : « إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي من غير هذه الوسائط . والمقصود منه : الرد على الفلاسفة
The third: that man is not formed except in a long period and extended time, by means of the celestial spheres and elements. So he (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image,' i.e., without these intermediaries. And its intended meaning is refutation of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage explains the third interpretation of the hadith, which is to refute the philosophers (falasifa) who claim that human formation requires long periods and the mediation of celestial spheres and elements. The hadith states that Allah created Adam directly without these intermediaries, thus refuting the philosophical view.

Correction: The hadith shows that Allah created Adam directly without any such intermediaries, affirming that divine power is not bound by natural laws.

The Fifth Interpretation: 'Image' as 'Attributes'
Imam Razi interprets 'image' as 'attributes', explaining Adam's created perfection and destiny.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith 'Allah created Adam upon His image' as referring to attributes and states, not physical likeness.

Core Idea
'Image' in the hadith means attributes—Adam was created perfect in knowledge, power, and destiny.

Imam Razi presents a fifth interpretation of the challenging hadith about 'image'. He argues that 'image' (sura) here means 'attribute' (sifa), drawing on linguistic usage. This lesson explores that interpretation and its implications for understanding Adam's creation and divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument as a whole. Let the author's own words establish the interpretation.
Pay attention to the linguistic examples Razi gives.
2
Understand the linguistic evidence
Note how Razi supports 'image' meaning 'attribute' through common Arabic phrases like 'the image of the event'.
This is a key step in his reasoning.
3
Connect to Adam's perfection
See that the hadith means Adam was created complete in knowledge and power from the start, unlike other humans who develop gradually.
This contrasts human weakness with Adam's initial perfection.
4
Consider the extension to destiny
Notice that Razi extends the interpretation to include felicity and misery, linking to predestination via another hadith.
This shows the breadth of 'image' as encompassing all attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قد تذكر الصورة ويراد بها الصفة . يقال : شرحت له صورة هذه الواقعة ، وذكرت له صورة هذه المسألة . والمراد من الصورة فى كل هذه المواضع : الصفة . فقوله عليه السلام : إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي على جملة صفاته وأحواله . وذلك لأن الإنسان حين يحدث ، يكون في غاية الجهل والعجز . ثم لا يزال يزداد علمه وقدرته ، إلى أن يصل إلى حد الكمال . فبين النبي أن آدم خلق من أول الأمر كاملا قائما في علمه وقدرته . وقوله : « خلق الله آدم على صورته ، معناه : أنه خلقه في أول الأمر على صفته ، التي كانت حاصلة له في آخر الأمر . وأيضا : لا يبعد أن يدخل في لفظة الصورة ، كونه سعيداً أو شقيا . كما قال عليه السلام : ( السعيد من سعد في بطن أمه ، والشقى من شقى في بطن أمه )، فقوله عليه السلام : « إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي على جميع صفاته من كونه سعيدا أو عارفا أو تائبا أو مقبولا من عند الله تعالى
The fifth: sometimes 'image' is mentioned and what is meant is 'attribute'. It is said: I explained to him the image of this event, and I mentioned to him the image of this issue. And what is meant by 'image' in all these places is 'attribute'. So his statement (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image,' i.e., upon the totality of his attributes and states. And that is because when man is generated, he is in extreme ignorance and incapacity. Then he continues to increase in knowledge and power until he reaches the limit of perfection. So the Prophet explained that Adam was created from the first beginning complete and established in his knowledge and power. And his statement: 'Allah created Adam upon His image' means that He created him in the first beginning upon His attribute which was later obtained by him at the end. Also, it is not far that the word 'image' includes whether he is fortunate or unfortunate. As he (peace be upon him) said: 'The fortunate is fortunate in his mother's womb, and the unfortunate is unfortunate in his mother's womb.' So his statement (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image' means upon all his attributes, whether he is fortunate, knowledgeable, repentant, or accepted by Allah Most High.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth interpretation, where 'image' (sura) is taken to mean 'attribute' (sifa). The author supports this with linguistic usage: one can say 'I explained the image of the event,' meaning its description. Thus, the hadith means that Adam was created upon the totality of his attributes and states. The author explains that man is initially born ignorant and weak, then gradually acquires knowledge and power until reaching perfection. The hadith indicates that Adam was created perfect from the start in knowledge and power. Additionally, the word 'image' can include his predetermined destiny of happiness or misery, citing the hadith about the fetus's fate. So Adam was created with his attributes, including being fortunate or otherwise.

Key Terms
الصورة
الصفة
الكمال
العلم
القدرة
السعادة
الشقاوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about 'image' implies that Allah has a physical form similar to Adam.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'image' here means 'attributes', not physical likeness, thus preserving divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth interpretation, what does 'image' (sura) in the hadith refer to?
A) Physical appearance
B) Attributes and states
C) The shape of the body
D) The soul

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's interpretation of 'image' as 'attributes' safeguards divine transcendence while affirming that Adam was created perfect in knowledge, power, and predetermined fate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own slow journey of acquiring knowledge and power, do you feel impatient with your limitations, or does Adam's immediate perfection inspire you to trust that Allah has placed you in a state of growth for a reason? How can you embrace gradual learning as part of your divine destiny?
?
The hadith includes that even your ultimate happiness or misery was predetermined in the womb. Does this thought bring you fear or surrender? In what area of your life do you need to actively submit to Allah's decree rather than resist it?
?
If 'image' means attributes, and Adam was created 'upon His image', what does that imply about the attributes you possess as a human? Are you using your knowledge and power in ways that fulfill the purpose for which you were created, or are you neglecting them?

First Interpretation: Image as Attribute and the Objection Answered
Razi presents the first interpretation and answers the objection of shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets 'image' as attribute in the hadith and how he defends this interpretation against the objection that it implies sharing in divinity.

Core Idea
Sharing remote attributes does not mean sharing divinity.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the first interpretation of the hadith when the pronoun refers to God. He explains that 'image' means attribute, referring to Adam's intellectual and practical abilities. Then he anticipates a serious objection: doesn't this make humans share God's attributes? His answer is subtle and crucial for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the first interpretation
Read from 'الطريق الثالث' up to 'بناء على هذا التأويل' and note how Razi defines 'image' as attribute.
Pay attention to the attributes listed: knowledge of intelligibles and ability to deduce crafts.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection starting with 'فإن قيل' and the reply beginning with 'قلنا'. Grasp why someone might think sharing attributes implies partnership in divinity.
The objection is a standard one in kalam.
3
Analyze the reply
Razi distinguishes between essential attributes and remote concomitants. Notice the Quranic and hadith evidence he cites.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this argument protects tawhid while allowing for human dignity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : ( أن يكون ذلك الضمير عائدا إلى الله تعالى ) وفيه وجوه : الأول : المراد من الصورة : الصفة - كما بيناه - فيكون المعنى : أن آدم امتاز عن سائر الأشخاص والأجسام بكونه عالما بالمعقولات، قادرا على استنباط الحرف والصناعات ، وهذه صفات شريفة مناسبة لصفات الله تعالى من بعض الوجوه . فصح قوله عليه السلام : « إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، بناء على هذا التأويل . فإن قيل : المشاركة في صفات الكمال تقتضى المشاركة في الإلهية . قلنا : المشاركة في بعض اللوازم البعيدة مع حصول المخالفة فى الأمور الكثيرة ، لا تقتضى المساواة في الإلهية. ولهذا المعنى ، قال تعالى : ( وله المثل الأعلى (۹) ، وقال عليه السلام : « تخلقوا بأخلاق الله » . الثاني : إنه كما يصح إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف ، فقد صح إضافتها إلى الخالق والموجد . فيكون الغرض من هذه الإضافة : الدلالة على أن هذه الصورة ممتازة عن سائر الصور بمزيد الكرامة والجلالة
The third way: that the pronoun refers to Allah Most High. And it contains aspects: The first: what is meant by 'image' is 'attribute' – as we explained – so the meaning is that Adam was distinguished from other persons and bodies by being knowledgeable of intelligibles, able to deduce crafts and industries, and these are noble attributes appropriate to the attributes of Allah Most High in some respects. So his statement (peace be upon him) 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image' is correct based on this interpretation. If it is said: Participation in attributes of perfection necessitates participation in divinity. We say: Participation in some remote concomitants while having difference in many matters does not necessitate equality in divinity. For this meaning, Allah Most High said: 'And His is the highest attribute' (30:27), and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Adopt the character traits of Allah.' The second: Just as it is correct to attribute the attribute to the described, it is also correct to attribute it to the Creator and Originator. So the purpose of this attribution is to indicate that this image is distinguished from other images by additional honor and majesty.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the third way of interpreting the hadith, where the pronoun 'His' in 'upon His image' is taken to refer to Allah. The author gives two aspects. In the first, 'image' means 'attribute' again, but here it signifies that Adam is distinguished by intellectual knowledge and ability to deduce crafts and industries, which are noble attributes somewhat similar to God's attributes. The author then anticipates an objection: sharing attributes of perfection implies sharing divinity. The reply is that sharing some remote concomitants (lawazim ba'ida) while differing in many essential matters does not imply equality in divinity. This is supported by Qur'anic verse (30:27) and the hadith 'takhallaqu bi akhlaq Allah' (adopt the character traits of Allah). The second aspect adds that attributing the image to God indicates honor and majesty, not a literal sharing.

Key Terms
الصفة
المشاركة
الإلهية
اللوازم البعيدة
المثل الأعلى
التخلق بأخلاق الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans share any attribute with God, they become partners in divinity.
Correction: Razi clarifies that sharing remote concomitants while differing in essential matters does not imply equality in divinity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does sharing some attributes with God not imply partnership in divinity?
A) Because the attributes are metaphorical
B) Because the shared attributes are remote concomitants and there are many differences
C) Because Adam was a prophet
D) Because the hadith is weak

Key Takeaway
Sharing remote attributes with God does not compromise tawhid; it highlights human dignity without equating to divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your intelligence or skill made you self-sufficient or proud, forgetting that it is merely a remote reflection of Allah's attribute of knowledge? How can you transform that pride into humble gratitude and a sense of responsibility to use those gifts for His sake?

When you look at your own abilities—whether in your career, studies, or relationships—do you see them as signs of Allah's generosity or as reasons to elevate yourself? What would it genuinely mean for you to 'adopt Allah's attributes' (takhallaqu bi akhlaq Allah) in your daily interactions, without crossing into self-deification?
?
This lesson draws a fine line between human dignity and shirk. Has there been a moment in your life where you unintentionally placed someone—or yourself—in a position that rivaled Allah's uniqueness? What concrete step can you take today to bring your heart back to pure tawhid?

Second Interpretation: Attribution to Creator Indicates Honor
Razi explains that attribution to God indicates honor and majesty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation where the image is attributed to God to indicate the special honor and majesty of Adam's form.

Core Idea
Attributing the image to God elevates Adam's status, not God's essence.

Imam Razi offers a second aspect in this interpretation: the pronoun 'His' in the hadith refers to God, but not to indicate that Adam's image is like God's. Rather, the attribution means that this particular image – Adam's – is given special honor and majesty because it is created directly by God and distinguished from all other forms. This interpretation avoids any literal anthropomorphism.

2
Grasp the purpose of attribution
Razi says the purpose is to indicate distinction and honor. Think about why this avoids anthropomorphism.
3
Connect to the overall argument
See how this second interpretation complements the first, providing another way to understand the hadith without compromising transcendence.

The second: Just as it is correct to attribute the attribute to the described, it is also correct to attribute it to the Creator and Originator. So the purpose of this attribution is to indicate that this image is distinguished from other images by additional honor and majesty.

Image as Attribute: A Kalam Argument
Imam Razi presents the first interpretation: 'image' means attribute, and refutes the objection of shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets 'image' in the hadith as attribute, and how he defends against the charge that sharing attributes implies sharing divinity.

Core Idea
Sharing a distant attribute does not make one equal to God.

Imam Razi continues the third approach to the pronoun reference. Here he explains that 'image' means attribute, so Adam's distinctive qualities are noble attributes resembling God's in some ways, but that does not imply equality. An objection and its answer clarify the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic thoroughly, then the translation. Note the flow: interpretation first, then objection and answer.
2
Understand the interpretation
Imam Razi says 'image' means 'attribute'. Adam's intellect and skill are noble attributes that reflect some divine qualities. Why does this interpretation make the hadith sound?
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection assumes sharing attributes implies sharing essence (shirk). Razi answers: sharing some distant attributes (like knowledge) does not require equality in divinity. The Quran and hadith support this distinction.
The key kalām principle: attributes are not the same as essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : ( أن يكون ذلك الضمير عائدا إلى الله تعالى وفيه وجوه : الأول : المراد من الصورة : الصفة - كما بيناه - فيكون المعنى : أن آدم امتاز عن سائر الأشخاص والأجسام بكونه عالما بالمعقولات، قادرا على استنباط الحرف والصناعات ، وهذه صفات شريفة مناسبة لصفات الله تعالى من بعض الوجوه . فصح قوله عليه السلام : « إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، بناء على هذا التأويل . فإن قيل : المشاركة في صفات الكمال تقتضى المشاركة في الإلهية . قلنا : المشاركة في بعض اللوازم البعيدة مع حصول المخالفة فى الأمور الكثيرة ، لا تقتضى المساواة في الإلهية. ولهذا المعنى ، قال تعالى : ( وله المثل الأعلى (۹) ، وقال عليه السلام : « تخلقوا بأخلاق الله
The third path: (that the pronoun refers back to Allah the Exalted) and it has aspects: The first: the intended meaning of 'image' is attribute – as we have explained – so the meaning is that Adam was distinguished from other persons and bodies by being knowledgeable of intelligibles, capable of deducing arts and crafts, and these are noble attributes suitable to the attributes of Allah the Exalted in some ways. So his saying (peace be upon him) is valid: 'Indeed Allah created Adam on His image' based on this interpretation. For if it is said: sharing in attributes of perfection requires sharing in divinity. We say: sharing in some distant concomitants while differing in many things does not require equality in divinity. And for this meaning, He the Exalted said: 'And His is the highest attribute' (30:27), and he (peace be upon him) said: 'Acquire the character of Allah.'
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a kalam argument: it first establishes that 'image' means attribute, so Adam's distinctive knowledge and skill are noble attributes that have some similarity to God's attributes. Then it anticipates an objection: if Adam shares God's attributes, doesn't that imply he is divine? The answer distinguishes between essential attributes and distant attributes; sharing some remote attributes does not make one equal to God. It cites Quran and hadith to support.

Key Terms
الصورة
الصفة
المشاركة
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone shares a quality with God, that makes them divine or equal to God.
Correction: Sharing a distant attribute (like knowledge) does not imply sharing the essence of divinity. Essential attributes are exclusive, but some remote attributes can be shared in a limited sense without implying equality.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi refute the objection that sharing attributes implies sharing divinity?
A) He denies any similarity
B) He says sharing only in distant attributes while differing in many things does not require equality
C) He says attributes are not real
D) He says Adam is divine

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi defends the hadith by interpreting 'image' as attribute and arguing that sharing some remote attributes does not necessitate equality with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God is too distant because He is described as having attributes we cannot fully grasp? How does this lesson's affirmation of a remote resemblance between God's attributes and our own change that feeling? What will you do this week to meditate on one of God's attributes as both transcendent and relatable?
?
When you hear a beautiful quality in another person—like wisdom or mercy—are you quick to attribute it to God or to see it as a gift from Him? Does this lesson's teaching that such qualities are 'distant concomitants' of God's attributes help you avoid the subtle sin of equating someone's goodness with God's? Commit to correcting your internal speech the next time you admire a quality in someone.
?
Read again the hadith 'Acquire the character of Allah.' Do you actively seek to embody God's attributes within your human limitations, or do you hold back out of fear of overstepping? What one attribute of God (e.g., mercy, justice) will you intentionally cultivate this month, knowing it is a distant reflection and not an attempt to become divine?

Imam Razi explains the second aspect: ascription to the Creator indicates honor.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Ascribing the image to God highlights its honor, not His form.

Imam Razi presents a second interpretation of the phrase 'image of God'. He uses an analogy: just as you can attribute a quality to the one who possesses it, you can also attribute it to the one who created it. The purpose is to emphasize the unique dignity of that image, not to suggest God has a form.

3

4

Ghazali's Spiritual Interpretation of the Hadith of the Image
Al-Ghazali interprets the hadith 'created on His image' via soul-body analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ghazali's interpretation that the soul's relationship to the body mirrors God's transcendent relationship to the world, avoiding anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The soul governs the body without being inside it, just as God governs the world without spatial location.

Al-Ghazali offers a profound spiritual interpretation of the hadith 'God created Adam on His image.' He argues that the true human is not the physical body but the soul, which is non-material and manages the body. This mirrors God's transcendent governance of the universe. This lesson unpacks that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the opening rejection of the body as the essence of man.
2
Identify the key claim about the soul
Al-Ghazali states that man is not the physical structure; rather, he is a non-material existent that manages the body. Identify this claim and its phrasing.
The terms 'not a body nor bodily' are central.
3
Understand the analogy
The hadith is interpreted as a comparison: Adam's essence relates to his body as God relates to the world—neither is located in the creation, yet both act upon it. Grasp the force of this analogy.
This interpretation avoids both anthropomorphism and pantheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قال الشيخ الغزالي - رحمه الله -: «ليس الإنسان(١٠) عبارة عن هذه البنية ، بل هو موجود ، ليس بجسم ولا بجسمانى ، ولا تعلق له بهذا البدن ، إلا على سبيل التدبير أو التصرف ، فقوله عليه السلام : «إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي أن نسبة ذات آدم عليه السلام إلى هذا البدن ، كنسبة البارى تعالى إلى العالم، من حيث إن كل واحد منهما، غير حال في هذا الجسم، وإن كان مؤثراً فيه بالتصرف والتدبير . والله أعلم .
The third: Al-Ghazali – may Allah have mercy on him – said: 'Man is not this physical structure, rather he is an existent that is neither a body nor bodily, and he has no attachment to this body except by way of management or disposition. So his saying (peace be upon him): "Indeed Allah created Adam on His image" means that the relationship of the essence of Adam (peace be upon him) to this body is like the relationship of the Creator the Exalted to the world, in that each of them is not indwelling in this body, although he is effective in it by disposition and management. And Allah knows best.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a metaphysical interpretation: the true reality of a human is the soul, which is non-material and governs the body. The hadith means that Adam's relationship to his body mirrors God's relationship to the world – both are transcendent and not located within the creation, yet they act upon it. This avoids anthropomorphism entirely.

Key Terms
الإنسان
البنية
الجسم
التدبير

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'created on His image' implies God has a physical form or that humans share divine essence.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explains that the likeness is in the relationship: as the soul manages the body without being inside it, so God manages the world without spatial indwelling.

Key Takeaway

Al-Ghazali's Analogy on Divine Image
Al-Ghazali interprets the hadith of divine image analogically using soul-body relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's analogical interpretation of the hadith of divine image, which preserves divine transcendence by comparing the soul-body relation to God-world relation.

Core Idea
The prophet's words 'upon His image' refer to management, not physical likeness.

Al-Ghazali tackles a challenging hadith that could be misconstrued as anthropomorphism. He uses a rational analogy between the human soul and the body to explain how God relates to creation. This passage shows how kalam methodology interprets scriptural texts without compromising tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The hadith 'God created Adam upon His image' seems to imply a physical likeness, which contradicts divine transcendence. Recognize why this is a theological challenge.
This is a classic issue in kalam.
2
Grasp al-Ghazali's premise
Al-Ghazali asserts that the true human is a non-corporeal soul that manages the body, not the physical structure itself. Note the key terms: 'not a body nor a corporeal being' (ليس بجسم ولا بجسمانى).
This premise is crucial for the analogy.
3
Apply the analogy
Al-Ghazali compares the soul-body relation to God-world relation: each is not indwelling but effective through management. Thus, 'image' refers to this relational similarity, not physical form.
This preserves God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قال الشيخ الغزالي - رحمه الله -: «ليس الإنسان عبارة عن هذه البنية ، بل هو موجود ، ليس بجسم ولا بجسمانى ، ولا تعلق له بهذا البدن ، إلا على سبيل التدبير أو التصرف ، فقوله عليه السلام : «إن الله خلق آدم على صورته ، أي أن نسبة ذات آدم عليه السلام إلى هذا البدن ، كنسبة البارى تعالى إلى العالم، من حيث إن كل واحد منهما، غير حال في هذا الجسم، وإن كان مؤثراً فيه بالتصرف والتدبير . والله أعلم .
The third: Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Man is not merely this physical structure; rather, he is an entity, not a body nor a corporeal being, and he has no attachment to this body except in the manner of management or disposal. Thus his statement, peace be upon him: "God created Adam upon His image" means that the relation of Adam's essence to this body is like the relation of the Exalted Creator to the world, in that each of them is not indwelling in this body, although it has an effect upon it through disposal and management. And Allah knows best.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Ghazali's interpretation of the prophetic hadith 'God created Adam upon His image.' Al-Ghazali begins by asserting that the human being is not merely the physical body; rather, the true human is a non-corporeal entity (the soul) that is related to the body only as a manager or disposer. He then applies this to the hadith: just as the soul is not inside the body but manages it, so God's relation to the world is not one of in-dwelling but of management. Therefore, the 'image' in the hadith refers to this relational similarity, not to a physical form. This interpretation upholds divine transcendence (tanzih).

Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that the hadith refers to a relational similarity of management (tadbir and tasarruf), not physical likeness.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali interprets the hadith of divine image through the soul-body analogy, showing that 'image' refers to a relational mode of management, not physical resemblance, thus preserving God's transcendence.

Ibn Khuzaymah's Two Interpretations of the Second Hadith
Ibn Khuzaymah gives two metaphorical interpretations for the second hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Khuzaymah interpreted the hadith about Adam being created upon the 'image of al-Rahman' in two figurative ways that preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Ibn Khuzaymah safeguards God's transcendence by interpreting 'image' as attribute or honorific.

This lesson examines a prophetic hadith that, if taken literally, would imply God has a form. The scholar Ibn Khuzaymah offers two figurative interpretations to uphold divine transcendence. Let's explore his approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its context
Read the Arabic passage once, noting that Ibn Khuzaymah considered the narration weak. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Identify the two interpretations: first, 'image' means 'attribute'; second, the phrase is an honorific genitive like 'House of God'.
Both interpretations avoid literal attribution of a form to God.
3
Reflect on the principle of metaphorical interpretation
Consider why Ibn Khuzaymah offered these interpretations: to protect the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih) while accepting the hadith if authentic.
This is a key methodology in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر الثانى : ما رواه ابن خزيمة في كتابه الذي سماه بـ«التوحيد بإسناده عن ابن عمر - رضي الله عنه - عن النبي أنه قال : لا تقبحوا الوجه ، فإن الله خلق آدم على صورة الرحمن ،

واعلم : أن ابن خزيمة ضعف هذه الرواية ، ويقول : إن صحت هذه الرواية ، فلها تأويلان :

الأول : أن يكون المراد من الصورة: الصفة - على ما بيناه -

الثاني : أن يكون المراد من هذه الإضافة : بيان شرف هذه الصورة كما في قوله : بيت الله ، وناقة الله
The second report: What Ibn Khuzaymah narrated in his book which he named 'al-Tawhid' by his chain from Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet that he said: 'Do not revile the face, for indeed God created Adam upon the image of al-Rahman.' Know that Ibn Khuzaymah deemed this narration weak, and he says: If this narration is authentic, then it has two interpretations: First: That what is meant by 'image' is the attribute – as we have explained. Second: That what is meant by this addition is a demonstration of the honor of this image, as in His saying: 'House of God' and 'She-camel of God.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second report narrated by Ibn Khuzaymah in his book 'al-Tawhid' which contains the hadith 'Do not revile the face, for God created Adam upon the image of al-Rahman.' The author notes that Ibn Khuzaymah himself considered this narration weak. Nevertheless, he provides two interpretations for it if it were authentic. The first interpretation takes 'image' (صورة) to mean 'attribute' (صفة). The second interpretation treats the phrase 'image of al-Rahman' as a genitive of honor, similar to expressions like 'House of God' and 'She-camel of God' where the addition indicates special honor, not literal ownership. Thus, the hadith is understood metaphorically, avoiding any literal attribution of a form to God.

Key Terms
الخبر
صورة
الصفة
إضافة تشريف
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'image of al-Rahman' means God literally has an image or form, and Adam was created in that form.
Correction: Ibn Khuzaymah shows that the phrase can be interpreted metaphorically: either 'image' means 'attribute' (صفة) or it is an honorific genitive (إضافة تشريف) like 'House of God'.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ibn Khuzaymah's two interpretations show that problematic hadith can be understood figuratively to protect divine transcendence.
