Two Figurative Meanings of 'Meeting' (al-Liqā')
Razi enumerates two valid figurative interpretations of 'meeting' (al-liqā') that avoid corporealism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'meeting' (al-liqā') when predicated of Allah must be interpreted figuratively, and you will learn the two valid interpretations given by Imam Razi: as vision (ru'ya) and as manifestation of divine authority.

Core Idea
The term 'meeting' in revelation points to Allah's transcendence, not physical contact.

In this passage, Imam Razi confronts a potential misunderstanding: the Quran and hadith speak of 'meeting' Allah, but the literal meaning implies physical proximity. Since Allah is not a body, such literalism is impossible. Razi explains two figurative meanings that preserve both the scriptural language and Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Examine the scriptural evidence
Read the Quranic verses and the hadith that use the word 'meeting' (al-liqā') in relation to Allah. Notice the context: all refer to the Hereafter.
These texts are the starting point for the discussion.
2
Identify the problem of literalism
Opponents argue that 'meeting' is an attribute of bodies because it involves spatial proximity. Understand why a literal reading would conflict with Allah's transcendence (tanzih).
The objection is that if taken literally, it implies Allah is a body.
3
Apply the core premise: Allah is not a body
Razi states that it has been proven by rational proof (dalil) that Allah is not a body. Therefore, the literal sense is impossible, and figurative interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary.
This premise is non-negotiable in Sunni kalam.
4
Learn the two figurative interpretations
First interpretation: meeting means vision (ru'ya) — giving the name of the cause (encounter) to the effect (perception). Second interpretation: meeting means being under divine authority and manifest power on the Day of Judgment.
Both preserve the meaning without corporealism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس فى لفظ اللقاء. أما القرآن فقد قال الله تعالى: «الذين يظنون أنهم ملاقوا ربهم» (البقرة 46)، وقال: «فمن كان يرجو لقاء ربه» (الكهف 120)، وقال: «بل هم بلقاء ربهم كافرون» (السجدة 10). وأما الحديث فقد قال عليه السلام: «من أحب لقاء الله أحب الله لقاءه، ومن كره لقاء الله كره الله لقاءه». قالوا: واللقاء من صفات الأجسام. يقال: التقى الجيشان إذا قرب أحدهما من الآخر في المكان. واعلم: أنه لما ثبت بالدليل أنه تعالى ليس بجسم، وجب حمل هذا اللفظ على أحد وجهين: أحدهما: أن من لقى إنسانا، فقد أدركه وأبصره. فكان المراد من اللقاء هو الرؤية، إطلاقا لاسم السبب على المسبب. والثاني: إن الرجل إذا حضر عند ملك، ولقيه، دخل هناك تحت حكمه وقهره، دخولا لا حيلة له فى دفعه. فكان ذلك اللقاء سببا لظهور قدرة الملك عليه — على هذا الوجه — فلما ظهرت قدرته وقوته وقهره وشدة بأسه في ذلك اليوم، عبر عن تلك الحالة باللقاء. والذي يدل على صحة قولنا: إن أحداً لا يقول بأن الخلائق تتلاقى ذواتهم في ذات الله تعالى على سبيل المماسة. وما بطل حمل اللقاء على المماسة والمجاورة، لم يبق إلا ما ذكرناه.
Chapter Five on the word 'meeting' (al-liqāʾ). As for the Qur'an, Allah Most High said: 'Those who are certain that they will meet their Lord' (al-Baqara 2:46), and said: 'Whoever hopes for the meeting with his Lord' (al-Kahf 18:110), and said: 'Nay, they are disbelievers in the meeting with their Lord' (al-Sajda 32:10). As for the hadith, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever loves to meet Allah, Allah loves to meet him; and whoever hates to meet Allah, Allah hates to meet him.' They said: Meeting (al-liqāʾ) is among the attributes of bodies. It is said: the two armies met, when one approached the other in place. Know that: since it has been established by proof that He Most High is not a body, it is necessary to interpret this expression in one of two ways: The first: that whoever meets a person has perceived him and seen him. Thus by 'meeting' what is meant is vision (al-ruʾya), giving the name of the cause to the effect. The second: that when a man attends before a king and meets him, he enters there under his authority and coercion, an entry in which he has no device to repel it. So that meeting was a cause for the manifestation of the king's power over him — in this respect. So when His power, strength, coercion, and severity of might appeared on that Day, that state was expressed by 'meeting'. And what indicates the correctness of our statement is that no one claims that the created beings meet in their essences in the essence of Allah Most High in the manner of touching (al-mumāssa). And when the interpretation of meeting as touching and adjacency is invalid, nothing remains except what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a hermeneutical argument about the word 'meeting' (al-liqāʾ) in the Qur'an and hadith. Imam Razi begins by citing the scriptural evidence: three Qur'anic verses and one hadith that use the term 'meeting' in relation to Allah. He then presents the problem: opponents argue that 'meeting' is an attribute of bodies, since it is said that two armies 'meet' when they come close to each other in space. Razi then lays down the foundational premise: it has been established by proof that Allah is not a body. Consequently, the word 'meeting' cannot be taken literally when applied to Allah; it must be interpreted figuratively. He provides two valid interpretations. The first interpretation: when someone meets a person, he perceives and sees him; therefore 'meeting' here means 'seeing' (al-ruʾya), which is a metaphorical extension—giving the name of the cause (the physical encounter) to the effect (the act of seeing). The second interpretation: when a subject comes before a king, he falls entirely under the king's authority and compulsion; thus 'meeting' on the Day of Judgment refers to the manifestation of Allah's absolute power and might over His creatures. Razi concludes by stating that no one claims a literal physical meeting (touching or adjacency) of creatures with Allah's essence. Since the literal sense is impossible (taʿṭīl), the only valid meanings are the two figurative interpretations he has given.

Key Terms
لقاء
رؤية
مماسة
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'meeting' (al-liqā') in the Qur'an and hadith implies that Allah has a body or that humans physically touch Him.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that since Allah is proven not to be a body, 'meeting' must be interpreted figuratively either as vision (ru'ya) or as the manifestation of divine authority on the Day of Judgment.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why must the word 'meeting' (al-liqā') when applied to Allah be interpreted figuratively?
A) Because all language about Allah is metaphorical.
B) Because it has been proven that Allah is not a body, so literal meeting (which involves spatial proximity) is impossible.
C) Because the Qur'an itself says so.
D) Because the hadith prohibits taking it literally.

Key Takeaway
When scriptural terms like 'meeting' (al-liqā') appear to imply corporealism, they must be interpreted figuratively in a way that upholds Allah's transcendence, as Imam Razi demonstrates with two valid interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about 'meeting Allah', do you unconsciously picture a physical encounter? How does Razi's argument force you to rewire that image? What specific thought will you replace it with starting today?
?
Which of Razi's two interpretations—meeting as seeing Allah or meeting as experiencing His absolute power—moves you more deeply? Why? And which one do you need to meditate on to strengthen your relationship with Allah?
?
If you have been avoiding the thought of meeting Allah because it felt distant or abstract, how does this lesson make that meeting real and personal? What will you do today to genuinely 'love to meet Allah' as the hadith teaches?

Refutation of Physical Contact in Meeting with God
Razi concludes that meeting God is not by contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the term 'meeting' (al-liqa') cannot be interpreted as physical contact or adjacency when referring to meeting God.

Core Idea
Meeting God is not a physical encounter.

Imam Razi concludes his discussion of the eschatological meeting with God by ruling out any interpretation that involves physical contact. This passage shows how kalam logic eliminates anthropomorphic readings and clarifies that the remaining interpretation is the correct one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage is a concluding argument; the context may come from preceding pages.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi asserts that no one claims that creatures meet God by physical contact. Find this statement in the passage and reflect on why he makes it.
This is a key premise for his argument.
3
Understand the logic of invalidation
Razi argues that once the interpretation of 'meeting' as contact or adjacency is invalidated, only his own interpretation remains. Trace this logical step.
The phrase 'وما بطل حمل اللقاء على المماسة والمجاورة' is crucial.
4
Consider the doxographical claim
Razi states that no one (one) says that creatures meet God by contact. Think about whether this is a claim about consensus or a hypothetical statement.
This may be a rhetorical move to dismiss the interpretation.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this argument upholds divine transcendence (tanzih) and rejects anthropomorphism (tashbih).
This is a classic kalam move.

وشدة بأسه في ذلك اليوم ، عبر عن تلك الحالة باللقاء . والذي يدل على صحة قولنا : إن أحداً لا يقول بأن الخلائق تتلاقى ذواتهم في ذات الله تعالى على سبيل المماسة . وما بطل حمل اللقاء على المماسة والمجاورة، لم يدق إلا ما ذكرناه ) وبالله التوفيق )
And the severity of his assault on that day, he expressed that state as 'meeting' (al-liqa'). And what indicates the correctness of our statement: That no one says that the creatures meet, their essences, in the essence of God Almighty by way of contact (mumassa). And when the interpretation of 'meeting' (al-liqa') as contact (mumassa) and adjacency (mujawara) became invalid, nothing remained except what we mentioned. And with God is success.

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument from a discussion on the term 'al-liqa'' (meeting). The author states that no one claims that the essences of creatures meet with the essence of God by way of physical contact (mumassa). He then asserts that once the interpretation of liqa' as physical contact and adjacency is invalidated, only his own interpretation remains. This is a refutation of anthropomorphic interpretations of eschatological meeting with God.

Key Takeaway

Five Proofs That God Is Not Literal Light
Imam Razi presents five arguments against God being perceptible light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand five rational arguments from Imam Razi demonstrating that the Quranic description of God as 'Light' does not refer to physical light perceptible by sight.

Core Idea
God is called 'Light' metaphorically; He is not literal, perceptible light.

Imam Razi begins by citing the famous 'Light Verse' and a prophetic supplication, then immediately clarifies that God is not the physical light we see. He presents five logical arguments to refute any literal interpretation. Each argument draws on the Quran itself and rational reasoning to affirm divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the five proofs enumerated by Imam Razi.
The proofs are marked 'first', 'second', etc.
2
Identify the core logic of each proof
For each proof, understand the logical structure: what premise does it use, and what conclusion does it draw?
The first proof uses the Quranic phrase 'Light of the heavens and earth' rather than just 'Light'.
3
Connect the proofs to Quranic verses
Each proof references a specific Quranic verse. Identify each verse and see how it supports the argument.
For example, the fourth proof uses 'the likeness of His light' and 'guides to His light'.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: Why is it important to affirm that God is not physical light? How does this protect divine transcendence?
This guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس فى لفظ النور قال الله تعالى : «الله نور السموات والأرض. مثل نوره : كمشكاة» (1) وروى ابن خزيمة فى كتابه عن طاووس عن ابن عباس رضى الله عنه : أن النبي ما كان يقول في دعائه : « اللهم لك الحمد . أنت نور السموات والأرض، ومن فيهن . فلك الحمد . أنت قيوم السموات والأرض ومن فيهن » واعلم : أنه لا يصح القول بأنه تعالى هو هذا النور المحسوس بالبصر . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى لم يقل إنه نور ، بل قال : إنه نور السموات والأرض، ولو كان نوراً فى ذاته ، لم يكن لهذه الإضافة فائدة . الثاني: لو كان كونه تعالى نور السموات والأرض ، بمعنى: الضوء المحسوس، لوجب أن لا يكون في شيء من السموات والأرض، ظلمة البتة . لأنه تعالى دائم لا يزال ولا يزول . الثالث : لوكان تعالى نوراً بمعنى الضوء ، لوجب أن يكون ذلك الضوء مغنياً عن ضوء الشمس والقمر والنار . والحس دال على خلاف ذلك) (٢) الرابع : إنه تعالى أزال هذه الشبهة ، بقوله تعالى: مثل نوره ، فقد أضاف النور إلى نفسه ، ولو كان تعالى نفس النور ، لا متنعت هذه الإضافة . لأن إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه ممتنعة وذاته وكذلك قوله تعالى : ( يهدى الله لنوره من يشاء) (۳) الخامس : إنه تعالى قال : « وجعل الظلمات والنور » (٤) فتبين بهذا: أنه تعالى خالق الأنوار .
Chapter Six on the word 'Light' (al-Nur). God Almighty said: 'God is the Light of the heavens and the earth. The likeness of His light is as a niche' (1). And Ibn Khuzayma narrated in his book from Tawus from Ibn Abbas, may God be pleased with them, that the Prophet used to say in his supplication: 'O God, to You be praise. You are the Light of the heavens and the earth and those in them. To You be praise. You are the Sustainer of the heavens and the earth and those in them.' And know: It is not correct to say that He Almighty is this light perceptible by sight. And evidence for this are several aspects: First: He Almighty did not say 'He is light', but said 'He is the Light of the heavens and the earth'. And if He were light in His essence, this addition would have no benefit. Second: If His being the Light of the heavens and the earth meant the perceptible light, then it would be necessary that there be no darkness at all in anything of the heavens and the earth, because He is eternal, ceaseless and not perishing. Third: If He Almighty were light in the sense of light, then it would be necessary that that light dispense with the light of the sun, moon, and fire. But sensory perception indicates the contrary. Fourth: He Almighty removed this ambiguity by His saying: 'The likeness of His light', for He added light to Himself. And if He Almighty were identical to light, this addition would be impossible, because adding a thing to itself is impossible. And also His saying: 'God guides to His light whom He wills' (3). Fifth: He Almighty said: 'And He made the darkness and the light' (4). So it is clarified by this that He Almighty is the Creator of lights.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the word 'nur' (light). The author begins by citing the Quranic verse (24:35) and a hadith from Ibn Abbas. He then states that it is not correct to say that God is the perceptible light. He provides five proofs: First, God said 'Light of the heavens and earth', not 'light' — the addition of 'of the heavens and earth' shows He is not identical to light. Second, if God were physical light, there would be no darkness anywhere, but darkness exists. Third, if God were physical light, He would replace all other lights, but they are still needed. Fourth, the verse says 'the likeness of His light', which adds light to God, so He is not identical to it. Fifth, God created darkness and light, so He is the Creator, not the created light. Each proof refutes literalism and affirms divine transcendence.

Key Terms
النور
الضوء المحسوس
المشكاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God being called 'Light' in the Quran means He is literal light perceptible by sight.
Correction: God is not literal light; He is the source and creator of light, and the term is metaphorical for guidance and radiance.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the phrase 'the likeness of His light' to argue that God is not identical to light?
A) The first proof
B) The second proof
C) The fourth proof
D) The fifth proof

Key Takeaway
God is not literal light; He is the Creator of light and metaphorically guides creation.

Will you commit to stopping at each attribute and asking 'What does the Qur'an actually say?'
?
If you truly internalized that Allah is not like anything you can perceive, how would that change your focus during prayer? Is there a specific moment in your day when you catch yourself visualizing Allah in a finite form? What concrete step will you take to redirect that thought?

Refuting Literal Light: Proofs 1-2
Razi opens the chapter and gives first two proofs against sensory light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two proofs why Allah cannot be literal sensory light: the unnecessary addition and the impossibility of darkness.

Core Idea
If Allah were sensory light, darkness would never exist.

Imam Razi begins by quoting the Quranic verse and a hadith, then declares that Allah is not sensory light. He presents seven proofs; this lesson covers the first two: the logical issue of 'light of the heavens and earth' and the consequence of no darkness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the phrase 'لا يصح القول' indicates a refutation.
2
Focus on proof 1
Identify why the addition 'of the heavens and earth' would be pointless if Allah were light in essence.
This shows linguistic reasoning rooted in Arabic grammar.
3
Focus on proof 2
Understand the logical consequence: an eternal sensory light would eliminate all darkness, which is false.
This uses the attribute of eternity to refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في لفظ النور
قال الله تعالى: «الله نور السموات والأرض. مثل نوره: كمشكاة» وروى ابن خزيمة في كتابه عن طاووس عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقول في دعائه: «اللهم لك الحمد. أنت نور السموات والأرض، ومن فيهن. فلك الحمد. أنت قيوم السموات والأرض ومن فيهن». واعلم: أنه لا يصح القول بأنه تعالى هو هذا النور المحسوس بالبصر. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: إنه تعالى لم يقل إنه نور، بل قال إنه «نور السموات والأرض»، ولو كان نوراً في ذاته، لم يكن لهذه الإضافة فائدة. الثاني: لو كان كونه تعالى نور السموات والأرض، بمعنى: الضوء المحسوس، لوجب أن لا يكون في شيء من السموات والأرض، ظلمة البتة. لأنه تعالى دائم لا يزال ولا يزول. الثالث: لو كان تعالى نوراً بمعنى الضوء، لوجب أن يكون ذلك الضوء مغنياً عن ضوء الشمس والقمر والنار. والحس دال على خلاف ذلك. الرابع: إنه تعالى أزال هذه الشبهة بقوله تعالى: «مثل نوره»، فقد أضاف النور إلى نفسه، ولو كان تعالى نفس النور، لامتنعت هذه الإضافة. لأن إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه ممتنعة. وكذلك قوله تعالى: «يهدي الله لنوره من يشاء». الخامس: إنه تعالى قال: «وجعل الظلمات والنور»، فتبين بهذا: أنه تعالى خالق الأنوار. السادس: إن النور يزول بالظلمة، ولو كان تعالى عين هذا النور المحسوس لكان قابلاً للعدم. وذلك يقدح في كونه قديماً واجب الوجود. السابع: إن الأجسام كلها متماثلة - على ما سبق تقريره - ثم إنها بعد تساويها في الماهية، تراها مختلفة في النور والظلمة، فوجب أن يكون الضوء عرضاً قائماً بالأجسام، والعرض يمتنع أن يكون إلهاً. فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أنه لا يمكن حمل النور على ما ذكروه. بل معناه: أنه هادي أهل السموات والأرض، أو معناه: منور السموات والأرض على الوجه الأحسن، والتدبير الأكمل. كما يقال: فلان نور هذه البلدة. إذا كان سبباً لصلاحها. وقد قرأ بعضهم: «الله نور السموات والأرض». وبالله التوفيق.
The sixth chapter on the word 'light'. Allah the Exalted said: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth. The likeness of His light is as a niche.' And Ibn Khuzaymah narrated in his book from Tawus from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to say in his supplication: 'O Allah, to You be praise. You are the light of the heavens and the earth and whoever is in them. To You be praise. You are the Self-Subsisting of the heavens and the earth and whoever is in them.' Know that it is not valid to say that He, the Exalted, is this light perceived by sight. Evidence for this is [through] aspects: First: He the Exalted did not say that He is light, but rather said that He is 'the light of the heavens and the earth.' If He were light in His essence, this addition [of 'of the heavens and the earth'] would have no benefit. Second: If His being the light of the heavens and the earth meant sensory light, then necessarily there would be no darkness at all in anything of the heavens and the earth, because He the Exalted is perpetual, not ceasing and not vanishing. Third: If He the Exalted were light in the sense of brightness, then that light would necessarily suffice [us] from the light of the sun, moon, and fire. But sensory experience indicates the contrary. Fourth: He the Exalted removed this doubt by His saying 'the likeness of His light', for He added light to Himself. If He the Exalted were the very light, this addition would be impossible, because adding a thing to itself is impossible. Likewise His saying 'Allah guides to His light whom He wills.' Fifth: He the Exalted said 'and He made darkness and light,' so it is shown by this that He the Exalted is the Creator of lights. Sixth: Light is removed by darkness, and if He the Exalted were identical to this sensory light, He would be subject to non-existence, and that detracts from His being eternal, necessary of existence. Seventh: All bodies are similar—according to what has been established previously—then after their equality in quiddity, you see them differing in light and darkness, so it is necessary that brightness be an accident subsisting in bodies, and an accident cannot be a god. So it is established by these aspects that it is impossible to interpret 'light' as they have done. Rather, its meaning is that He is the Guide of the people of the heavens and the earth, or its meaning is that He is the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth in the best way and the most perfect management. Just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the light of this town,' if he is the cause of its rectification. And some recited: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah is literally sensory light. Imam Razi begins by quoting the Quranic verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' and a prophetic supplication. He then declares that it is invalid to say Allah is this perceived light, and presents seven proofs (وجوه) to demonstrate this. The first proof argues that the addition 'of the heavens and the earth' would be meaningless if Allah were light in His essence, since a thing is not added to itself. The second proof argues that if Allah were sensory light, darkness would never exist anywhere, because Allah is eternal and unchanging. The third proof argues that such light would suffice for all illumination, contradicting our experience of needing sun, moon, and fire. The fourth proof points to the phrase 'the likeness of His light,' which adds light to Allah, impossible if He were identical to it. The fifth proof cites the verse 'He made darkness and light,' showing that light is created, so Allah cannot be it. The sixth proof notes that light is subject to removal by darkness, which would imply possibility of non-existence, contradicting Allah's necessary and eternal existence. The seventh proof argues that bodies are similar in essence but differ in light and darkness, so light must be an accident (عرض) subsisting in bodies, and an accident cannot be divine. After these proofs, Razi concludes that the true meaning of 'light' for Allah is either that He is the Guide (هادي) of the heavens and earth or that He illuminates them in the best manner, similar to metaphorical usage in Arabic. This passage guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih) by affirming Allah's transcendence.

Key Terms
النور
المحسوس
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is literal physical light.
Correction: Allah is not literal light; the phrase 'light of the heavens and earth' is metaphorical, indicating guidance or illumination in a non-physical sense.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, why is the addition 'of the heavens and the earth' problematic if Allah were literal light?
A) Because it would imply Allah is multiple lights.
B) Because a thing cannot be added to itself.
C) Because the heavens and earth are not light.
D) Because the addition is redundant in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
Allah is not sensory light because the linguistic form and logical consequences rule it out.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth', do you ever catch yourself forming a mental image of a radiant light? How will you actively reject that image and affirm that Allah is beyond all physical attributes, even as you use the word 'light'?
?
The second proof shows that if Allah were sensory light, darkness could never exist – yet you experience darkness daily. Does your faith in Allah's transcendence truly permeate your moments of darkness (ignorance, hardship, sin), or do you still think of Him as limited by what you can see and feel?
?
The addition 'of the heavens and earth' strips away any claim that Allah is 'light' in His essence. Do you sometimes add human-like qualities to Allah in your worship, imagining He hears like you hear or sees like you see? What concrete step will you take today to purify your belief in His absolute otherness?

Refuting Literal Light: Proofs 3-4
Razi gives the third and fourth proofs against sensory light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand proofs 3 and 4: that sensory light would make other lights superfluous, and that the phrase 'like His light' shows Allah is not light itself.

Core Idea
If Allah were light, we would not need sun, moon, or fire.

Imam Razi continues his refutation with two more proofs. The third proof uses empirical observation: if Allah were literal light, it would suffice for all illumination, but we need other lights. The fourth proof turns to the Quranic phrase 'the likeness of His light', which adds light to Allah, impossible if He were identical to it.

Guided Path
1
Read proofs 3 and 4
Read the Arabic and translation for these two proofs, noticing how each targets a different angle.
Proof 3 is empirical, proof 4 is linguistic.
2
Analyze proof 3
Consider the logical claim: if Allah were sensory light, it would be sufficient and other lights would be unnecessary. Since they are needed, the premise fails.
This uses the principle of sufficient reason.
3
Analyze proof 4
Examine the phrase 'مثل نوره' (the likeness of His light). Adding 'like' or 'His' shows light is not identical to Allah.
Also note the verse 'يهدي الله لنوره من يشاء' similarly adds light to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting Literal Light: Proofs 5-7 and Conclusion
Razi gives proofs 5-7 and concludes the chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand proofs 5-7: light is created, perishable, and an accident, so cannot be Allah; and the correct metaphorical meaning.

Core Idea
Light is created and perishable, but Allah is eternal and necessary.

Imam Razi gives three final proofs and then concludes. The fifth proof shows that Allah created light, so He cannot be it. The sixth shows light can vanish, so it cannot be eternal. The seventh shows light is an accident in bodies, not a god. Then Razi gives the true metaphorical interpretation: light means Guide or Illuminator.

Guided Path
1
Read proofs 5,6,7
Read the Arabic and translation for these three proofs, noting they focus on creation, perishability, and category.
These arguments rely on kalam principles about accidents and necessity.
2
Understand proof 5
The verse 'وجعل الظلمات والنور' shows that light is created (جعل), so Allah cannot be created light.
3
Understand proof 6
Light disappears with darkness, so if Allah were light, He could cease to exist, contradicting His necessary existence.
4
Understand proof 7
Bodies are similar yet differ in light/darkness, proving light is an accident (عرض), and an accident cannot be a god.
5
Read the conclusion
Razi concludes that the true meaning is that Allah is the Guide (هادي) or the Illuminator metaphorically.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Fifth: He the Exalted said 'and He made darkness and light,' so it is shown by this that He the Exalted is the Creator of lights. Sixth: Light is removed by darkness, and if He the Exalted were identical to this sensory light, He would be subject to non-existence, and that detracts from His being eternal, necessary of existence. Seventh: All bodies are similar—according to what has been established previously—then after their equality in quiddity, you see them differing in light and darkness, so it is necessary that brightness be an accident subsisting in bodies, and an accident cannot be a god. So it is established by these aspects that it is impossible to interpret 'light' as they have done. Rather, its meaning is that He is the Guide of the people of the heavens and the earth, or its meaning is that He is the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth in the best way and the most perfect management. Just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the light of this town,' if he is the cause of its rectification. And some recited: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah is literally sensory light. Imam Razi begins by quoting the Quranic verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' and a prophetic supplication. He then declares that it is invalid to say Allah is this perceived light, and presents seven proofs (وجوه) to demonstrate this. The first proof argues that the addition 'of the heavens and the earth' would be meaningless if Allah were light in His essence, since a thing is not added to itself. The second proof argues that if Allah were sensory light, darkness would never exist anywhere, because Allah is eternal and unchanging. The third proof argues that such light would suffice for all illumination, contradicting our experience of needing sun, moon, and fire. The fourth proof points to the phrase 'the likeness of His light,' which adds light to Allah, impossible if He were identical to it. The fifth proof cites the verse 'He made darkness and light,' showing that light is created, so Allah cannot be it. The sixth proof notes that light is subject to removal by darkness, which would imply possibility of non-existence, contradicting Allah's necessary and eternal existence. The seventh proof argues that bodies are similar in essence but differ in light and darkness, so light must be an accident (عرض) subsisting in bodies, and an accident cannot be divine. After these proofs, Razi concludes that the true meaning of 'light' for Allah is either that He is the Guide (هادي) of the heavens and earth or that He illuminates them in the best manner, similar to metaphorical usage in Arabic. This passage guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih) by affirming Allah's transcendence.

Key Terms
الخالق
القديم
واجب الوجود
العرض
الهداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which proof shows that light is an accident and therefore cannot be a god?
A) The fifth proof
B) The sixth proof
C) The seventh proof
D) The conclusion

Key Takeaway
Light is created, perishable, and an accident, so it cannot be Allah; rather, 'light' means Guide or Illuminator metaphorically.

Will you now turn to Him as the source of spiritual light and wisdom with a deeper awareness? Commit to a specific action today that reflects this reliance.

The Sixth Proof: Physical Light Is Transient
Sixth argument against literal interpretation of light for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof that Allah cannot be physical light because light is subject to annihilation, which contradicts His eternal and necessary existence.

Core Idea
Physical light perishes; the Eternal cannot perish.

Imam Razi continues his systematic refutation of those who interpret Allah's 'light' literally. The sixth argument is crisp: light disappears with darkness. If Allah were that light, He would be capable of ceasing to exist — but that is impossible for the Necessary Existent. Let's see how this reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the sixth argument carefully. Note the logical structure: premise, conditional, conclusion.
The argument is self-contained and numbered.
2
Identify the key premise
The premise is that light disappears when darkness comes. This is an empirical observation accepted by all.
This premise does not rely on theoretical assumptions.
3
Trace the conditional and conclusion
If Allah were identical to this physical light, He would be susceptible to non-existence. That contradicts His being eternal and necessary. Therefore, the interpretation is false.
The conclusion is a logical consequence of the conditional.

The Seventh Proof: Light as an Accident
Seventh argument: Light is an accident, thus not divine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof that light is an accident (arad) subsisting in bodies, and an accident cannot be God.

Core Idea
Light depends on bodies; the Self-Sufficient does not depend.

The seventh argument follows a different line: all bodies are similar in essence, yet they vary in light and darkness. This shows that light is an accident that inheres in bodies. Since accidents are dependent and contingent, they cannot be divine. Let's unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the seventh proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how it builds on the earlier established principle that bodies are similar in quiddity.
The argument assumes the reader accepts the similarity of bodies.
2
Understand the middle step
Bodies are alike in essence but differ in light and darkness. Therefore, light is not part of the essence; it is an accidental quality.
This is a classic kalam argument: difference in attributes indicates they are accidents.
3
Apply the impossibility
An accident requires a substrate (body) and is contingent. Such a dependent entity cannot be God, who is independent and necessary.
Thus, Allah cannot be light in a physical sense.

Source Text

The Correct Meaning of Allah's Light
Conclusion: Correct metaphorical interpretation of Allah's light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct metaphorical interpretation of 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' as guidance and illumination, not physical light.

Core Idea
Allah's light means guidance, not physical radiance.

After providing arguments against the literal interpretation, Imam Razi now states what 'light' actually means when applied to Allah. He offers two interpretations: guidance and illumination, and supports them with a common Arabic expression and a variant Quranic reading.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the author first negates the literal meaning, then affirms the metaphorical one.
The passage begins with 'فثبت' (so it is established).
2
Understand the metaphorical meanings
The first meaning is 'guide' (hadi). The second is 'illuminator' (munawwir) in a spiritual sense. Both are common in Arabic idiom.
The phrase 'light of the town' is used metaphorically for a benefactor.
3
Note the variant reading
Imam Razi mentions a variant recitation (qira'a) which changes the grammatical structure. This reinforces the figurative interpretation.
The variant reading is 'نورُ السموات والأرض' without the definite article possibly, but the exact variant is not specified.

The Chapter on the Veil: Evidence from Hadith
Imam Razi introduces the chapter and cites three hadiths about Allah's veil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of Allah's veil as described in the Quran and hadith, and how it refutes anthropomorphic interpretations.

Core Idea
Allah's veil is light, not a physical barrier; it protects creation from His overwhelming majesty.

Imam Razi begins the seventh chapter of Asas al-Taqdis by addressing the topic of the veil (al-hijab). The opponents of the Ash'ari position argue that a veil implies a body, so they claim that if Allah has a veil, He must be corporeal. In response, Razi cites three hadiths that describe the veil as light, emphasizing that it is a non-physical luminous reality that veils creatures from direct vision of Allah's glory. These narrations underscore divine transcendence and the created nature of the veil.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and objection
Read the opening Quranic verse (83:15) and the opponents' claim that a veil is only conceivable for bodies. Note how this sets up the theological problem.
The opponents are likely the Jahmiyyah or those who deny divine attributes.
2
Examine the first hadith
Read the first hadith carefully. It describes Allah's veil as light and states that if removed, the glories of His face would burn everything. Consider what this implies about the nature of the veil.
The hadith is authenticated by the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim).
3
Examine the second hadith
Read the second hadith, which mentions seventy veils of light. Notice the similarity to the first hadith and how it reinforces the same point.
4
Reflect on the third hadith
The third hadith is incomplete, but consider how it likely connects to the theme of divine recompense for the righteous. Reflect on the overall message of the chapter.
The full hadith probably interprets 'للذين أحسنوا' from the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في الحجاب
قال تعالى : «كلا . إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ محجوبون(۱) قالوا : و الحجاب لا يعقل إلا في الأجسام .
وتمسكوا أيضاً : بأخبار كثيرة :
الخبر الأول : روى صاحب شرح السنة رحمه الله - في باب الرد على الجهمية ، قال : قام فينا رسول الله بخمس كلمات . فقال : إن الله تعالى لا ينام ، ولا ينبغي أن ينام ، ولكنه يخفض القسط ، ويرفعه . يرفع إليه عمل الليل قبل النهار ، وعمل النهار قبل الليل ، حجابه من نور ، لو كشفه لأحرقت سبحات وجهه ، ما انتهى إليه بصره خلقه ، فقال المصنف : هذا حديث أقر به الشيخان . وقوله : يخفض من القسط ويرفعه ، أراد : أنه يراعى العدل فى أعمال عباده . كما قال تعالى : و ما ننزله إلا بقدر معلوم »
الخبر الثاني : ما يروى فى الكتب المشهورة عن النبي : « إن الله تعالى سبعين حجابا من نور ، لو كشفها لأحرقت سبحات وجهه كل ما أدرك بصره ،
الخبر الثالث : روى في تفسير قوله تعالى : « للذين أحسنوا :
Chapter Seven on the Veil. He said, the Exalted: 'Nay! Indeed, they will be veiled from their Lord on that Day.' They said: The veil is only conceivable in bodies. And they also held fast to many narrations. The first narration: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah narrated—in the chapter on refuting the Jahmiyyah—he said: The Messenger of Allah stood among us with five words. He said: Indeed Allah, the Exalted, does not sleep, and it is not proper that He should sleep, but He lowers the measure and raises it. The action of the night is raised to Him before the day, and the action of the day before the night. His veil is light; if He removed it, the glories of His Face would burn whatever His sight reaches of His creation. The author said: This hadith is acknowledged by the two shaykhs. His saying: 'He lowers the measure and raises it' means that He observes justice in the actions of His servants, as He said, the Exalted: 'And We do not send down except in a known measure.' The second narration: What is narrated in well-known books from the Prophet: 'Indeed Allah, the Exalted, has seventy veils of light; if He removed them, the glories of His Face would burn everything that His sight perceives.' The third narration: It was narrated in the interpretation of His saying, the Exalted: 'For those who do good...'
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage begins Chapter Seven of the work, focusing on the 'veil' (الحجاب) of Allah. The opponents argue that a veil is only conceivable for physical bodies, implying that if God has a veil, He must be a body. To counter this, the author cites three prophetic narrations (أخبار) without commenting extensively, leaving the reader to infer that the veil is not a physical barrier but light—itself a created entity that does not imply divine corporeality. The first hadith describes Allah's veil as light and states that if it were removed, the 'سبحات وجهه' (glories of His Face) would burn everything. This emphasizes divine transcendence: the veil veils creatures from direct vision of God, not because God is bodily hidden, but because His majesty is overwhelming. The second hadith mentions seventy veils of light. The third hadith is only introduced and cut off, but it likely connects to the interpretation of 'للذين أحسنوا' (for those who do good) from the Quran.

حجاب
نور
سبحات وجهه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a 'veil' implies God is a physical entity hidden behind a curtain.
Correction: These narrations show the veil is light, not a material barrier, and points to God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the nature of Allah's veil according to the first hadith?
A) Darkness
B) Light
C) Water
D) Fire

Key Takeaway
The veil of Allah is described as light in authentic hadith, indicating it is a created luminous screen that protects creation from the overwhelming majesty of Allah, not a physical barrier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s veil of light protects creation from being consumed by His glory, what does that tell you about your own unworthiness to stand before Him without that veil? Have you ever truly felt the weight of your sins as deserving of destruction, or do you take His mercy for granted? Today, commit to a moment of silent awe, thanking Him for the veil that preserves you.
?
The hadith says the veil is light. Light reveals, but it can also burn if too intense. How does this shape your concept of approaching Allah in prayer? Do you rush into His presence with casual familiarity, or do you remember that you are speaking to the One whose unveiled glory would consume everything? Will you change your prayer to reflect this reverence?
?
Imagine if the veil were lifted for just a second — you would be destroyed. Yet Allah has chosen to veil Himself, not because He is hidden, but because He is merciful. How does this truth affect your relationship with Him? Will you live today with a sense of both awe and gratitude, knowing that every moment you exist is due to His veiled mercy?

The Opponents' Prooftexts for the Veil
Razi enumerates the opponents' prooftexts for a divine veil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key Quranic verse and hadiths that opponents use to argue for a literal veil for Allah, and how they interpret these texts anthropomorphically.

Core Idea
Opponents use 'hijab' texts to argue Allah has a physical barrier.

Razi now presents the arguments of his opponents, who rely on scriptural texts to support the idea that Allah has a veil. These include a Quranic verse and several hadiths that mention a 'veil' of light. Understanding these prooftexts is essential before Razi refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse and opponents' reasoning
Read the verse from Surat al-Mutaffifin and note the opponents' argument: 'hijab' is only conceivable for bodies, so applying it to Allah implies corporeality.
2
Examine the first hadith about the veil of light
Read the hadith reported in Sharh al-Sunna. Note that the Prophet says Allah's veil is light, and if removed, the glories of His face would burn everything. Also note the author's explanation of 'lowering and raising the scale' as referring to justice.
This hadith is acknowledged by Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Review the second and third hadiths
Read the second hadith about seventy veils of light and the third hadith about the believers looking upon Allah's face when the veil is raised.
The third hadith is based on the verse 'for those who have done good is the best and even more'.
4
Identify the common thread
Notice how all these texts use the concept of a veil (hijab) in relation to Allah. The opponents take them literally to argue for a physical barrier.
Razi will later refute this interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في الحجاب قال تعالى : «كلا . إنهم عن ربهم يومنذ المحجوبون» قالوا : و الحجاب لا يعقل إلا في الأجسام . وتمسكوا أيضاً : بأخبار كثيرة : الخبر الأول : روى صاحب شرح السنة رحمه الله - في باب الرد على الجهمية ، قال : قام فينا رسول الله بخمس كلمات . فقال : . إن الله تعالى لا ينام ، ولا ينبغي أن ينام ، ولكنه يخفض القسط ، ويرفعه . يرفع إليه عمل الليل قبل النهار ، وعمل النهار قبل الليل ، حجابه نور ، لو كشفه لأحرقت سبحات وجهه ، ما انتهى إليه بصره خلقه . فقال المصنف : هذا حديث أقر به الشيخان . وقوله : يخفض من القسط ويرفعه ، أراد : أنه يراعى العدل فى أعمال عباده . كما قال تعالى : «و ما ننزله إلا بقدر معلوم» الخبر الثاني : ما يروى فى الكتب المشهورة عن النبي : « إن الله تعالى سبعين حجابا من نور ، لو كشفها لأحرقت سبحات وجهه كل ما أدرك بصره» الخبر الثالث : روى في تفسير قوله تعالى : « للذين أحسنوا : الحسنى ، وزيادة» . إنه تعالى يرفع الحجاب ، فينظرون إلى وجهه تعالى .
The seventh chapter on the veil. He said, exalted be He: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled.' They said: The veil is only conceivable in the case of bodies. And they also clung to many reports: The first report: The author of Sharh al-Sunna, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated in the chapter on refuting the Jahmiyya, saying: The Messenger of Allah stood among us with five words. He said: 'Indeed Allah, exalted be He, does not sleep, nor is it befitting that He sleep, but He lowers the scale and raises it. The deeds of the night are raised to Him before the day, and the deeds of the day before the night. His veil is light; if He were to remove it, the glories of His face would burn everything that His sight reaches of His creation.' The author said: This is a hadith acknowledged by the two shaykhs. His saying 'He lowers the scale and raises it' means that He observes justice in the deeds of His servants, as He said: 'And We do not send it down except with a known measure.' The second report: What is narrated in the famous books from the Prophet: 'Indeed Allah, exalted be He, has seventy veils of light; if He were to remove them, the glories of His face would burn everything His sight perceives.' The third report: Narrated in the interpretation of His saying: 'For those who have done good is the best and even more.' Indeed, He, exalted be He, raises the veil, and they look upon His face, exalted be He.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the opponents' evidence. It begins with a chapter heading on the veil. The opponents cite the Quranic verse (al-Mutaffifin 83:15) that the disbelievers are veiled from their Lord, and they argue that the concept of a veil (hijab) is only intelligible for physical bodies, thus implying corporeality for Allah. They further support their claim with multiple hadiths. The first hadith, from the Prophet, states that Allah's veil is light, and if removed, the glories of His face would burn everything. The author of Sharh al-Sunna notes this hadith is acknowledged by al-Bukhari and Muslim, and explains the phrase about lowering and raising the scale as referring to Allah's justice in deeds. The second hadith mentions seventy veils of light. The third hadith, based on the verse about 'the best and even more' (Yunus 10:26), says Allah will raise the veil and the believers will look upon His face. The opponents use these texts to argue that the veil is a real attribute of Allah, implying a barrier that can be removed. The passage thus presents the scriptural basis for the anthropomorphic interpretation.

Misconception: The opponents believe that because the Quran and hadith use the word 'hijab' for Allah, it implies a physical barrier or that Allah is hidden behind a screen.
Correction: Mainstream Sunni scholars interpret 'hijab' metaphorically as a veil of might and majesty, not a literal physical barrier.

Checkpoint

B) That 'hijab' implies a body
C) That the verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The opponents support their anthropomorphic view by citing scriptural texts that mention a veil, but these texts are interpreted allegorically by mainstream Sunni scholars.

The Power of Language: احتجاب vs حجوب
Razi distinguishes between احتجاب and حجوب and states the correct interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between 'ihtijab' (veiling oneself) and 'hijab' (being veiled) and why the former is used for Allah while the latter is not, according to Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
'Ihtijab' implies power; 'hijab' implies weakness — so we say Allah is muhtajib, not mahjub.

Razi addresses a subtle theological distinction in how we describe Allah's relationship to creation. The choice between saying Allah is 'muhtajib' (veiling Himself) versus 'mahjub' (veiled) is not arbitrary — it reflects deep principles about divine transcendence and appropriate language. In this passage, he explains the linguistic and theological reasons for preferring one term over the other, and clarifies what 'veil' means when applied to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three parts: the ruling, the linguistic connotation, and the metaphorical interpretation.
2
Identify the two terms and their connotations
Find the terms 'muhtajib' and 'mahjub' and note how Razi explains that 'ihtijab' conveys strength and power while 'hijab' conveys weakness and humiliation.
The root letters are the same (ح-ج-ب), but the verb form changes the meaning.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples of the sultan: a sultan is 'muhtajib' from his subjects, but a subject is 'mahjub' from the sultan. This shows the active vs. passive connotation.
This analogy helps ground the abstract distinction.
4
Understand the reinterpretation
Razi says the literal meaning of 'hijab' (a body) is impossible for Allah, so it must be interpreted metaphorically. Learn the two interpretations: Ash'ari (no vision) and Mu'tazili (prevention of favors).
This shows how kalam reconciles scriptural language with tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الكلام في الآية هو : أن أصحابنا – رحمهم الله ـ قالوا : إنه يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى محتجب عن الخلق ، ولا يجوز أن يقال : إنه محجوب عنهم . لأن لفظة الاحتجاب مشعرة بالقوة والقدرة والحجاب مشعر بالعجز والذلة . يقال : احتجب السلطان عن عبيده . ويقال : فلان حجب عن الدخول على السلطان . وحقيقة الحجاب بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى : محال . لأنه عبارة عن الجسم المتوسط بين جسمين آخرين . بل هو محمول عندنا : على أن لا يخلق الله تعالى في العين رؤية متعلقة به . و عند من ينكر الرؤية على أنه تعالى يمنع وصول آثار إحسانه وفضله من إنسان .
And know: the discussion regarding the verse is that our companions—may Allah have mercy on them—said: It is permissible to say that He, exalted be He, is 'muhtajib' (veiling Himself) from creation, but it is not permissible to say that He is 'mahjub' (veiled) from them. Because the term 'ihtijab' conveys strength and power, while 'hijab' conveys weakness and humiliation. It is said: The sultan is muhtajib from his subjects. And it is said: So-and-so was mahjub from entering upon the sultan. The reality of the veil with respect to Allah, exalted be He, is impossible, because it is a body intermediate between two other bodies. Rather, it is interpreted by us to mean that Allah, exalted be He, does not create in the eye a vision belonging to Him. And according to those who deny vision, it means that He, exalted be He, prevents the arrival of the effects of His goodness and favor from a person.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual clarification by Razi. He explains that the author's school (the Ash'aris) permits saying that Allah is 'muhtajib' (veiling Himself) but not 'mahjub' (veiled), because 'ihtijab' implies power and authority, while 'hijab' implies weakness and humiliation. He gives examples: a sultan is said to be muhtajib from his subjects, but a person is said to be mahjub from entering upon the sultan. The actual reality of a veil for Allah is impossible, because a veil is a body that intervenes between two other bodies. Rather, the term is interpreted metaphorically: in the Ash'ari view, it means that Allah does not create in the eye a vision pertaining to Him (i.e., He does not allow sight of Himself in this world). For those who deny the vision of Allah entirely (the Jahmiyya or Mu'tazila), it means that He prevents the effects of His goodness and favor from reaching a person. This passage thus defends the transcendence of Allah by reinterpreting the anthropomorphic language.

Key Terms
احتجاب
حجاب
محجوب
محتجب
مشعر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'mahjub' and 'muhtajib' are interchangeable when referring to Allah.
Correction: The Ash'ari school states that only 'muhtajib' is permissible because it implies divine power, while 'mahjub' implies weakness.

Checkpoint

D) Because both are acceptable

Key Takeaway
When speaking of Allah's veiling, we must use 'ihtijab' (muhtajib) to preserve divine majesty, and avoid 'hijab' (mahjub) as it implies weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The sultan's ihtijab denotes power, while hijab denotes weakness. How does this reshape your longing to see Allah? Will you ask yourself whether your desire for vision stems from impatience or from reverent hope?
?
Have you ever described Allah in a way that might unintentionally limit His transcendence? Commit to reviewing your vocabulary about Allah and discarding any words that imply weakness or confinement.

Razi's Argument from Hierarchy of Perfection
Razi analyzes the hadith about the veil of light using a hierarchy of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi demonstrates the absolute transcendence of Allah by applying a causal principle to the hadith about the veil of light, showing that all created perfections are as nothing compared to His perfection.

Core Idea
All created perfections are as nothing before Allah's perfection.

Razi begins by interpreting the Prophet's statement about the veil of light not as a physical description but as a theological demonstration. He establishes a fundamental causal principle: every perfection in an effect is derived from its cause, and exists more fittingly in the cause. From this, he builds a hierarchy of created perfections, culminating in the conclusion that even the greatest created perfections are utterly insignificant relative to Allah. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: it begins with a principle, then applies it to Allah, then to a descending hierarchy.
The phrase 'حجابه النور' is the starting point.
2
Identify the causal principle
The first sentence presents a key principle: any perfection in an effect comes from its cause, and the cause possesses that perfection more fittingly. Understand this principle before moving on.
This is a foundational concept in Islamic theology.
3
Follow the hierarchy of perfection
Razi then applies the principle in a chain: Allah gives all perfections, so all contingent perfections are as nothing. Then he compares the elemental world to celestial spheres, the inhabited quarter to elements, and the individual to the quarter. Trace each step.
Notice the diminishing scale: from all contingents to a single person.
4
Ask: what does this show about the veil?
Consider how this hierarchy supports the hadith. The veil of light symbolizes the overwhelming majesty of Allah, which would obliterate all created senses if unveiled. The grades of perfection show why nothing can perceive Him directly.
This connects the logical argument to the hadith's message.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Contrast a literal reading of the veil with Razi's interpretation. The hadith is not about a physical barrier but about the absolute transcendence of Allah.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر الأول : وهو قوله عليه السلام : « حجابه : النور ، فاعلم : أن كل شيء يفرض مؤثراً في شيء آخر ، فكل كمال يحصل للأثر ، فهو مستفاد من المؤثر. ولاشك أن ثبوت ذلك الكمال لذلك المؤثر ، أولى من ثبوته في ذلك الأثر ، وأقوى وأكمل . ولا شك أن معطى الكمالات بأسرها هو الحق تعالى فكان كل كمالات الممكنات بالنسبة إلى كمال الله تعالى كالعدم . ولاشك أن جملة الممكنات ليست إلا عالم الأجسام وعالم الأرواح . ولاشك أن جملة كمالات عالم العناصر بالنسبة إلى كمالات الأفلاك كالعدم . ثم كمال حال الربع المسكون بالنسبة إلى كمال العناصر كالعدم . ثم الشخص المعين بالنسبة إلى كمالات الربع المسكون كالعدم .
As for the first report, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'His veil is light,' know that whenever something is assumed to be an effect in something else, every perfection that occurs to the effect is derived from the cause. There is no doubt that the existence of that perfection in the cause is more fitting than its existence in the effect, and stronger and more complete. There is no doubt that the giver of all perfections is the Truth, exalted be He; so all perfections of contingents relative to the perfection of Allah, exalted be He, are as nothing. There is no doubt that the totality of contingents is nothing but the world of bodies and the world of spirits. There is no doubt that the totality of the perfections of the world of elements relative to the perfections of the celestial spheres is as nothing. Then the perfection of the state of the inhabited quarter relative to the perfections of the elements is as nothing. Then the specific individual relative to the perfections of the inhabited quarter is as nothing.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a theological analysis of the first hadith about the veil of light. Razi argues from a causal principle: every perfection in an effect is derived from its cause, and the perfection in the cause is more fitting and complete. Since Allah is the ultimate giver of all perfections, all perfections of contingent beings are as nothing compared to His perfection. He then illustrates this with a hierarchy: the entire world of bodies and spirits (all contingents) are nothing compared to Allah; the perfections of the elemental world are nothing compared to the celestial spheres; the state of the inhabited quarter (the known world) is nothing compared to the elements; and a specific individual is nothing compared to the inhabited quarter. This chain shows that even the greatest created perfection is utterly insignificant before Allah. Thus, the veil of light is not a physical barrier but a metaphor for Allah's transcendent majesty, which is beyond all created senses. The hadith's mention of the veil of light is meant to convey that Allah's glory would overwhelm all creation if unveiled.

Key Terms
مؤثر
أثر
كمال
الممكنات
الربع المسكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about the veil of light is often misunderstood as referring to a physical barrier of light that hides Allah from creation.
Correction: Razi explains that the veil is a metaphor for Allah's absolute transcendence; the hierarchy of perfections shows that no created being can perceive Allah because all created perfections are as nothing compared to His perfection.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does the hierarchy of perfections in the hadith analysis illustrate?
A) That light is a physical veil
B) That all created perfections are as nothing compared to Allah
C) That the Prophet saw Allah
D) That the veil is multiple

Key Takeaway
Razi's analysis of the hadith of the veil of light demonstrates that all created perfections are utterly insignificant compared to Allah's perfection, using a descending hierarchy of causes and effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi shows that even the highest created beings (celestial spheres) are as nothing compared to Allah. Yet you often seek validation from people and worry about your status. Where in your life do you fear being 'as nothing' in the eyes of others? How might embracing your utter dependence on Allah free you from that fear?
?
If the veil of light were lifted and you saw Allah's majesty, you would be annihilated. Yet you live as if you are self-sufficient and can approach Allah on your own merit. What is one habit or attitude you need to change to live in constant awareness that you are nothing without Him? Be specific—name one practical change you will make today.

The Distinction Between Muhtajib and Mahjub
Razi explains the permissibility of saying God is muhtajib but not mahjub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari scholars permit saying God is muhtajib (actively veiling Himself) but not mahjub (passively veiled), and how the term is understood metaphorically.

Core Idea
The active form indicates strength; the passive indicates weakness — so only muhtajib befits God.

Razi addresses a subtle linguistic distinction crucial for speaking about God's transcendence. The terms muhtajib and mahjub are often confused, but carry very different connotations. This lesson unpacks the Ash'ari position on which one is permissible and why a literal veil is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very precise linguistic argument.
2
Analyze the linguistic reasoning
Focus on the distinction between 'ihtajaba' and 'hujiba'. Why does the active form imply strength and the passive weakness? Think about the analogy of a sultan.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Razi explains that a literal veil is impossible for God. Then he gives two metaphorical meanings depending on one's view of ru'ya. Make sure you see how these interpretations avoid anthropomorphism.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسنى ، وزيادة (ه) ، . إنه تعالى يرفع الحجاب ، فينظرون إلى وجهه تعالى .
واعلم : أن الكلام في الآية هو : أن أصحابنا – رحمهم الله ـ قالوا : إنه يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى محتجب عن الخلق ، ولا يجوز أن يقال : إنه محجوب عنهم . لأن لفظة الاحتجاب مشعرة بالقوة والقدرة والحجاب مشعر بالعجز والذلة . يقال : احتجب السلطان عن عبيده . ويقال : فلان حجب عن الدخول على السلطان . وحقيقة الحجاب بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى : محال . لأنه عبارة عن الجسم المتوسط بين جسمين آخرين . بل هو محمول عندنا : على أن لا يخلق الله تعالى في العين رؤية متعلقة به . و عند من ينكر الرؤية على أنه تعالى يمنع وصول آثار إحسانه وفضله من إنسان .
Al-husna and ziyadah. Indeed, He, exalted, lifts the veil, so they look at His face, exalted. Know that the discussion concerning the verse is that our companions (may God have mercy on them) said: It is permissible to say that He, exalted, is muhtajib (actively veiled) from creation, but it is not permissible to say that He is mahjub (passively veiled) from them. Because the word ihtijab implies strength and power, while hijab implies weakness and humiliation. It is said: The sultan screened himself from his subjects. And it is said: So-and-so was prevented from entering upon the sultan. The reality of hijab with respect to God, exalted, is impossible, because it refers to a body that mediates between two other bodies. Rather, it is interpreted among us as: that God, exalted, does not create vision in the eye that is connected to Him. And according to those who deny the vision (ru'ya), it is that He, exalted, prevents the arrival of the effects of His bounty and grace from a person.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a definition and lexical distinction. Razi begins by quoting a Quranic reference (10:26) about the vision of God after the veil is lifted. Then he states the rule held by Ash'ari scholars: it is permissible to say that God is 'muhtajib' (actively screening Himself) from creation, but not that He is 'mahjub' (passively veiled or prevented from being seen). The reason is linguistic: the active form 'ihtajaba' implies strength and power, like a king screening himself from subjects, while the passive 'hujiba' implies weakness and humiliation, like a subject being barred from entering. However, Razi clarifies that the literal reality of a veil (hijab) as a physical barrier between bodies is impossible for God. So the term is metaphorical: for those who affirm the possibility of seeing God (like the Ash'aris), it means God does not create vision in the eye directed at Him; for those who deny ru'ya altogether, it means God withholds the effects of His bounty from a person. This distinction hinges on the active/passive connotation and the impossibility of a physical veil for God.

Key Terms
محتجب
محجوب
الحجاب
الرؤية
الاحتجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'hijab' for God refers to a physical barrier or curtain.
Correction: Razi clarifies that a literal veil (body mediating between bodies) is impossible for God; the term is metaphorical.

Checkpoint

B) Because 'muhtajib' is a synonym for 'mahjub' but used in the Quran.
C) Because 'muhtajib' is the only term used in hadith.
D) Because 'mahjub' implies a physical barrier which is impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between muhtajib and mahjub hinges on the active/passive connotation, affirming God's power and negating any implication of weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel distant from God, do you ever think He is passively hiding from you, as if He is indifferent? How does knowing that God is muhtajib—actively veiling Himself out of strength and wisdom—change that feeling? What is one step you can take tonight to seek His face despite the veil?
?

?
If the literal meaning of hijab is impossible for God, then God's 'veiling' is always a deliberate act of wisdom. Think of a recent trial or hardship you faced. Can you see it as God actively screening Himself for a purpose? Write a short prayer thanking Him for His sovereignty over when He reveals Himself to you.

God's Perfection and the Impossibility of Vision
Razi argues from the hadith about God's veil to prove human incapacity for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument from the hadith of the veil that human beings cannot see God in this life due to the overwhelming disparity of perfection.

Core Idea
Human perfection is as nothing compared to God's; hence vision is impossible.

Imam Razi draws on a famous hadith — 'His veil is light' — to argue that direct vision of God is impossible for any created being. He builds a chain of comparisons showing that even the smallest degree of God's perfection would overwhelm the human spirit. Let's follow his logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how Razi moves from a general principle to a specific conclusion.
2
Identify the key principle
Razi begins with a principle: every perfection in an effect comes from its cause, and the cause has that perfection more fully. Understand this foundational idea.
3
Follow the hierarchy of perfections
Razi compares levels of creation: elements are nothing to celestial spheres, the inhabited quarter is nothing to elements, a single human is nothing to the inhabited quarter. Trace this chain.
4
Apply to God and human capacity
The conclusion is that human perfection is negligible compared to God's. Therefore, the human spirit cannot endure even a glimpse of God's glory. This is the meaning of the hadith.
5
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question. It will reinforce the central conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر الأول : وهو قوله عليه السلام : « حجابه : النور ، فاعلم : أن كل شيء يفرض مؤثراً في شيء آخر ، فكل كمال يحصل للأثر ، فهو مستفاد من المؤثر. ولاشك أن ثبوت ذلك الكمال لذلك المؤثر ، أولى من ثبوته في ذلك الأثر ، وأقوى وأكمل . ولا شك أن معطى الكمالات بأسرها هو الحق تعالى فكل كمالات الممكنات بالنسبة إلى كمال الله تعالى كالعدم . ولاشك أن جملة الممكنات ليست إلا عالم الأجسام وعالم الأرواح . ولاشك أن جملة كمالات عالم العناصر بالنسبة إلى كمالات الأفلاك كالعدم . ثم كمال حال الربع المسكون بالنسبة إلى كمال العناصر كالعدم . ثم الشخص المعين بالنسبة إلى كمالات الربع المسكون كالعدم .
فيظهر من هذا : أن كمال الإنسان المعين بالنسبة إلى كمال الله تعالى : أولى بأن يقال : إنه كالعدم . ولاشك أن روح الإنسان وحده ، لا تطيق قبول أدنى ذلك الكمال ، ولا يمكنه مطالعته . بل الأرواح البشرية تضمحل في أول مرتبة من مراتب تلك الكمالات . فهذا هو المراد بقوله عليه السلام : لو كشفها لأحرقت سبحات وجهه كل شيء ، أدركه بصره .
As for the first report, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'His veil is light,' know that any thing that is assumed to be effective in another thing, every perfection that occurs to the effect is derived from the cause. And there is no doubt that the existence of that perfection in the cause is more fitting than its existence in the effect, and stronger and more complete. And there is no doubt that the giver of all perfections is the Truth, exalted, so all perfections of contingent things compared to the perfection of God, exalted, are like nothing. And there is no doubt that the totality of contingent things is only the world of bodies and the world of spirits. And there is no doubt that the totality of perfections of the world of elements compared to the perfections of the celestial spheres is like nothing. Then the perfection of the state of the inhabited quarter compared to the perfection of the elements is like nothing. Then the specific person compared to the perfections of the inhabited quarter is like nothing. Thus it becomes clear from this that the perfection of a specific human compared to the perfection of God, exalted, is more deserving of being called nothing. And there is no doubt that the human spirit alone cannot bear to receive the least of that perfection, nor can it contemplate it. Rather, human spirits are annihilated in the first level of those perfections. This is the meaning of his saying, peace be upon him: 'If it were uncovered, the glories of His face would burn everything that His sight perceives.'
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and gradation, drawn from the hadith that God's veil is light. Razi first establishes a general principle: every perfection that appears in an effect is derived from its cause, and the cause possesses that perfection more fully and fundamentally. Then he applies this principle to God, the giver of all perfections, concluding that the perfections of all contingent things are as nothing compared to God's perfection. He then compares hierarchical levels: the elements' perfections are nothing compared to the celestial spheres; the inhabited quarter of Earth is nothing compared to the elements; a specific individual is nothing compared to the entire inhabited quarter. By this chain, the perfection of a single human is utterly negligible relative to God's perfection. Consequently, the human spirit cannot tolerate even the slightest direct perception of God's glory. Razi interprets the hadith about the 'subuhat' (glories) of God's face burning everything as a metaphor for the overwhelming intensity of God's perfection, which would annihilate any finite being that attempts to see Him. Thus the impossibility of seeing God in this life is grounded in the creature's incapacity, not in any deficiency in God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith about God's veil being light implies a physical light barrier that hides God from view.
Correction: Razi explains the veil as a metaphor for the overwhelming intensity of God's perfection, which would annihilate any finite being that attempts to see Him. The impossibility is due to the creature's incapacity, not a physical obstruction.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the chain of perfections in this passage?
A) That human perfection is equal to the perfection of the celestial spheres.
B) That the perfection of a specific human is as nothing compared to God's perfection, so the human spirit cannot bear to see Him.
C) That God's perfection is equivalent to the sum of all contingent perfections.
D) That the hadith about the veil being light means God's essence is literal light.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that the impossibility of seeing God stems from the infinite disparity between God's perfection and human capacity, as illustrated by the hadith of the veil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says your perfection compared to God's is as nothing. How does this truth confront your sense of self-importance and entitlement in your daily actions? What would change if you truly lived as that 'nothing' before God?
?
If the human spirit cannot bear even the least of God's perfections, what does that say about your attempts to approach God through your own strength? In what areas are you relying on your own capacity rather than God's mercy?
?
The hadith says God's glories would burn everything. What attachments or habits in your life would be 'burned' if you were to stand before God's absolute perfection today? Are you willing to let them go?
